THE RED PAGE.
My Heart.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I loved a little maiden- •
Alas ! she loved not me :
And my heart with grief so laden
Straight broke in pieces three.
One piece I gave to the children,
Who tossed it about with glee—
A heart is a pretty plaything
When red with misery.
One piece 1 gave to my lost love
To wear when I was dead—
It hangs on her frozen bosom
Liice a piece of ruddied lead.
One piece I gave to the people—
It oft had bled for their woe—
But they dried it for exhibition
In a magic-lantern show.
FRED J. BROOMFIELD.
Two Books by Stephen Crane .
George s Mother ” and “ Maggie ” are worthy
of the author of “The Red Badge of Courage.”
His method is unchanged : his results are just as
remarkable. His intuition, power of obser-
vation, and faculty of expression, taken to-
gether, amount to genius. Yet, asking one’s self
whether his books will be permanent, whether
they stand on the same plane as fic-
tional standards like “Vanity Fair” or “Les
Miserables,” the answer is curiously uncertain.
W ork so brilliant should be lasting. Yet it leaves
on the mind, after all, the impression of a momen-
tary tourde force.
Perhaps at present Crane suffers somewhat
because he is so entirely novel : nobody knows ex-
actly where to place him. He makes his stories
m a way in which no one ever made stories before,
lolstoi may have given him hints, but they are
improved almost out of knowledge. There is a
Dickensy fiction and a Thackerayan fiction, and a
fiction of the Howells kind, and the New Woman
kind, and half-a-dozen other kinds, and the nor-
mal worker in any of these kinds of fiction has
innumerable standards of comparison awaiting
him. But Crane has the good fortune to be ab-
normal. Many people praise his work ; many are
indifferent to it; some blame it ; but there is no
doubt that of its kind, and to his extent, it is
unique. He has created a mode.
His chief weakness is that he has not yet created
anything else. He displays prodigious imagina-
tive power in dissecting minds and laying bare
emotions. His identification of himself with his
characters is so complete that one cannot but
believe that his characters thought and felt in the
exact fashion ascribed to them. But he stops at
analysis. He sees and observes men' and women
doing this and that, and he takes the reader
behind the clock-face and shows the wheels and
springs, the checks and impulses, which account
for the movement. His skill in language makes
him an unsurpassable showman, and yet his men
and women impress one rather as men and «omen
in a cinematographe than as living human beings.
Despite the wonder of them there is an ever-
present sense of artful mechanism. And nobody
wants cinematographe all the time.
Stephen Crane, in truth, lowers his imagination
to the level of his facts : the greatest writers of
fiction have always raised their facts to the level
of their imagination. The essential characteristic
of a work of art, whether in fiction or elsewhere, is
that it should be no isolated presentment of de-
tached things or persons, but should have
a visible relation to all things and persons,
be in contact with every facet of existence.
Every great book is more or less a new edition of
the Book of Life —maybe in octavo, or mavbe
only in duodecimo; perhaps with many chapters
missing, perhaps ill-bound, or ill-printed, or
written in a foreign language of which only now
and again a sentence is intelligible; but withal
unmistakeable in its scope and purport. Crane’s
work is only a fragment of a page —what one
man can see and describe for himself. It is ad-
mirable so far as it goes, but it lacks artistic
breadth and unity.
♦-
“ George’s Mother ” is a poor, narrow, religious
old woman drifted to New York with her son, a
workman. She has memories of a country life, of
a husband, other children —all gone : George is
all she has to live for. As the story opens George
is coming home from work. Passing a street
corner a man recognises him—an old country
friend he hasn’t seen for years. They go into a
saloon for a drink, after the asinine habit of men.
Jones, the friend, isiolder.
There was something very worldly and wise about him.
Life did not seem to confuse him. Evidently he under-
stood its complications. His hand thrust into his
trousers-pocket, where he jingled keys, and his hat
perched back on his head, expressed a young man of vast
knowledge. His extensive acquaintance with bar-tenders
aided him materially in his habitual expression of wisdom.
George reaches home late, and his mother is
anxious. He resents her anxiety :it assails his
manly dignity. And she always worries him by
telling him to hang his coat up behind the door.
Nevertheless, she asks him to go to prayer-
meeting with her. He is fond of her, and fences
for an excuse.
‘ But— 'he said attain. He was obliged to make great
search for an argument. Finally he concluded: ‘But
what th’ blazes would I do at prayer-meetin’?
In liis ears was the sound of a. hymn, made by people
who tilted their heads at a prescribed angle of devotion.
It would be too apparent that they were all better than
he. When he entered they would turn their heads and
regard him with suspicion. This would be an enormous
aggravation, since he was certain that he was as good as
they.
‘Well, now, y’ see,’ he said, quite gently. ‘I don't
wanta go, an’ it wouldn’t do me no good t’ go if I didn’t
wanta go.’
His mother’s face swiftly changed. She breathed a
huge sigh, the counterpart of ones he had heard upon
like occasions. She put a tiny black bonnet on her head,
and wrapped her figure in an old shawl. She cast a
martyr-like glance upon her son, and went mournfully
away. She resembled a limited funeral procession.
The young man writhed under it to an extent. He
kicked moodily at a table-leg. When the sound of her
footfalls died away he felt distinctly relieved.
Gradually the need of excitement, the pleasure
of conviviality, and the disgust born of being told
every evening to hang his coat on the hook, lead
George into drinking habits. There is a picture
of the knot of cronies in the saloon on his first
evening out. They were all so happy and friendly
and proud of their company. In their little back
room they felt themselves on a sort of island
away from the world of coarse humanity.
Each man explained in his way that he was totally out
of place in the beforementioned world. They were
possessed of various virtues, which were unappreciated
by those with whom they were commonly obliged to
mingle—they were fitted for a tree-shaded land where
everything was peace.
Now that five of them had congregated, it gave them
happiness to speak their inmost thoughts without fear of
being misunderstood.
As he drank more beer George felhis breast expand
with manly feeling. He knew thatf'he was capable of
sublime things. He looked upon the beaming faces and
knew that if at that instant there should come a time for
a great sacrifice he would blissfully make it. He would
pass tranquilly into the unknown, or into bankruptcy,
amid the ejaculations of his companions upon his many
virtues.
Then George nourishes amatory sentiments
which, suddenly nipped, help along the rake’s
progress.
An indefinite woman was in all of George’s dreams. . .
He was icy, self-possessed ; but she, the dream-girl, was
consumed by wild, torrential passion. He went to the
length of having her display it before the people. He
saw them wonder at his tranquillity. It amazed them
infinitely to see him remain cold before the glory of this
peerless woman’s love. . . .
One day he met Maggie Johnson on the stairs. She
had a can of beer in one hand and a brown-paper parcel
under her arm. She glanced at him. . . . He was silent
for the most part at supper that night. He was particu-
larly unkind when he did speak. His mother, observing
him apprehensively, tried in vain to picture the new ter-
rible catastrophe. She eventually concluded that he did
not like the beef jitew. She put more salt in it. . . .
He met a young man in the halls one evening who said
to him: ‘ Say, me frien’, where d’ d’ Johnson birds live
in, hey ? ’
‘Two flights up,’said George, stonily. He had felt a
sudden quiver of his heart. The grandeur of the clothes,
the fine worldly air, the experience, the self-reliance, the
courage that shone in the countenance of this other
young man, made him suddenly sink to the depths of
woe. ...
They were going out to a show, perhaps. That pig of
the world in his embroidered cloak was going to dazzle
her with splendour. He mused upon how unrighteous it
was for other men to dazzle women with splendour.
Then he goes home, and ... his mother tells
him to hang up his coat on the nail. George
explodes !
During the next few days he suffered from his first
gloomy oonviction that the earth was not grateful to him
for his presence upon it. . . . He could perceive that
the universe hated him. He sank to the most sublime
depths of despair.
Then Jones.invites him to “ol’ Bleeoker’s blow-
out t’-morrah night. Anything yeh want t’
drink.” George accepts with fervour. y
As he walked home he thought that he was a very
grim figure. He was about to taste the delioious revenge
of a partial self-destruction. The universe would regret its
position when it saw him drunir.
George gets very drunk, and doesn’t go home till
morning. He tells his mother he was knocked
over by a truck : his remorse is so great that he
even goes to prayer-meeting with her. But the
descent goes on. He loses his situation. His
friends look askance at him. His mother is heart-
broken. She takes to her bed; and the closing
scene might have been imitated from the dexterous
close of “Madame Bovary ”
Over the transom from.the hall-way came two voices.
•Johnnie!’
‘Wot! ’
‘ You come right here t’ me! I want yehs t’ go fd’
store fer me! ’ *
‘ Ah, ma, send Sally 1 ’
‘No, I will not! You come right here! ’
‘ All right, in a minnet ! ’
‘ Johnnie 1’
‘ In a minnet, I tell yeh 1 ’
‘Johnnie ’ There was the sound of a heavy tread,
and later a boy squealed. Suddenly the clergyman
started to his feet. He rushed forward and peered. The
little old woman was dead.
“Maggie”—she is apparently the Maggie of
George’s dream—is hardly so good : less compact
and more conventional. It is the story of a slum-
girl a little more finely fibred than the others. A
chapter of prologue shows herself and her brother
Jimmie as children in an environment of squalid
horror. Tliere is a baby, and a drunken virago of
a mother, and an ordinary, decent-enough father.
The infant dies; the father disappears; Jimmie
grows up rather more honest than could have beeta
expected.
He clad his soul in armour by means of happening
hilariously in at a mission church where.a man composed,
his sermons of “ yous.’' Once a philosopher aslted this
mm why hedid not say “we” instead of “you.” The
man replied, “ What?”
While they got warm at the stove he told his hearers
just where he calculated they stood with the Lord. Many
of the sinners were impatient over the pictured depths of
their degradation. They were waiting for soup-tickets.
A reader of the words of wind demons might nave been
able to see the portions of a dialogue pass to and fro be-
tween the exhorter and his hearers.
“You are damned,” said the preacher. And the reader
of sounds might have seen the replv go forth from the
ragged people : “ Where’s our soup ?""
Jimmie and a companion sat in a rear seat and com-
mented on the things that didn’t concern them, with all
the freedom of English tourists. When they grew thirsty
and went out, their minds confused the speaker with
Christ.
When Jimmie was older, he became a truck-
driver. He began to be arrested when he was a
little boy. Before he reached a great age, he had
a fair record.
Two women in different parts of the city, and entirely
unknown to each other, caused him considerable annoy-
ance by breaking forth, simultaneously, at fateful inter-
vals, into wailings about marriage and support and
infants.
Nevertheless, he had, on a certain star-lit evening, said
wonderingly and quite reverently, “Deh moon looks
like h—l, don’t it?”
Maggie, poor Maggie ! was pretty. And to her
came Pete, a big, loud-mouthed, coarse Bowery
bar-tender. Hugo and Hardy, on opposite sides
of the Channel, stop in similar circumstances to
wonder why it is so-why so much feminine frag-
rance should be perpetually gathered up to be
perpetually scattered to the winds. The problem
has a fine sentimental fascination for the male
mind. Poor Maggie ! She was sick of the sewing-
factory “ where sat twenty girls in various shades
of yellow discontent.” And Pete “waved his
hands like a man of the world who dismisses re-
ligion and philosophy, and says ‘ Rats !’ ” Like
Othello, he recounted his exploits ; and, like Des-
demona, Maggie was charmed. She perceived that
here was the ideal man.
Pete took her to Bowery concerts, where quiet
Germans sat listening to the music with the ex-
pressions of happy cows. And one night
Maggie’s mother, in a drunken rage, drove her
from home. “ Git th’ devil outa here.” Maggie
went. A gnarled and leathery woman who lived
at the foot of the stairs waylaid Jimmie next
night.
“Ah, Jimmie, what do yehs think I tumbled to, las’
night! It was deh funnies’ t’ing I ever saw,” she cried,
coming close to him and leering. She was trembling
with eagerness to tell her tale. “ I was by me door las°
night when yer sister and her jude feller came in late,
oh, very late. An’ she, the dear, she was a-cryin’ as if
her heart would break, she was. It was deh funnies’
t’ing I ever saw. An’ right out here by me door she
asked him did he love her, did he. An’ she was a crying
as if her heart would break, poor t’ing. An’ him, I could
see be deh way what he said it dat she had been askin’
orfben, he says, ‘Oh, gee, yes,’ he says, says he, ‘Oh,
gee, yes.’ ”
Jimmie tries to formulate a theory that he had
always unconsciously held, that all sisters, ex-
cepting his own, could, advisedly, be ruined. He
thought he had a great idea of women’s frailty,
but he could not understand why any of his
kin should be victims. “ Curse her !” he said
fervidly. And her mother curses her more
vehemently still. Nevertheless, with a dim idea
of vengeance, Jimmie attacks Pete in the bar.
Once or twice again Maggie is seen with Pete in
concert-rooms.
Maggie was pale. From her eyes had been plucked all
look of self-reliance. She leaned with a dependent air
toward her companion. She was timid, as if fearing his
anger or displeasure. She seemed to beseech tenderness
of him.
Pete’s air of distinguished valor had grown upon him
until it threatened to reach stupendous dimensions. He
was infinitely gracious to the girl. It was apparent to her
that his condescension was a marvel.
He could appear to strut even while sitting still, and he
showed that he was a lion of lordly characteristics by the
air with which he spat.
Poor Maggie! The end hurries. Pete returns
to an older flame; despairing Maggie goes home,
and is driven forth by virtuous mother and brother
with words of contempt and loathing. Then, for
a little while, the street. Then the river. Then
the mother wailing : “ I kin remember when her
two feet was no bigger dan yer thumb, and she
wearad worsted boots.”
“Yeh’ll forgive her, Mary?” pleaded the woman in
black. . . . The mother’s voice came and arose in a
scream of pain. “ Oh, yes, I’ll forgive her! I’ll forgive
her 1"
No decent Scotchman should miss Cun-
ninghatne Graham’s article on Salvagia (to wit,
Scotia) in Sat. Review of Sept. 12. Of a village
in the said Salvagia he notes “At the street
corner groups of men standing spitting. . . .
Women and children are afraid to pass them. Not
quite civilised, not quite savage, a set of demi-
brutes exclaiming, if s’woman in a decent gown
goes past, ‘ There goes a butch.’”

[The following verses give some idea of the powers of
I' ion« Macleml, a young S ottish writer who has the cur-
rent ardor to exploit ' eltic legend, or endeavors to intuse
into her more modern wor& what is considered the
characteristic t'eltic twang. “ The Prayer of Women ”is
extracted from a poem called “Pharais,” published tw#
years ago.]
THE PRAYER OF WOMEN".
O Spirit, that broods up m the hills
And moves upon the face of the deep,
And is heard in the wind,
Save us from the desire of men’s eyes,
And the cruel lust of them,
And the springing of the cruel seed
In that narrow house which is as the grave
For darkness and loneliness.
That women carry with them with shame, and
weariness, and long pain,
Only for the laughter of man’s heart,
And the joy that triumphs then-in,
And the sport that is in his hearC,
Wherewith lie inocketh us,
Wherewith he playeth with us,
Wherewith he trampleth upon us . « ,
Us, who conceive and bear him ;
Us, who bring him forth ;
Who feed him in the womb, and at the breast, and
at the knee :
Whom he ealleth mother and wife,
And mother again of his children and his children’*
children.
Ah, hour of the hours,
When he looks at our hair and sees it is grey;
And at our eyes and sees they are dim ;
And at our lips straightened out with long pain;
And at our breasts, fallen and seared as a barren
hill;
And at our hands, worn with toil !
Ah, hour of the hours,
When, seeing, he seeth all the bitter ruin and
wreck of us—
All save the violated womb that curses him
All save the heart that forbeareth . . . for pity—
All save the living brain that condemneth him—■
All save the spirit that shall not mate with him,
All save the soul be shall never see
Till he be one with it, and equal;
He who hath the bridle, but findeth not ;
He who hath the whip, yet is driven;
He who as a shepherd ealleth upon us,
But is himself a lost sheep, crying among the hills 1
O Spirit, and the Nine Angels who watch us,
And thy Son, and Mary Virgin,
Heal us of the wrong of man :
We, whose breasts are weary with milk,
Gry, cry to thee, O Compassionate.
Nil nisi : “ The red—with the gore of
literary assassinations—page accuses Parkes of
selfishness and neglect of his relatives. Any un-
biassed reader of Parkes’s letters can , see that
the poor old chap always treated his English’rela-
tions with unremitting kindness.”
N.S.W. Govt, should run its own daily
newspaper—amalgating Hansard, Government
Gazette, and Agricultural Gazette. (A
Mining Gazette is also projected.) It could be
made fairly readable without being partisan
(somewhat on the model of M.L. Labor Dept,
reports)—and think of the advt.-saving, with
advts. confined to its columns exclusively, pro-
perly displayed; and issues posted at” Govt,
offices.
A SONG OF ETHLENN STUART.
He laid his dear face next to mine,
His eyes aflame burned close to mine,
His heart to mine, his lips to mine,
O he was mine, all mine, all mine.
Drunk with old wine of life I was,
Drunk as the wild-bee in the grass
Singing his honey-mad sweet bass,
Drunk, drunk with wine of love I was!
His lips of life to me were fief,
Before him I was but a leaf
Blown by the wind, a shaken leaf,
Yea, as the sickle reaps the sheaf,
My Grief!
He reaped me as a gathered sheaf!
His to be gathered, his the bliss,
But not a greater bliss than this !
All of the empty world to miss
For wild redemption of his kiss !
My Grief!
For hell was lost, though heaven was brief
Sphered in the universe of thy kiss—
So cries to thee thy fallen leaf,
Thy gathered sheaf,
Lord of my life, my Pride, my Chief,
My Grief!

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE MEN WHO COME BEHIND.
There's a class of men (and women) who are always
on their guard—
Cunning, treacherous, suspicious—feeling softly—
grasping hard—
“ Brainy,” yet without the courage to forsake the
beaten track—
Cautiously they feel their way behind a bolder
spirit's back.
If you save a bit of money, and you start a little
store—
Say, an oyster-shop, for instance, where there
wasn’t one before ;
When the shop begins to pay you, and the rent is
off your mind,
You will see another started by a chap that comes
behind.
So it is, and so it might have been, my friend, with
me and you—
When a friend of both and neither interferes
between the two ;
They will fight like fiends, forgetting in their pas-
sion mad and blind,
That the row is mostly started by the folk who
come behind.
They will stick to you like—sin will—while your
money comes and goes,
But they’ll leave you when you haven’t got a shil-
ling in your clothes.
You may get some help above you, but you’ll
nearly always find
That you cannot get assistance from the men who
come behind.
There are many, far too many, n the world of
prose and rhyme,
Always looking for another’s “ footsteps on the
sands of time.”
Journalistic imitators are the meanest of man-
kind ;
And the grandest themes are hackneyed by the
pens that come behind.
If you strike a novel subject, write it up, and do
not fail,
They will rhyme and prose about it till your very
own is stale,
Like they raved about the region that the wattle-
boughs perfume
Till the reader cursed the bushman and the stink
of wattle-bloom.
They will follow in your footsteps while you’re
groping for the light;
But they’ll run to get before you when they see
you’re going right;
And they’ll trip you up and baulk you in their
blind and greedy heat,
Like a stupid pup that hasn't learned to trail
behind your feet.
Take your loads of sin and sorrow on more ener-
getic backs !
Go and strike across the country where there are
not any tracks !
And—we fancy that the subject could be further
treated here,
But we’ll leave it to be nackneyed by the fellows
in the rear.
HENRY LAWSON.
ON THE BEACH.
Q.
Poisonous, bloated, crab-like shapes
Crawl in gangs around these capes—
Stopping here and feeding there,
Listening, crawling everywhere ;
Searching every rotten weed
With a frothing, wild-eyed greed ;
Fighting o’er a lump of scurf
Or a red boil of the earth ;
Thrusting up their writhing claws
To their grinning, fiend-like maws.
And these horrid creatures wet
With a thick, unwholesome sweat
Have most hideous banquets here
On the poor drowned marineer.
Down they hurry eagerly
Cluttering all the way with glee :
They have smelt the tain red air
From that body festering there.
How they twitch their claws and pry
Into each distorted eye ;
How they spit on him with spite
As their nippers pinch and bite ;
How they strip him clean and bare,
Leaving not a morsel there,
Till they’re gorged and all squat near
Fleshless remnants with a leer.
When the billows near them roll
Each will scoop himself a hole
In the mud-banks, and therein
Sleep like an embodied sin.
In the world so crass and blind
Human crabs feed on the.r kind :
Glutted creatures that devour
All that fall within their power;
Skulking each near his own hole.
They smell out each human soul
Tossed up on Life's stony shore,
Weary, friendless, weak and poor.
ARTHUR A.D. BAYLDON .

added for postage. England £l, with 6s. 6d. added for
postage.

prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 14, 1896.
The Republican Victory.
Last week’s elections have definitely settled
that—bar sudden death or some equally un-
expected circumstance— M‘Kinley will be
the next President of the United States, and
Bryan, of Nebraska, and the bi-metallic and
Freetrade and more or less Single-tax party
are nowhere. The Bryan crowd went down,
apparently, for two principal reasons. The
country has been trying, under Cleveland’s
presidency, a very slight experiment in
Freetrade, and the result has been seri-
ous disaster, just as it was on previous
occasions when the U.S. wandered tem-
porarily into the paths of Shoddy. The
community wants no more Freetrade till the
results of the recent awful experiment are
forgotten, and as Bryan offered more Free-
trade, while M‘Kinley represented the
strongest and most uncompromising variety
of Protection, the Nebraska politician went
under on this question, as a matter of course.
Further, the Democrats who followed Bryan
represented the silver craze in its most
aggravated form, and were even more
vigorous Bimetallists than Freetraders.
Also, they were a great deal more sincere as
Bimetallists than as Freetraders. In fact, it
is doubtful if there was ever much sincerity
about the Democratic party as a Freetrade
organisation, but as it largely represents
the silver-producing States, and its central
idea is that the Federal Treasury should
buy at a fancy price all the silver offered to
it, and coin it into cartwheel dollars for
which there is no demand, and thereby put
an artificial value on the product of the silver
states, its sincerity as a Bimetallic party
was never in question for a moment. And
the Bryan crowd went down because the
great bulk of the community couldn’t see
why the State should spend its gold to boom
the price of silver any more than to boom
the price of coal or bark or salt water, or
any other product that it didn’t want.
* * * m
The Bryan party consisted partly of the
old democratic element in the Southern
States ; partly of the new Silver crowd, the
Single-Tax party, and various other odds and
ends. The old party lines have been practi-
cally obliterated, and the new order of things
has arrived so suddenly that it is difficult to
accurately follow the situation. But the
bulk of the Bryan following was the Silver-
men—one of the many groups of faddists
who profess to believe that all the woes of
this woeful earth can be cured with their one
little nostrum. There are an enormous
number of such fads, and they are about
equally depressing. The extreme Cold-Tea
faction is of opinion that everything that is,
but which shouldn’t be, is the result of
liquor, and that the abolition of liquor will
put all things right. The extreme Freetrade
and Protection and Single-tax apostles, and
the extremists of every other political creed,
are similarly afflicted. Every political faction
of any importance is sub-divided into two
sections ; the moderate men, who believe
that their idea, if adopted, will help to make
the world better, and the fanatics, who hold
that it will make the world quite well, all by
itself, immediately. The Bimetallists con-
tain a very large proportion of fanatics, who
hold that the unlimited coinage of silver will
make the world quite well immediately, and
Bryan, in the main, represented the
crowd which has the silver idea in its
worst form. So it was preached inces-
santly that, with the unlimited coinage
of silver, interest would go down, rents
would diminish, wages would go up, the
grinding tyranny of monopolists, capitalists,
banks, corporations, landlords, and the like
would cease, or at least greatly diminish, and
a halcyon period would arrive when every-
body would be contented and at peace.
• » m *
What would have happened supposing the
Silver Age really had arrived no one knows
for certain—or rather, no one knows for cer-
tain all that would have happened ; but it
would have been a great and beautiful ex-
periment to watch from a safe distance. The
central idea was that the U.S. Treasury
should buy and coin all the silver offered to
it, sixteen ounces of coined silver to be de-
clared worth an ounce of coined gold.
Therefore, all the superfluous silver in the
world would have been brought along to the
U.S. Treasury and sold to the long-suffering
American nation. If it was paid for in paper
the country would become so hopelessly
flooded with Government paper that it would
be next to worthless, and something must
needs break. If it was paid for in gold the
supply of gold would presently run out, and
in that case also something must needs break.
The silver coin couldn’t, so far as appears
from all past experience, be put into circula-
tion. In 1878 the Bland Act, passed by the
influence of the silver men and with a view
to booming their industry, enacted that the
U.S. Mint should coin from two to four
million silver dollars per month ; and the
Sherman Act, passed in 1890, enacted that
silver to the value of 4jy million dollars was
to be purchased and paid for monthly. In
1893 the scheme broke down. The accumu-
lated stock of silver coin which wouldn’t
circulate amounted to something like
400,000,000 dollars. The public had no
use for this stupendous mass of heavy
money. It wasn’t wanted for local cir-
culation. It couldn’t be used to pay the
country’s foreign debts unless it was given
away by weight at its value in the open
market; in other words, for foreign exchange
purposes it was only worth about half its
nominal value, and as the Government
couldn’t get rid of it on that basis without
losing about £40,000,000 on the deal, and as,
of course, foreign countries wouldn’t take it
on any other basis, there was a deadlock.
The notes or “silver certificates” issued
against it only kept up their value because
the Government accepted them in payment
of taxes ; they were partially useless notes
backed up by partially useless coin, and
when the quantity in circulation swelled to
something like 340,000,000 dollars the situa-
tion began to grow serious and it became
obvious that if the system was pushed too far
something disastrous would happen. No
one knew for certain what would happen,
but the silver policy came to a sudden end.
As a certain amount of silver is always
required for small change, and as the small
change gradually wears out, it is probable
that, if coinage stops where it is, the accu-
mulated stock will all drift into circulation in
the course of 500 years or so, and the silver
certificates will be cashed by instalments,
and there will be no great panic or deprecia-
tion or collapse, and the trouble will cure
itself.
* * * *
The policy of the U.S. Government un-
doubtedly did something, while it lasted, to
keep up the price of silver, and thereby
greatly benefited the silver states. But the
Government only did this by buying silver
at more than its value, and stowing it away
in a cellar because it couldn’t sell it again at
its nominal worth, and, provided the cellar
was sufficiently capacious, a similar artificial
value could be given to coal or crockery or
stuffed emus, by exactly the same course of
action. And, as the Government had no use
for the product thus purchased, there seems
to be no reason why it should boom the value
of silver, any more than that of stuffed emus,
at the expense of the nation. But the price
kept declining all the same, because the
Government purchases were not sufficient to
absorb the superfluous output. If all the
nations of the world agreed to make silver
and gold equally legal tender, and to fix the
respective values ah the same figure, there
seems every probability that they could use
up the silver output, and make the price of
the white metal as steady as that of gold.
But, even after allowing for the quantity used
for other than coinage purposes, and for the
demands of the few countries which still
adhere to a silver standard, and for the small
amount used by gold countries for the coinage
of tokens, the surplus is too vast for any one
country, however rich and populous, to absorb,
as the experiences of the U.S. in the past
have fully demonstrated. Yet, in the face
of the huge accummulation of useless dollars
now on hand, the discredited Bryan party
demanded that the nation should buy all the
silver that offered, and pay for it somehow,
and coin tons and tons more of useless money,
and, in short, that it should keep up the
value of silver at any price, for the benefit of
the Western States which want a market for
their metal. The beginning and end of their
policy was to keep up the price of silver by
making the nation buy it for more than it
was worth, and generally “ dash the conse-
quences.” The only probable result would
be that all the country’s gold would gradually
drift away to pay for the silver which poured
in from abroad, and its credit would be pledged
for all it was worth to pay for more silver,
and when its gold and credit were both used
up it would go broke for want of the only
metal in which it could meet its foreign obli-
gations. Then when it stopped buying, the
artificial value of silver would vanish because
the one reliable market had closed up, and the
accumulated mountain of metal would be
worth in the open market about ss. in the
£ on its cost price. The result should have
been the biggest panic the commercial world
has ever seen, and a general falling of the
bottom out of every American institution.
* * * *
If the Bryan party had proposed to make
the United States a silver-standard country,
or to go in for Bimetallism based on some-
thing like the present relative values of the
two metals, it might have passed for a sane
organisation. As it was the question natur-
ally arises : Why should any country set it-
self, at ruinous expense, to keep up the price
of silver throughout the world more than the
price of any other article ? Also, why should
silver necessarily be forced into use for coin-
age purposes more than half-a-dozen other
comparatively rare metals. Iron, copper,
shells, leather, pewter, salt fish and many
other things have all served as currency and
have dropped out again because they became
so plentiful that they ceased to be a con-
venient measure of value. Silver is drop-
ping out in the same gradual fashion, and
why any violent effort should be made to re-
habilitate it any more than to rehabilitate iron
or salt fish it is difficult to understand. The
beautiful things which bimetallists say will
come to pass when their system is adopted
are more than dubious ; their system
had a very long trial in very many
countries, and no millennium ensued.
There were discrepancies, certainly, in the
application of it; it was not a science in
these old days, nor was it worked on the
alleged principles of mathematical accuracy
which are set forth in the theories of to-day;
still it was bimetallism, and it didn’t regene-
rate the earth in the slightest degree. The
cry of the Bimetallist that poverty is caused
by want of the “ meejum of exchange,” and
that if the “ meejum ” —which is now mainly
gold—is increased in quantity by the issue of
an immense mass of white money, a great
want will be filled, comes off rather badly in
view of the fact that the United States
Government is imploring the people to
absorb 400,000,000 dollars of the substance
which is said to be so badly required to
fill the alleged want, and it still lies idle
and useless in the Treasury cellars, and,
instead of filling a want, it has helped
to bring the country to the verge of disaster.
Also, the gorgeous statement of the Bryan
party that mankind “shall not be crucified
on a cross of gold ” sounds well enough, but
probably crucifixion on a cross of silver
would be equally unpleasant. And so long
as the bloated American capitalist owns the
railroads, the banks, the land, bricks and
mortar, stocks, shares and debentures, the
gas and water and tramway concessions, the
mines, and other visible property, he will
own the “meejum of exchange” also,
whether it be gold, paper, silver, copper,
iron, platinum, or anything else. In the
old, old days, long before the time of Jor-
genson’s republic, when salt fish was the
circulating medium in Iceland, the capitalist
crucified his debtor on a cross of dried cod
with just the same facility ; until State
Socialism arrives the cross will be always
handy, and any kind of material will serve
to construct it with.
* * * *
Whether the U.S. silver party will con-
tinue to exist and will fight future elections,
possibly with greater success, or whether it
will evaporate as the Know-Nothings and
other ephemeral political factions have done,
it is impossible to say. One thing seems
reasonably certain : bitter as is the present
hostility between East and West, the talk of
another attempt at secession is altogether
premature. The Solid South, which forms
one end of the Bryan party, has no silver
interests, and would hardly fight for the
mine-owners of the West, and the Western
men have little in common with the interests
of the cotton States. These two sections
were sufficiently united to vote together
against the Republican party, but their ob-
jection to M‘Kinleyism is almost their only
point of agreement. And the Single-tax
party, which is alleged to have formed a
third incongruous element in the Bryan
following, has very little more in common
with the land-holding aristocrats of
Louisiana than with the mining millionaires
of Nevada. If the Bryan party had come
uppermost it would probably have broken to
pieces through internal dissensions, and the
end of it would have been even more frag-
mentary than the beginning.
A Subject of No Popular Interest.
Bathurst, N.S.W., advertises another confer-
ence about Federation. There is no harm in
that. Presumably people are as fond of talk
in Bathurst as elsewhere, and the time and
place of talk are seasonable. Spring is not yet
over; there have been fertilising rains; and
Bathurst is a salubrious township 2100 feet
above sea-level, where abstract questions are
naturally assailed with energy. And if, in the
course of discussion, the pre-eminent claims of
Bathurst to become the federal capital should
outcrop incidentally w'hat odds ? Again :
there is no harm in that. A while ago the
leaders of opinion in Bournemouth, a south-
coast English health-resort, perceived that the
invalids were not flocking thither as they
should. So they invited the British Medical
Association, many hundreds of doctors strong,
to hold a congress at Bournemouth ! And they
dined those doctors, and they wined ’em, and
made flattering speeches to them, and drove
them about, and gave them a good time gener-
ally. The grateful doctors departed, aim next
month such a stream of moneyed consumptives
and paralytics began to flow into Bournemouth
that the boarding-house accommodation had to
be quadrupled, and the pockets of the leaders
of opinion grew too full to chink. Bourne-
mouth—Bathurst—ah ! those busv B’s
Anyway, the more talk about Federation the
better. It will not convince the average Aus-
tralian that Federation is desirable, for the
average Australian has no definite opinions
about the matter, and is incapable of conviction
one way or the other. But it will help to
accustom him to the idea that Federation is
inevitable. That is all that can be hoped from
the average Australian. He'is like Daniel
Lambert, the celebrated fat man, of whom his
mother said, when he was aged 16 and measured
64 inches round the waist : “ Dan doesn’t like
walking, but he moves quite nicely if you shove
him a little.” The average Australian will
move quite nicely into Federation, if you shove
him a little. He will never walk into it of his
own motion, unless under the influence of some
very extraordinary stimulus. And the average
Australian fills three-fourths, perhaps five-
sixths, of this great country. His nationality
varies, but, in the matter of Federation, he is
all tarred with the same apathetic brush.
“Federation!—what is Federation?” “Oh,
something they talk about in Parliament. Haw
anything on Newhaven for the Cup ? ”
• * * *
Slippery Sam Griffith the other day said
words to the effect that since the ’9l Convention
the no-value of a machine-made Federation had
impressed him. That is, he perceives that a
federal constitution theoretically admirable
may be practically useless or dangerous unless
suited to precedent conditions, giving full play
to existing impulses. That’s so, undoubtedly. A
people is an organism which can’t be grown suc-
cessfully like a dwarf tree in a Chinese flower-
pot —however elegant the flower-pot. You can
tether it to principles just as you can tether a
calf to a sapling, but the longer the tether the
better. And it is certain that, by-and-by, you
will have to shift the tether to a sapling further
on. A constitution must leave room for expan-
sion, change of views and change of conditions.
The U.S. constitution-makers recognised this,
but no man’s wit can look ahead two centuries.
The Yankee nation has outgrown the Yankee
constitution, and very soon the constitution
must begin growing too, or something will
snap. Griffith’s thesis was sound all along •
its soundness is a commonplace.
* * * *
But Griffith did not sufficiently elaborate
his thesis. He sees that our federal constitu-
tion must be loose—a big suit for a growing
boy. But he did not say that the looseness
Federation all the easier ; for it is the
tight suit that needs careful fitting. And, once
Federation is put on, it will not come off. No-
body opposes Federation with any warmth
except certain Parliamentary sections. The
average Australian, grafted on an English
stock, exhibits the characteristic English
indifference to ideals. He is occupied with
cricket, and the cheap loaf, and the land-tax
assessment —things which obviously hit a fellow
where he lives. But he has no objection to
Federation. Once plunged into it, he will
swim all right. The new order of things will
suit his equilibrium as well as the old. But he
must be shoved over the edge ; and we look to
publicists and representative men to administer
the shove. If the question rested with the
Australian press, Federation would have been
a fact long ago. The devotion of the press to
the “ fair ideal ” is as marked as the lukewarm
hostility of politicians. Unfortunately, the
average Australian mostly leaves his politics to
be managed by men with loud mouths and
mean motives, who are always ready to roll
their little logs in the path of national progress.
And—worse luck ! —the question of Federation
rests with the politicians.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Ass on “Freedom.”
Maoriland National Ass party appeals to the
electors of the Cold Tea Land with one of those
large, vague, hazy programmes which mean any-
thing or something or nothing, and which don’t
commit the party to one thing more than
another. The programme is a mass of un-
defined bad intentions seen vaguely through a
fog ; it doesn’t contain a straight-out assurance
that the proposed Ass Government will do one
single definite act, but only promises that it
will hover round according to certain principles
not very clearly stated. The party is in favor
of reducing taxation and gradually paying off
the public debt; this is about the only reason-
ably distinct item in its manifesto, and it is
about as distinct as a mirage and not quite so
definite as a Mahatma. Every other party is
in favor of the same things, but as the. debt can’t
be reduced unless more revenue is raised, and
taxation can’t be reduced without increasing


the debt, the Ass party might as well state that
it is in favor of the abolition of sin and believes
in having a full moon every night. It doesn’t
even profess to show how it can reduce debt
and taxation at the same time ; it is only in
favor of these things happening if they
manage to come about. The freehold system
of land tenure is to be “preserved and
encouraged ”; it always has been preserved, so
that is nothing, and how it is to be encouraged
doesn’t appear. The “speedy utilisation” of
Native lands is another item, but whether this
means voluntary oj compulsory purchase, or
lease, or pure steal, is unexplained. The Ass
party also believes in as little as possible
“ State interference with industrial and com-
mercial enterprise.” This might mean that the
Tory crowd wanted to abandon the State enter-
prises already on hand, and sell the State rail-
ways, and shut up the State Land Bank and
State Life Insurance Department, and put the
B.N.Z. again under private management, and
dispose in one lump of the estates acquired
under the Land Settlement policy, and sur-
render the Midland line to the old syndicate.
Or it might mean that the party only
intended going in for no new enterprises.
Or, again, it might mean that the Russell-
Stout crowd was in favor of Freetrade as
against Protection, or it might mean almost
auything. Some further obscurity is shed on
the subject by the announcement that the Tory
organisation is in favor of “ freedom for men,
freedom for industry, and freedom for enter-
prise.” Naturally enough, the question arises
here : What is the Ass’s idea of freedom ? It
may mean liberty for every man to work his
employes 12 hours a day in an insanitary work-
shop, or liberty to start swindling companies,
or to pay sweater’s wages, or to be poisoned by
unspeakable grog, or to charge 250 per shent,
or to have open drains on his premises, or to
sell typhoid germs in his milk, or any other
kind of liberty. There are quite as many bad
varieties of freedom as good ones; the
word sounds large and beautiful, but it
isn’t a thing to be taken on trust.
Whenever freedom is conferred on one indi-
vidual ft means restriction on another. Even
the emancipation of the enslaved nigger wasn’t
all freedom ; the law conferred on the nigger
the privilege of owning himself, and it took
away from the white man the privilege of own-
ing the nigger, and some of him still defines
liberty as the right of the Caucasian to own and
wallop the African as he feels inclined. There-
fore, when the Maoriland Tory party says
vaguely that it is in favor of freedom it is a fair
thing to ask it whether it proposes to free the
sweater, and the land-grabber, and the money-
lender, and the jerry-builder, and the bogus
bank man, and the usurer, and the syndicator,
and the importer of foreign goods made by
pauper labor, and the Chow and Jap and coolie
—or if it means to set free the other fellow.
Until this point is cleared up—and the Tory
party carefully refrains from clearing it up—
Maoriland should keep strictly clear of the
Russell crowd and all its works.
♦
The Poor Old Gentleman.
The plea constantly advanced by the Vic. Fat
Man crowd, and the Argus, and the National
Ass following generally, against any alteration
of the Vic. company law in the direction of
increased stringency, is that it will drive “ men
of the right stamp ” out of the directing busi-
ness. Also, it will lead to the retirement of
“able business men,” and “men of probity
and acumen,” and “men of intelligence and
standing,” and “ men with a stake in the
country,” and other beautiful sorts and varieties
of men too numerous to mention. This course
of reasoning naturally implies that these men
are already in the business ; if they are not in
it they can’t be driven out of it, and the
country can’t lose their valuable services if it
hasn’t got them, and it is impossible for it to be
ruined by their departure if they are not there.
Under these circumstance the line of argument
adopted by Purves, who belongs to the crowd
above-mentioned, in the City of Melbourne
Bank case, is interesting and casts a lot of in-
structive darkness on a matter of some import-
ance. According to Purves, the four directors
on trial, Loader, Williamson, Collier and
Roberts, were mere imbecile tools in the hands
of the Man that’s Dead. “ What chance,” he
asked, “ had these poor old gentlemen with a
combination like that of Fink and Long-
muir ? ” Evidently, on Purves’s line of
argument, they had none at all. They were
four old, weak, foolish, doddering persons full
of senile good intentions, but, in a business
capacity, of no account at all. And, by a
curious coincidence, almost every other com-
pany-director who has been in trouble in Vic-
toria for the last four years has also been clearly
proved, by his lawyer, to be an irresponsible
person, on the verge of idiocy or something, and
utterly incapable of running a company intelli-
gently. This has become so much a matter of
course that it is about time for the Vic. direc-
tor to decide which capacity he will per-
manently assume out of the two that are open
to him. Hitherto, when company reform was
in question, the Vic. director has been an able
and brainy man who mustn’t be scared out of
the business for the sake of any fancied reform,
because his brightness and acumen and mental
grasp make his services absolutely priceless.
And when company dishonesty is under dis-
cussion he generally hires a lawyer to tell the
jury that he is a poor, weak, incapable old
dodderer who dodders so badly that he
can’t be held responsible for anything,
and that no one could possibly take him seri-
ously as a director or as anything else of import-
ance. Obviously, however, he can’t go on in
this dual capacity for ever. If he is the bril-
liant person that he is alleged to be when times
are good, then he must be held fully responsible
for his actions, and it must be assumed that he
knows his business and isn’t built to be the
helpless tool of Longmuir or any other corpse.
And if he is the amiable wreck which he is said
to be when times are bad, then a law is badly
wanted to reform the present kind cf director
out of existence. He can’t be, at one and the
same time, so valuable that the province must
put up with any risk for fear of losing him, and
so weak and useless that he can plead some-
thing that sounds very like incipient insanity
when he is accused of wrongdoing. Which of
the two capacities is he going to stick to ?
And if lie won’t stick to either, will Parliament
take the responsibility of making him per-
manent one way or the other, so that the com-
munity will know how to take him ?
The N.S.W. Land-Tax Muddle.
The great swear of the man whose land-tax
schedule has come to hand still rages through-
out N.S.W. Since last week the reports and
the bad language have had time to arrive from
the backblocks, and it appears that the valua-
tions are about equally astounding in all
parts of the province. A man who has just
sold a bit of land at Longueville (Sydney) for
£5O receives a tax-paper in which it is valued
at <£7oo. Another individual who owns a
stony waste out back, many miles from any-
where, was recently offered by the Government
ss. per acre for two acres to be resumed for
some public purpose. The agriculturist didn’t
object much; in his own discouraged way he
reckoned it was about all the place was worth ;
but now that the local assessor wants to tax
his property on a value of £56 an acre even he
rears up. A Sydney man received two valua-
tions inside a month ; one was a statement from
the Government that it would give him £6O
for an acre of his land which was to be
resumed, and the other was a land-tax
valuation which assessed the same ground at
over <£Boo an acre. A person at North Shore
who is selling his land at from <£6 to <£B per
foot feels annoyed at being taxed on £2l, and a
Rockdale man who is trying to sell a block of
land and a large building for £7OOO uses cuss
words because the land alone is valued at
£10,950. These are a few out of hundreds of
tales that are wailed forth in the daily papers
and elsewhere. The Reid Government is
hustling through a bill to extend the time for
making appeals, it being quite evident that it
will be impossible to deal with a quarter of
them in the period originally allowed. Also, it
promises that liberal reductions will be made,
and there is even talk of a general re-assessment.
And there are other complications. The exten-
sion of the days of grace will carry the collec-
tion of the tax into 1897, and, as even the Reid
Government can’t well start collecting the
second instalment of the tax till the first one is
dealt with, the great idea of getting 18 months’
tax into one financial year begins to look un-
workable. The reductions will necessarily re-
duce the proceeds of the tax considerably. The
re-assessments, and appeals, and the £1 a day
to be paid to assessors for attending appeal
courts, will swell the expense of collection
enormously and further reduce the net proceeds.
The moral stench of the affair will seriously
damage the Ministry, for it is difficult to believe
that there could have been such a wide and
general and unanimous inflation unless the
assessors were instructed to inflate ; at least it
will take a lot of very tough swearing to con-
vince the public that it was all a huge coinci-
dence. Likewise, there are ugly stories about
valuations being increased after they left the
assessor’s hands, and it will take more hard
swearing to dissipate them. Altogether, re-
garded as a muddle, this muddle about breaks
the N.S.YV. record.
The S.A. Drought.
South Australia is apparently on the verge of
a worse drought than even that of last year ; in
fact, it is announced that the crops are utterly
ruined over the largest part of the agricultural
area. The Government already admits that the
case is desperate, and is coming to the rescue.
It proposes making loans on easy terms to those
farmers who have any assets left as security,
and offers to take the most liberal view possible
of the security ; also, it promises to supply seed
to those who have no security to offer; and
private subscriptions are being taken up to
assist in saving those who are in the worst state
of destitution from absolute starvation. South
Australia is none too prosperous at the best of
times ; it is a country with a huge debt, the
smallest gold output in Australasia (and, con-
sidering how often the gold-mining industry has
helped the other provinces through desperately
tight places, this is a serious deficiency), no
silver output, hardly any local coal-supply,
a great deal of desert, about the smallest rain-
fall in Australasia, and a want of almost every-
thing that has helped toshove the other provinces
along. Agriculture has always been about its
strongest point, and now that this industry has
practically gone smash under the influence of
the drought the prospects of the province are
lamentable. The prospects of the Government
are also none too bright. In its last financial
statement, seeing a small surplus ahead, it
followed the usual fatuous Australian policy of
reducing taxation in order to swallow up the
balance before it arrived, and it did this so
effectually that, even on its own figures, there
was a deficiency in front of it. When the
drought has reduced the railway revenue
and the land revenue and' the income-tax
returns, and Parliament has voted a large
sum to relieve the unexpected distress,
the Kingston Ministry will probably realise
that in matters of money, it is, like most
other Australian Ministries, a pretty dismal
failure. Meanwhile the province is struggling
with another serious problem of finance. In
ordinary seasons, S.A. is a large exporter of
grain, and the duties exist mainly as a matter
of form, for there are no grain imports. This
year it is possible that the province won’t sup-
ply its own requirements, and at the best the
surplus will be microscopic. If the supply
doesn’t meet requirements grain must be im-
ported, and the duty, in that case, may possibly
increase the price, and the cheap-loaf party is
already demanding that the tariff in this res-
pect should be suspended for a time. One side
of the question is that if the duty is tempor-
arily removed wheat may be cheaper—and, as a
natural consequence, the agriculturist will get
less for the scanty crop that remains, and
low prices will be added on to deficient quan-
tity to accelerate his ruin. The case for the
suspension, therefore, rests mainly on the doc-
trine of the cheap loaf. The case against the
suspension is that the maintenance of the tariff
may, in this instance, make bread dearer;
therefore, the agriculturist will get better
prices, and instead of having to stand the loss
all by himself, it will be spread over the entire
community, and thus the bucolic inhabitant
may be able to struggle through and keep out
of the Benevolent Asylum until better times
arrive. Also, incidentally, the wheat duty
will bring in a little revenue, and thus help to
keep the Treasury solvent at a time when sol-
vency is a matter of importance. There are
two aspects to the cheap loaf problem at all
times, and in the present case the probabilities
are that the duty wall remain. Just now a
very cheap loaf would probably finish the pro-
cess of ruin and compel South Australia to file
its disconsolate schedule by the Great Austra-
lian Bight.

Queensland’s Defection.
Westralia has passed the Federation Bill, and
five of the provinces are thus brought into line
on the subject of Australian Unity. Queens-
land has decided to be the one dissen-
tient. Its Government, after some more dead-
lock, intimated, the other day, that unless the
Council gave in the Kanaka province would
take no hand in the approaching Convention.
Whereupon the Council threw the mangled car-
case of the Bill back over the fence for the
several-dozenth time, and Queensland definitely
stands out. Under these circumstances Premier
Reid, if he really has any sympathy with
Federation, has no excuse for delaying matters
any longer. Tasmania and South Australia are
understood to be in agreement with Premier
Turner’s idea that a move should be made with
a view to holding the Convention early next
year, but they have hitherto been waiting for
New South Wales, and New South Wales, as
represented by G. H. Reid, decided, wisely
enough, to wait for Westralia and Queensland.
But now that Westralia has come in and
Kanakaleperland isn’t coming at all, there is
nothing more to wait for, and delay only means
letting the subject grow cold, and giving the
public enthusiasm time to die out until it is
even more dead than at present. The present
time and situation are the most favorable that
couldbe imagined for going on with the work. The
absence of Queensland is not only the best thing
that could have happened for the Federation,
but it is also the best thing that could have
happened for the true interests of Queensland
itself. Had all the six provinces been repre-
sented, Tasmania would probably have sent a
majority of Tory delegates, and the Queensland
and Westralian contingents, being appointed by
Tory legislatures, would have been almost
wholly Tory ; and as a consequence the repre-
sentatives from the other three provinces and
any casual Liberals who might be sent by
chance from the stagnant regions would have
made a very small Democratic majority indeed.
A convention, with Queensland and Judas
Griffith included, would be much more likely
to draw up a constitution on fine old bigoted
lines than one without Queensland, where the
three progressive provinces will form a strong
majority. When the Kanaka State comes into
the Federation later on, as it almost certainly
will, it will have to accept a ready-made system
of Government, and as the sort of men whom
the Nelson Government would have appointed
had no hand in its construction it will be all the
better for the white Queenslander of the future.
Despite Nelson’s efforts to make the Northern
delegation a strictly Tory and wholly unrepre-
sentative affair it would have been bad policy
for the other provinces to attempt or even pro-
pose to shut Queensland out, but the fact that
she has decided to shut herself out for the pre-
sent is rather a matter for congratulation than
otherwise, and the other Australias should take
her at her word and go straight ahead.

Loans and Labor.
Editor Bulletin, —The loan-hunger of the
Australias gives sign of breaking out afresh,
and should recall attention to the advantages of
internal over external borrowings—-long pointed
out by The Bulletin. An external loan is a
lasting tax, in goods, on the country that bor-
rows, making the debtor a slave to the creditor
and the market fluctuations in the value of
his produce. On the other hand, an internal
loan may be self - destructive : money bor-
rowed from people who have to pay the
interest on it, is really taking from one
pocket and putting in the other. Let a society
of ten persons borrow from each of its members
£1 ; as a society they would owe £lO ; as indi-
viduals they would be creditors for the amount
of their liability. And the principle is the
same irrespective of amount. The present con-
dition of France, with the largest debt in the
world, compares very favorably with that of
most other countries, even practically debtless
Germany; because the debt is paid by French-
men, who both receive and pay the interest;
and a community, like an individual, however
big its dealings, is solvent while the books
balance. Its only extravagance is waste or idle
labor. Finally, loans are so much goods
advanced by the creditor. Goods stand for
labor, a big loan being a big lending of labor
and the interest is paid in labor. So that the
question resolves itself into this : “Shall we
labor for ourselves or for other people ? ” —H.F.
Attorney-General Isaacs’ constitutional point
in re a flaw in the constitution of the Yic. Land-
lords’ House continues to excite interest in the
South. If there’s anything in the point, it is
to be hoped that the Yic. democrats will refuse
to remedy the trouble at any less price than
the Referendum, and a referendum of a nature
which will practically shelve the Landlords’
House altogether. But, from all appearances,
there is little in the point. In the legal
view, the consolidation carried out by the late
Chief Justice was intended to leave the law
exactly as it was before, and one case decides
that that was so in that particular case. (The
only legal crumb of comfort is contained in a
letter by one of the collaborators in the con-
solidation to the effect that nothing was really
omitted, the obvious inference being that the
Council constitution was “ cronk ” before the
consolidation.) Practically, the flaw means
little for another six years or so, anyway. It
affects elected members only, but, as Council
members are usually unopposed, that doesn’t
signify much. And with regard to the elected
members, the time for appeal has gone by, and,
if it hadn’t, the Elections and Qualifications
Committee would meet and declare the members
fully qualified—which would finish the matter
till next election. But it’s useful to have a
whip of some kind, and doubtful constitutional
points can usually be made very effective whips
by tactful and resourceful Assembly leaders.
There is a fishy odour about a small Mining
Act Amendment Bill which passed almost un-
noticed through YVestralian Parliament during
the last few days of the session. A little before,
a private Bill to empower a certain speculator
to erect poles and convey electric wires over
Crown and certain private lands, in connection
with a large electric-power-transmission scheme,
had been mooted. The Bill provided for a
deposit and the observance of conditions strict
enough, in the opinion of the promoters, to
allow it to run the gauntlet of public and par-
liamentary criticism. It was late in the session,
and in order to get the Bill through the suspen-
sion of the standing orders re private bills
was necessary. As the Attorney-General had
been secured as a shepherd for the Bill, no
trouble was anticipated by the promoters, but
the apple-cart was upset by the Speaker ruling
the motion for the suspension of the standing
orders out of order on the ground that it was
not proper to so relax the safeguards of the
public interest. The Attorney-General in vain
protested against the ruling, but “ got there”
just the same. Immediately he introduced a
short Mining Act Amendment Bill, which
would enable the Govt, to give power to erect
poles and carry wires over holdings, leases, &c.,
under the Mining Act, subject to such con-
ditions and stipulations as the Ministry should
impose and to regulations which might be made
under the Act. The Bill passed both Houses
without its identity with the wrecked measure
being noticed—or, at least, mentioned. The
incident is a fine object-lesson re the methods
of private enterprise in Westralia. The moral
seems to be that if one wants a concession it
is wise to enlist the sympathy of a Minister ol
the Crown, and if one then cannot carry his
little Bill in at the front-door, the Minister
will take it in at the back.
Stumpjumfer to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin,—l notice Sydney Daily Telegraph
gushes every Friday morning- over the number of people
“ permanently” settled on the land since the ’95 Act came
into operation. At the first opportunity, when the
“strongman” who takes credit for discovering that the
Almighty didn’t make the whole of N.S.W. worth 20s. an
acre (selectors have been shouting the fact for years) is
engaged in his favorite occupation of booming his hope-
lessly-muddled department, some country member with a
hard, unenthusiastic face ought to rise and enquire if, say,
6 per cent, of the number stated to be already settled in
the Telegraph’s weekly whoop, “ Lands Office Day,” are
actually in legal possession of the holdings applied for.
It does not follow that because two or three thousand
would-be settlers have lodged their cash at the Lands
Office and duly completed their side of the bargain, that
the Lands Minister has performed his. Not by a whole
cask-full. The free-raid Govt, still has its clutches
on a deposit I lodged with an application for land nearly
a year ago. Over seven months passed before the local
Land Board sat, and then I found that my application
was not even set down for a first hearing. As it might be
another seven months before the Board came round again,
and still another seven before the surveyor turned up —
and life is uncertain—I decided to apply for a refund of
my money whilst there was yet time to spend it. lam
still waiting to spend it. After I withdrew, another ap-
plicant lodged a deposit for the same block. My money,
doubtless, helped to swell the last free-raid surplus ; the
other applicant’s deposit, perhaps both, will be kept in
hand to add to another surplus—or reduce a deficit.
Meanwhile, the land for which two deposits have been
paid still remains undealt with. It would be interesting
to learn how much of the Reid surplus will have to be
returned to disgusted applicants. Little things like these
make the unsettled “settler” ask how long it will be
before a “ Select Committee ” or a “ Royal Commission ”
is appointed to enquire into the how-not-to-do-it methods
of that hide-bound collection of sleepy fossils in N.S.W
Lands Department. How long, O Lord ! how long?
Extract from article by Max O’Rell in North
American Review :
I know of one country only where the government by
woman was given a fair trial, and that is New Zealand.
The law was passed and the experiment was made. The
law had to be repealed after six months.
Quite so. But Max missed an excellent oppor-
tunity to describe the struggle which preceded
the said repeal. The last Maoriland Parlia-
ment, elected under adult (male and female)
suffrage, passed an Act empowering the police-
force, composed entirely of Social Purity Society
females, to arrest any male found in the streets
after sunset, take him to the nearest police-
station, and detain him there until 10 a.m. the
next day. Driven frantic by this calculated
assault on its virtue, the male population
flew to arms under the leadership of Capt,
Russell, and after numerous sanguinary battles
stormed Parliament House. Entering the Legis-
lative Chamber, the Captain, pointing to the
cradle in which lay the sleeping infant of the
Speakeress, shouted “Take away that bauble ! ”
All females were then expelled, and an Act
passed by the males present abolishing Female
Suffrage and ordering the pubs, to remain open
all night. Thus ended a memorable epoch in
the history of Maoriland.
The Tory party in Vie. Assembly made a
great effort to rush into the One Adult One
Vote Bill a casual clause largely reducing
the number of members in the Assembly.
The reduction in the membership of the
Legislative Council could be effected, the
MTntyre party blandly remarked, by another
bill later on. Why, cert’nly ! The Council
would cheerfully pass a measure reducing the
membership of the Assembly, partly because
the fewer members the larger the electorates,
and the more expensive the elections, and the
less chance for the candidate without money.
Also, partly because a scheme must be devised
some day to remedy deadlocks, and the simplest
and most probable device in such cases is to let
the two Houses sit as one and decide the dis-
puted point by a majority of the whole Legis-
lature, and then the bigger the Fat Man Cham-
ber is in proportion to the other the better.
The enthusiasm of the Tory party for reducing
the Assembly membership now and reducing the
Council later on when there is more time is a
beautiful sentiment.

Exhibition Building Carnival of All Nations.
“Romeo and Juliet,” originally staged at
Sydney Lyceum for three nights, did' so well that
it was afterwards extended to six, and the first
Australian production of Dumas’ “Francillon”
was shifted on from Saturday to Wednesday
(yesterday) night. Whether it will really appear
yesterday night, or whether an intense demand for
more Romeo and an increased supply of Juliet will
again upset the arrangements before this paper
comes out, it is impossible to say at time of writing.
There are a lot of things about the present tragedy
to justify the popular demand. It is beautifully
dressed and staged and there is a great deal of it;
the first night it lasted from 7.45 to 11.20, and the
public has seldom got so much Juliet for its money.
Mrs. Potter is responsible for a lot of this elonga-
tion ; when she gets the stage to herself Juliet
wanders dreamily around with her hair down her
back, and her robes drifting behind her, and visions
in her eye, and attitudinises to the verge of
despair. Also, she looks out at the stars a
great deal, and throws herself down on all the
furniture in succession, and then a sudden idea
strikes her and she throws herself up again. Still,
though she is more prolonged than most Juliets,
Mrs. Potter makes a reasonably good heroine,
and is brighter and more natural than usual, and
her elocution is far above the Potteresque average,
and the metallic ring in her voice is less pro-
nounced than it generally is, and she hardly
wriggles once, and she has got hold of the audi-
ence all the time. The Mercutio of Scot Inglis is
a fine, finished performance with hardly a flaw in
it, and is probably the best thing in the tragedy ;
and Bellew is a good enough Romeo, though he
hardly looks so young as that glittering Italian
ought to be, and he is even damper and more
weepful than the part seems to call for. The rest
of the co. includes an aggressively nude apothe-
cary — The Bulletin never saw such a nude
apothecary before, and when he sells the poison to
Romeo, there is a dreadful moment when it
seems as if his dish-cloth was going to fall off alto-
gether—and various others, who are all good
enough for any practical purpose.
The Bulletin, being a Goth and a Vandal,
candidly admits that it is somewhat tired of
“Romeo and Juliet.” Also, it believes that the
bulk of the community doesn’t care a straw for
that production in a literary sense, but is afraid
to say so, and that the play’s real popularity de-
pends largely on its intense hugginess. The fact
that Mrs. Potter hugs beautifully has made the
present “Romeo” season a decided success.
Shakespeare is a universally popular author whom
the mass of the community never reads, and the
most beloved dramatist on earth whom hardly
anybody goes to see for his own sake, and one of the
troubles of our effete civilisation is that it is neces-
sary in the name of culchaw to profess to love the
unread dramatist with an intense affection. There-
fore this paper has occasionally thought of starting
a society for being somewhat tired of the immortal
Shakesbacon, on the ground that it is better to be
a Goth and be candidly indifferent to him than
drag the corpse of the alleged enthusiasm around
any longer. Also it holds that the said enthusiasm
has become a corpse mostly because everybody
asserts so persistently that it is alive, and that the
Immortal Bard is being conventionally hoorayed
to death. In other words, it is afraid that if
people aren’t allowed to openly hate Shakespeare
they will presently give up liking him altogether,
and something bad will happen to the Bard. The
annual subscription to the new society for being
rather tired of Shakespeare will be 2s. 6d.
* * * *
After the rush of the “ only authorised. ”
cinematographe, and the one that was partially
authorised, and the only one that didn’t say
whether it was authorised or not, Jimmy
MacMahon has opened still another cinema-
tographe in Pitt-street, opposite Sydney Lyceum.
This is the “ only real ” cinematographe—at least,
The Bulletin has heard so — and it is certainly
real enough for all purposes. The pictures are
beautifully clear and bright, and well-defined and
lifelike. In the ladies’ duel, the combatants
hustle off their skirts and emerge in reform cos-
tume, and prod each other in a masterly fashion.
The riding lesson, where the horse bucks every-
body off, is guaranteed genuine, for the cloud of
dust which arises when the rider’s head bangs
against the ground is clearly visible. The Royal
Artillery is about the most realistic picture in the
collection—the hundreds and thousands of uni-
formed warriors gallop past with their guns, in an
ever darkening haze as the dust-cloud which they
raise as they go rages more and more furiously ;
but the audience pins its faith principally to the
dancing girl—Loie Fuller, life-size, in about 25’
colors, amid a whirlwind of ankles and gaudy
skirts, and wild back-hair. Also, it loves a certain
upside-down picture of a busy street, where every-
thing is working backwards from its destination
to the place it started from. Some day The
Bulletin intends to fix up a picture of an execu-
tion on the same principle. It will begin with the
dissection of the criminal. Then he will go
together, and hang dead at the end of the rope,
and in a minute or two he will drop up alive and
retire to his cell and be untried. After which he
will go to his dead victim, and murder him back-
wards, thereby bringing him to life again. There
ought to be a fine moral lesson in this somewhere
—this paper doesn’t quite know where.
* * * *
The cinematographe is a very simple
machine after all. It works in a small back-room.
There are 3672 wheels in it, and each separate pic-
ture is made up of 30,000 photos, or thereabouts.
There are sundry electric wires about, which
would probably strike Jimmy MacMahon dead on
the spot if he got mixed up in them, and the light
is produced by a lighting apparatus which gives
out a heat of about 150 deg. Fahr. If any one of
the 3672 wheels goes wrong, or the wires trip up,
or anything sticks or bursts or slides, then several
thousand pounds worth of show will go to flinders
on the spot, and the very last picture of all will be
a distracted MacMahon trying to struggle out
from under the ruins.
* * * *
Bland Holt leaves Sydney Royal after
Monday, and the fragmentary but gaudy “ St.
Paul’s ” will last out the brief remainder
of the season. The realism of the conflagra-
tion scene, when the Panther’s intoxicated hus-
band sets fire to the gilded haunt of sin and
wassail and dissipated evening-dress female, will
probably keep the show on a sound financial basis
to the end, despite the fact that Sutton Vane’s
tale seems to have been built in sections and then
dashed together with a club. George Rignold
will be the next occupant of the Royal, and as
Sydney has always had a warm spot in its heart
for Henry V., and he has been absent long enough
to be seriously missed, he should get a reception
which will make the ancient foundations shake.
* m * *
The melodrama with the stuffed reptile in
it is steadily pursuing its habitual path of suicide,
premeditated butchery, crime, riot, arson, battle,
murder, forgery, and sudden death, and Her
Majesty’s is still doing well on the strength of it.
With a view, however, to the fact that even a
drama where everybody bathes in gore can’t go on
for ever, “Face to Face” is in preparation for
Saturday.
* * * *
Nothing special has to be chronicled this
week about Sydney Tivoli, beyond the fact that
the Vaidis sisters have arrived, and have proved
to be reckless persons, whose trapeze performance
will some day lead to there being only one sister,
and afterwards no sister at all. Apart from this,
it is only necessary to mention that the old show
is prospering the same as usual, and that Harry
Rickards is accumulating more money than any
one man ought to have in a democratic com-
munity.
* * * *
“ A Mother of Three,” at Melb. Bijou, is
discussing unstable trousers-buttons and the
horrors of solitary confinement for her legs in the
presence of crowded audiences. This comedy
tickles the soft female fancy, and makes strong
men more than ever doubtful of Mrs. Grundy’s
bona-fides. It seems to The Bulletin that
Brough’s unfettered show is under the patronage
of Mrs. G., who brings her girls along to laugh at
a long series of such broad suggestions and spicy
innuendoes as would strike the grim female stiff if
they were perpetrated in private life within her
family’s hearing. Therefore the complete success
of “ A Mother of Three ” is a matter for con-
gratulation. Written by a woman for women, it
will be accepted as an object-lesson in humor to suit
the fair sex. The politician of the future, wooing
female suffrage from a public platform, will know
the exact sort of funny remark to win a vote of
confidence with, and the bluer the Tory the bigger
will be his majority on polling-day. Meanwhile a
daily paper has remarked, with artless enthusiasm,
that Mrs. Brough deserves praise for not empha-
sising the cerulean points in her character-part,
and several newspapers have stated that the dia-
logue is very witty. Anyway, the show thrives
exceedingly, and everybody is glad to find the
Brough company travelling on top of the tide.
* * * *
George Rignold’s advt. of “Henry V”
claims that “there is no vicious or evil thought
suggested from beginning to end.” But in all other
respects Shakespeare’s historical play is quite up to
date. King Henry, before and after Agincourt,
appoints himself special agent for Providence, just
as though he were the late King William or his
reigning grandson. Also the gallant monarch’s
threats of what would happen to the town of
Harfleur, if his troops were-compelled to carry it
by storm, might have been inspired by the very
latest details of Turkish atrocities in Armenia.
And what an immortal jingo was the Bard ! There
is one craven Hinglishman introduced into
“ Henry V,” yet he shines by comparison with his
French captive, whom he drags along with a rope.
Lord Scroope, the leading villain of the
piece, is a professional pietist of the modern
type, and the discontented soldier, complaining
that a battle is a bad end for any poor
private who may happen to get killed, speaks with
the tongue of Tommy Atkins. Even the funny
business in “Henry V.” has retained its original
freshness, because it deals chiefly with the unalter-
able vanities of human nature under the influence
of liquor. Altogether the production at Mel-
bourne Royal is a worthy thing, which appears to
have earned for Rignold Rex a lot more glory out
of doors than financial profit on the premises.
Audiences last week were large, but not large
enough for a show that employs 100 people on the
stage, so unless the fat-headed public should
become suddenly seised—as they say in Parlia-
ment —of the excellence of this fascinating jingo
lesson, it will be replaced on Saturday by some-
thing better suited to their lack of intelligence.
George Rignold’s season will conclude next week
in any case.
