Next Thursday (19th) is the date appointed
for a huge matinee combination show in aid of
Melb. Blind Asylum, or, rather, for the advance-
ment of Lady Brasseys latest charity boom. The
Blind Asylum is a much smaller consideration
than Lady B. in this matter, but the money will
get there all the same.
** * *
The utterly conscienceless cussedness of
the Law and its myrmidons was exposed once
again last week at Melb. Exhibition Carnival. It
was the week in which the Government ordains a
public holiday for the benefit of all bookmakers,
the week when the hideous hypocrisy of the
Gambling Act is more hideous than usual, and a
rejected Totalisator Bill rises from a champagne-
sprinkled grave to jeer at the sweated punters who
put its opponents in power. On Saturday
night four detectives came round to warn
the Carnival management that race-games at
sixpence and a shilling per game were illegal, im-
moral, desperately wicked, and not to be any
longer endured by the authorities. This action is
said to have sprung from the case of a young
criminal tarradiddler, who pleaded that he had
been “ruined by gambling at church bazaars.”
Tickets in the golden art union have not been sup-
pressed, however ; likewise, the legality of Fitz-
geralds’ Circus remains unquestioned, so the
Carnival will proceed as before.

The Frantz Family and the ’Graphe figure
in large type on a satisfactory bill at the Opera
House, but the bills of the Alexandra and Cam-
bridge are withdrawn from circulation, and those
two receptacles for ill-starred enterprise have
lapsed into their normal condition of dust and
darkness.

The Carnival concert by Melbourne
Liedertafel, last week, was not far behind the best
work of its rival, the Metropolitan, in accuracy of
part singing. Perhaps the Metropolitan Lieder-
tafel male choristers, apart from the Associated
Ladies’ choir, are less obviously mechanical than
the other crowd, and if they have a slightly in-
ferior voice judged in the bulk, the difference is
not worth remarking. One well-trained Lieder-
tafel is very like another. Miss Edith O. King,
from Sydney, was the special new soloist for the
occasion, and artistically she filled the bill. But
in the big Town Hall, her light soprano seemed a
rather small matter compared with Maggie
Stirling’s fruity mezzo, which, by the way, was
“commanded” to warble Strezlecki’s “Dreams”
for His Excellency Brassey and suite. Gov. B.
has a sailor’s simple musical taste. Give him a
ballad, with a good tangible tune in it, in prefer-
ence to operatic kickshaws, you know.
* * *■ *
Theatrical criticism in Melbourne Age and
Argus has of late been contemptible, sometimes
for its unfairness, and, almost invariably, for its
display of ignorance. Whilst anything staged at
the. Princess’s is pretty sure of the puff direct, pro-
ductions at the Royal are damned with faint
praise or mild disparagement. Allowing that the
relative justice or injustice of newspaper praise
and blame is a matter of opinion, nobody can deny
the absurdity of such stuff as the Age published
re George Rignold’s present revival of “Henry
V.,” a drama with which the veteran’s name is
identified all over the English-speaking world.
“ It was in this play,” says the Age critic, “ that
Mr. Rignold first made the acquaintance of a Mel-
bourne audience 12 years ago. . . . He is a
little too ample in girth and limb to correctly
represent the physical aspect of Shakespeare’s
youthful hero, but all the kingly dignity and
resolve and courage of the young man suddenly
ennobled and grown grave with weight of office
he portrays with a high measure of success.”
Twelve years ago Rignold was in management at
Melb. Opera House, where he appeared in
numerous plays previous to the revival of Henry
V. His first acquaintance with Melb. audiences
had been made long before then at the Royal. As
regards his present impersonation of Shakespeare’s
“youthful hero,” what are the facts? Rignold is
to all stage appearances a handsome, sturdy hero
of, ,say, 35. The real Henry was in his 28th
year at the Battle of Agincourt, and when
the Treaty of Troyes (where Shakespeare’s
drama concludes) • • arrived, the “youthful”
monarch had reached the age of 32. There
is really no excuse for captious criticism of
Rignold s present girth and limb measurements
in his famous impersonation, whereas there is
much cause for praising his elocution, by-play, and
naturalism, which, by comparison with the Age’s
extravagant lauding of less accomplished per-
formers in less exacting parts, are scarcely praised
at all. For a daily newspaper to say that Rignold
as Henry Y. achieves “ a high measure of success ”
is to insinuate that several other actors could be
found to do the work as well as he, if not better.
Where are they? ,
At Poverty Point.

standings ought to be put to death as a public enemy.

menally auriferous Coolgardie district, and finds a gold-
mine at one fell swoop. If that advt. doesn’t give a fillip
to the wild-oat trade, London must indeed have had
enough of Westralian speculation for the present.

We want actresses as sensitive, as delicate, as highly-

The news that Frances Saville has instituted divorce-
proceedings against her husband falls very flat. So many
lady artists have made connubial shipwreck that, as an
advt., the incident, like the lost jewel-case, has grown
stale. Only one resource is left to;.the would-be celebrity
or prima-donna—to get her nanie involved in a scandal
with a king. The public appreciation of soiled doves is
insatiable if the mud be aristocratic.
Infatuated Irvingites waited outside London Lyceum
for twelve hours, so as to ensure a ‘ first night ’ view of
their much-mannerised hero in Shakespeare’s “Cymbe-
line.” They brought their damper and billies with them,
and all through a miserable chilly day these fanatical
devotees chewed and chatted and discovered what an in-
efficient support a pair of bony or flabby legs are when
called on to prop up an exhausted body from 8 a.in. to 8
p.m. When the play began, a much-chafed, considerably-
warped crowd, shedding hair pins and loose locks, fell
heavily into the front seats, and applauded furiously and
indiscriminately, hoping to shake Off the ‘ slept-in-your-
clothes’ feeling, or their resemblance to stagnant, half-
frozen dirty puddles. And then the critics gave the show
universal ‘slops.
The demise of “What Cheer ’Ria” Bessie Bellwood
(writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent) removes
the last of the witty, smutty, rearly-repartee idols of the
Halls. Impromptu wit and original .freshness have been
succeeded by labored coarse, or vulgar, humor and deadly
respectable cleverness. Bessie was Irishly impulsive, and,
some of her retorts to the jibing gods ” were more
crushing than choice. On one occasion the audience
chose to see an evil meaning in her apology for late ap-
pearance, and roared loud and lustily. The indignant
artiste shouted out, “ You dirty beasts, I didn’t mean
that,’’ which “ broke up ’’ and disorganised the house for
that evening. A recent satirical article on the “Halls”
appeared in the Saturday Review, and referred to “ Mis 3
Elizabeth Bellwood —that remarkable moral force in a
Paisley shawl.”

Rignold found trouble in getting a reliable stage-horse
for Henry V.’s entry into London. A rampageous animal
that he tried for a night or two concluded its engagement
by buck-jumping behind the scenes, and King Henry had
to make his entry for once on foot. Fitzgeralds, hearing
of the difficulty, volunteered the loan of an imposing
grey from their circus.

The Wild-Cat Column.
In reply to 18 people who, inspired by the U.S.
elections, "have written to The Bulletin, at great length,
on the “ fneejum of exchange,” and 176 mote people
whose letters will probably arrive next week, this paper
tries to state, as clearly as possible, how it sits on the
fence upon this subject. If a country has a currency
based on gold only, then it doesn’t matter much wr ether
you coin one sovereign or eight out of an ounce of gold ,
the coin will find its level and the prices of other things
will adjust themselves to it. If there are 20 shillings in
the sovereign, and the sovereign contains an ounce of
gold, then tea will probably be 4d. per lb. ; if there are 20
shillings in the sovereign, but the latter only contains an
eighth of an ounce of gold, then the same tea will be 2s.
Bd. per lb. If there, is a silver currency only, then it
doesn’t matter whether the alleged shilling or dollar or
yen, or whatever the standard coin may be, represents joz.
of silver or lOoz.; the prices of commodities will adjust
themselves to it. If a country has a gold and silver
standard, and they are both legal tender, and there are
20s. in the £, then it is necessary, in order to keep things
straight, that the silver in 20s. should have the same, or
practically the same, market value as the gold contained
in the sovereign, and that the prices should keep at the
same rates or very near it. Therefore, if the U.S. started
coining legal tender silver on the present basis, which is
practically that 30oz. silver equal loz. gold, and the
demand thereby created made silver go up in price, some-
thing would depreciate (gold, The Bulletin thinks, but
the situation is so mixed that it is difficult to be sure of
anything). And if it started coining legal tender silver on
the assumption that 16oz. silver equalled loz. gold (which
it doesn’t), and the demand thus created wasn’t sufficient
to raise-the price of silver so that it would really be
worth one-sixteenth as much as gold in the open market,
then silver coin would begin to depreciate or would have
been depreciated all along. And when there are two
standards, and one of them is depreciated, something
happens—The Bulletin doesn’t exactly remember what.
Apparently the way to establish a double stan-
dard (supposing one country tackles it by itself) is to
think out this problem. The relative prices of gold and
silver are so much. The relative production of gold is so
many tons per annum, and of silver so many tons per
annum. The surplus of each, after providing for the
world’s present requirements (said requirements to be
figured out carefully) is so much. The production of one
metal is likely to increase or diminish so much, and of the
other metal so much also. The demand for each is likely
to increase or diminish at such and such a rate until in so
many years it is so much. The stock on hand of each
metal is so much, of which x represents the amount in
actual use, and y represents the surplus, if there is one.
Allowing, therefore, that the standard to be set up is ex-
pected to be fixed and permanent for, say, a century,
the average surplus over requirements during that
period, after allowing for present supplies, present and
probable future production, and present and probable
future requirements On the present and probable future
basis of things, is likely to be so much. The establish-
ment of a silver standard in this country will increase the
demand to a certain extent, to be carefully estimated.
The increased demand will lead to increase of supply by a
certain, number of tons per annum. The present price
being so-and-so, this change in the relative demand
and supply will make the average future price so-
and-so, and if the new silver coinage is based on
that price it will- be a stable affair which will
neither depreciate nor appreciate, and thereby lead
to trouble. If other countries (not allowed for in the
estimate) also establish a silver standard, and increase the
demand beyond what was expected, and thus raise the
price further than: was reckoned upon, there will probably
be chaos. If some of the present silver countries unex-
pectedly abandon "silver and thus decrease the demand
and lower the price in an unlooked-for fashion, there
will prbbably be frenzy. If the calculation prove unex-
pectedly very far wrong in some other respects, then there
will, most likely be h—l. This is The Bulletin’s idea of
the bi-metallic problem as far as concerns the U.S. or any
other country which tries to settle the question “on its
own,” subject, of course, to correction from the 176 cor-
respondents whose letters re the “meejum of exchange”
haven’t: yet come to hand.
Roughly speaking, mono-metallism is entering
one horse for a race all by himself, and betting that he
will always keep pace with himself—which he generally
does. And bi-metallism is entering two horses for a race
that is. to last a century or two, and betting that they will
exactly keep pace with each other, which is a much larger
contract and requires a lot of careful management.
* * * *
There is a considerable scarcity of information
as to how the N.S.W. local 3 per cent, loan is going off.
When it was first put on the market it was announced
that £150,000 had been subscribed within three days, and
since then there has been comparative silence. It has
since been alleged on more or less unreliable authority that
this £150,000 was not subscribed by the public, but con-
sisted of certain trust funds under Government control,
and that though the public is taking up the new stock it
is doing so very gradually. The fact can’t be got over
that the 3 per cent, issue at par is a very shabby way of
encouraging the local investor. The 3 per cent, loan re-
cently floated in London was sold at £95 2s. net, and costs
the State £3 4s. per cent, interest, while the amount
offered to the miserable Cornstalk capitalist is £3, less in-
come-tax from which the British investor is exempt.
Allowing for the income-tax and the saving in exchange
through not having to remit interest to England, the
local investor should have been offered 3} per cent, to
Sut him on a level with the favored Bull. The Reid
[inistry, however, isn’t alone in the opinion that the
British speculator, should be offered better terms than the
local one.. When the Maoriland Government wanted
£2,000,000 at 4 per cent, to prop up the B.N.Z. it sold the
loan in London and didn't give the M.L. investor a look-
in. Then it-gave him a chance to put his money into
Maoriland Consols at'3£ per cent, and felt good over it.
**;; * • *
Tasmania also proposes to float a 3 percent, loan
locally at par, the amount asked for being l 250,000.
Considering that last year Tasmania floated a loan in
London at a price which cost the province £3 12s. Bd. per
cent., this demand that the Tas. investor should be con-
tent with £3 per cent, net is an attempt to shear the local
capitalist pretty close. Apparently Treas Fysh, like
Treas. Reid, thinks that the local man should only get a
chance to put his money into Govt, securities if he will
accept a great deal less interest than is paid to J. Bull.
If he won’t put up the money at 3 per cent, presumably
Bull will get-the loan at 3£, and it will be announced that
the local market is a failure !

The London Bank of Australia has decided,
like a lot of other banks, to attempt some further
arrangement of its affairs, and intends applying to its
depositors for a reduction of interest. The London,
which has hitherto been doing reasonably well, seems to
have struck a sudden patch of bad luck. Last half-year
the profit-balance carried forward was £11,167 ; it is now'
cabled as £10,935, so it w'ould seem that the six months
operations have resulted in a loss of £232. The profits
for the previous three half-years were (December,’9s),
£5673; (June, ’95), £7082; (December, ’94), £8216; so
they have been pers stently fading for some time past,
and the alleged slump is hardly surprising. Also, the
institution seems to have paid its usual 5% per cent,
dividend on preference shares (£4728) out of the loss, and
thereby reduced its accumulated profit balance to £6207,
which is a decline of £4960 in six months.
The London Bank has reduced its reconstructed
deposits in two years from £4,800,000 to £3,800,000, but
this relief doesn’t seem to have done it any permanent
good. It has £1,500,000 cash on hand, which is more
than the w'hole amount of its new deposits and current
accounts, and it has no reconstructed deposits falling due
till 1900. Therefore, it looks as if it could easily pay off
half-a-million more of its 4J per cent, deposits, and thus
save £22,500 a year in interest. If things become no
worse, and if the institution gets a reduction of interest
to 3 per cent, (which is about the ordinary figure just
now), it ought to show a profit of £55,000 a year, which
would enable it to pay a 3 per cent, dividend on its ordi-
nary shares as well as the usual 5(- per cent, on
preference stocks. But the London has already made very
large savings by paying off part of its 4£ percent, deposits,
and the saving has all been absorbed by the steady falling
off in profits, and things have grown worse instead of
better, so the probabilities are that the new'saving, if it
is effected, will be gradually absorbed by the steady reduc-
tion in banking profits, and the ordinary shareholders will
be as far off their dividend as ever. The falling-off in
banking profits seems to have absolutely no end. The
North Queensland, Commercial of Sydney, National, Vic-
toria, and Colonial all made large savings, four of them
by paying off high-priced deposits, and the fifth by re-
ducing interest, and the depression absorbed the savings
as fast as they were made and left things very much where
they were. The absorbing capacity of the depression
seems simply incalculable.

According to the report just presented to Maori-
land Parliament, the Bank of N.Z. Estates Co., when it
raised its famous loan of £1,500,000 in London, paid
£305,000 in discounts and expenses for the accommoda-
tion. Also, it paid 5) per cent, interest, so during the
four years that the loan existed it cost the co. 12 per
cent, on the money actually realised. What a sample of
private banking management!

Hudson Bros. (Limited), a huge N.S.W. en-
gineering enterprise representing £360,000 of capital, has
decided to w'ind up. Most of the capital has already been
written off as lost, and the shares have been valueless for
some time. In his budget speech of July 9, Premier Reid
pointed enthusiastically to this co. as an example of the
prosperity which came of Foreign-raid, and stated that it
was just beginning to boom for the first time in several
years. The present collapse suggests that Jollyandfat
had blind staggers in his facts, as usual.
• * * *
Treasurer Reid (N.S.W.) was so far moved by
the jeers of the Opposition at his “permanent and re-
productive” loan items that the additions to the Art
Gallery and the foundations of Gov. Phillip’s statue are
not to be made permanent burdens but are to go into the
section of the loan expenditure which is supposed to be
repaid by instalments spread over 30 years. This new way
of fooling the country is just as objectionable as the old
one. The instalment can only be paid if there is a sur-
plus. There has been a deficit almost every year for many
years (even the year of Reid’s £330,000 alleged surplus is
shown in Coghlan’s latest “ Seven Colonies” to have had
£600,000 shortage); there is no prospect of the required
surplus unless more revenue is raised, and the revenue is
steadily diminishing instead of increasing. Therefore, the
pretence of paying for old hangman Phillip’s st itue in 30
years when there is no provision made for finding the
money is a mere formality.
* • * •
The Vic. Upper House has struck a snag. It
referred the Companies’ Bill to a committee whose business
it was to take evidence from reliable capitalists, and
supply reasons for rejecting the measure. But the
carefully-selected capitalists didn’t object nearly so much
as was expected, and their case was miserably weak all
through, and the committee, instead of presenting the re-
port anticipated, only Suggested a few comparatively
small amendments. Now, the Council has either got to
accept most of the measure, or reject it despite the fact
that its committee, after patient inquiry and much evi-
dence, gave no reasons for so doing, and 48 very grumpy
old men are considering the situation.
* * >■< * *
Judged by statistics, Victorian industry touched
bottom in ’93 or ’94, and since then a distinct revival has
set in. Butter and cheese factories employed 360 hands
in ’93 and 820 hands in ’95. Woollen mills employed 552
hands in ’93 and 690 in ’95. Flour mills, 685 hands in ’93
and 705 in ’95. Brew'eries, 960 hands in ’94 and 1007 in
’95. Distilleries, 127 hands in ’93 and 155 in ’95. Tobacco
faci ories, 588 hands in ’93 and 772 in ’95. Soap and candle
works, 375 hands in ’93 against 438 in ’95. The number of
hands employed in tanneries rose, in the two years, from
1492 to 1639. In brickyards, the figures were 897 in ’94
and 964 in ’95. Taking ifactori.es, works and quarries
all together, the employes in ’95 were 1458 more than in
’94 —the first real improvement since 1890.

In Edwards v. Melb. Metropolitan Board of
Works, plaintiff’s costs were-taxed at £1762, so that this
action, which could at the outset have been settled for
£lOO, cannot have cost the M. B. W. less than £5OOO. As the
Board’s servants lit a fire which burnt plaintiff’s crop and
fences, any honest man would have admitted the liability
and settled with good grace.

Total sugar manufactured in Q’sland for the
year ending 30th June, ’95, was 86,255 tons. This,shows a
decrease on the preceding year of 5457 tons, in spite* of
the greater area under cane. Decrease due principally to
grubs in the north and frost in the south. The yield for
the year was only 29Jcwt. per acre. Domestic consump-
tion required 24,191 tons ; export, 62,064 tons —all to other
Australasian provinces.

Mines Minister Smith has been called upon to
defend the little toy plant at the Clyde, called “ the
N.S.W. Govt. Metallurgical Works,” and his defence is as
weak as the works. He w'as informed that another £2OO
would enable the plant to treat 100 tons a week, and he
had authorised the erection of chlorination and cyanide
plants. If it takes a year to put lip the toy works, how
long will be occupied in erecting the additions now
authorised? AVanted in N.S.AV. a Mines Minister who
knows something of the business. Hardly possible while
selection is confined to M’sP. Why not have a well-
paid, expert Mines Commissioner to conduct the whole
business free from political interference?

increase their crushing capacity “ by the erection of Frue
vanners,” and then wondered why some of the more
knowing members laughed. ’Tis said that the works have
been started on ore containing a large percentage of anti-
mony. These toy works will produce more curses
than metal, precious or otherwise. They will add to the
humiliation of the N.S.W. Mines Department, which is
sufficiently disgraced already.
• * * *
N.S.W. has got a cinnabar mine in the North,
and the mine has got a well-satisfied and confident man-
ager : “I think the ore we are now getting is better than
the prospector’s. It shows antimony, but the antimony
will disappear as depth is reached.” If it does, the
manager has made a correct guess, and the betting is
only even anyway.
*' * * •
The half-yearly report of the Broken Hill Junc-
tion Co. is no better than shareholders and the public had
reasonable grounds to expect. By a skilful arrangement
of dates the six months embraced but five dividends ; and
the accounts show that in order to pay even these, totalling
£25,000, the previously-earned and undistributed profits
had to be encroached upon to the extent of a few pence
short of £3OOO. The answer to this probably will be that,
as the directors put it, the cost of railway and sidings to
connect with the Silverton line has been borne, while
“large sums have been disbursed for machinery and
general mine equipment and development.” The "heavi-
ness of the flow of water encountered when the lode was
struck in the deep level also interfered with operations
and entailed expense. But, as against all this, it has to
be put that of the 36,000 tons of ore of all grades dealt
with during the term about 18,000 tons were high-grade
stuff—from 26 to 290 z. and from 35 j to 36 per cent, lead
per ton. And it is quite certain that half the ore in the
mine is not of that grade, so that this good-quality stuff
was disproportionately extracted. The directors don't
say that in those words, but they do say it in these: —
“ There are enormous bodies of second-grade ore assaying
about 20oz. silver and 28 per cent, lead that cannot be so
readily disposed of” (they had said they had been rushed
for the high-grade ore), “ although negotiations are pend-
ing for the sale of large quantities ; and unless satisfactory
arrangements are made at an early date the directors pro-
pose to increase the ore-reduction plant to the capacity
of, say, 2000 tons of crude ore per week, for the product
of which there is a large demand.” With the most recent
additions the plant has a capacity of 1000 tons weekly.
Altogether 36,000 tons of ore have been used up
and the profit is £22,000. That is a fraction under 12s. 3d.
per ton. But, as before said, shareholders cannot place
much reliance on these figures as a guide to the value (to
them) of the average ore in the mine. It may be more
than that or it may be less. On the face of it, seeing that
a disproportionately large quantity of high-grade ore was
sold, the value should be much lower. That, however, is
not certain, because by' concentrating instead of selling
outright the lower-grade ore a higher profit per ton may
be made out of it. Yet on past experience this milling
(lower grade) ore will not return a profit of more than
about 9s. per ton at the very outside. At that rate a plant
for treating 2000 tons per week would give enough profit
to pay 3i divs. in the six months. Then there is the
higher-grade ore to be sold outright. With silver and
lead at their present prices the shilling div. ought, there-
fore, to be reasonably possible. But with even a small
decline in the price of either metal the div. will only be
regularly maintained at the expense of a further dispro-
portionate extraction of the richer stuff—by repeating,
that is, in connection with the sulphides what was done
in connection with the carbonate and other rich smelting
ores —a policy' which came near ruining even the Barrier
silver-mining industry.
The Junction directors intimate that a London
office has been opened “ for the registration and transfer
of shares and the payment of dividends,” and a London
board of six has been appointed. Most, if not all, of these
six are said to be not unknown to the Camden (John
Howell's Illawarra works) syndicate.
An interesting calculation has been made to
show the proportion of the value of the contained metals
in the sulphides which reached the Junction Co. “There
were in the concentrates and crude ore despatched from
the mine approximately 721,0000 z. silver and 9960 tonslead,
of the aggregate value of £189,710. For this the co. got
just over £60,000. Out of £60,000 it had to pay £12,000
for freights, &c., £4600 for mine timber, £2047 for fuel,
and over £16,000 for wages.” Leaving £22,000 for the
shareholders. But this proportion is higher than usual
with sulphide-mining cos. on account of the unusually-high
value of the sulphides.
Block 14, for example, sent away during its last
half-year, also just ended, the following quantities of stuff :
Carbonate ore to smelting works, 6549 tons; carbonate con-
centrates to ditto, 108 tons; sulphide concentrates to ditto,
7835 tons ; sulphide concentrates for shipment, 7629 tons ;
slimes to smelting works, 4620 tons ; zinc concentrates for
shipment, 982 tons—a total of 27,623 tons. But this re-
presents about 72,000 tons of ore, because 64,607 tons of
ore was required to produce the concentrates and slimes
turned out during the half-year. Also, time
£5OOO onl.y was paid in divs., although a further £7752
was spent on plant and machinery, and the liquid assets
were increased by £5223. The co. doesn’t give much in-
formation about its ores : but it bought nearly 10,000 tons
of sulphides for the Junction which assayed 20oz.
silver and 28 per cent. lead. That is slightly beyond Block
14’s own average, and also the Central’s. The average
of the ore treated would be about 18oz. silver and
25 per cent. lead. The approximate value of the contained
silver and lead, as shown by assay, would be £340,000 ;
out of which the co. gets fairly, say, £ls,ooo—because the
whole of the additions to the plant, &c., can hardly be
reckoned in. The balance of 1.325,000 (not to speak of the
zinc) goes for wages, timber, fuel, freight, stores—and
over the dump. Much of it over the dump. And this is
the best that such skilled, persistent men as Mr. T. J.
Greenway, the general manager, can do with the great
sulphide problem up to date. Giving Block 14 the
whole £15,000 as profit, the ore treated during this last
half-year yielded just 4s. 2d. per ton profit. Taking only
the amount distributed and added to liquid assets, the
profit comes down to 2s. Bd. per ton.
Says general manager Green way, of Block 14 :
“ We have continued our experimental operations bearing
upon the production of marketable zinc concentrates, and
much valuable experience has been gained. During the
half-year we have produced -nearly 1000 tons of this pro-
duct with satisfactory results'. I am now preparing plans
for a small zinc plant, which I hope to submit for your
consideration in a short time.”

“ The Mt. Drysdalemine has sent 14 tons of ore
to Oobar for treatment.” Presumably to the Oobar cop-
per mine. Two years ago, when Drysdale shares went to
£BOO each, they were sending ore to Wallaroo, S.A., and
elsewhere, at a cost of about £lO a ton. They spent over
£lO,OOO in this way, while the ore could have been thrown
into the Cobar water-jackets and treated at a cost of at
most fl a ton. That is what they propose to do now the
mine has next to no ore to treat.
* * * *
The writer has been privileged to see a copy of
the “private and confidential” prospectus of the “ Bar-
rass and Conley Freehold G.M. Syndicate,” Cobar,
N.S.W. Capital, £64,000, divided into 100 shares of £640
each, payable £l6O on application, balance as arranged.
Object, to buy one-half interest from the present owners,
and, in conjunction with them, to form a no-liability co. ;
capital, £200,000, in 400,000 shares of 10s. each. The syn-
dicate’s £64,000 to be applied—£4o,ooo to owners and
£20,000 working capital, and £4OOO to cover commission
and other charges, securing the property and floating
the syndicate. If the public take up the shares when the
£200,000 co. is formed, the holder of each syndicate share
will get 800 bonus shares. In other words, l-5th of the
whole property will be given for underwriting the amount
to be paid the owners, providing the working capital and
the £4000 commission, charges, &c. If the public don’t
come in, the syndicate will be “ stuck ” with it, though
the prospectus doesn’t say so. Underwriting mines is a
new business here, and to underwrite by prospectus is
quite a novelty anywhere. The only information re the
mine is a statement that 1201 tons have been crushed for
3552oz. Very good, but not so much as the public have been
led by the press to believe had been got out of the mine.
Everything depends on how much of this sort of stuff is
left in it. Before forming either a syndicate ora co. of the
size proposed some attempt should be made to expose ton-
nage. It is fair to remember that, allowing only a fair
amount for cost of raising and treatment," &c., it will take
as much gold as the owners have got out altogether to
pay one little 6d. div. on 400,000 shares, and they have
worked the mine for over a year.

repute : —“ Dear Bulletin, do all you can to stop this
mad rush of men to this goldfield, especially at this time
of the year. What with the ‘ slump ’ in England and
the terrible heat and drought, I cannot but think an
exodus must take place from here immediately.” At-
tached is a newspaper clipping :—“ Patients are beginning
to arrive in increased numbers at the Govt. Hospital
(Coolgardie) daily, and yesterday no fewer than seven
were admitted, three suffering from typhoid fever, two
from dysentery, and the others from different disorders.
Now tliat the hot weather has set in fever is steadily on
the increase, and by next month the fever season may
fairly be said to have s»t in.”

The first report from the new boss of the General
Gordon mine in W.A., will not set the share-market on
fire. “ At this level (70ft.) lode was cut through from
footwall to hanging wall, showing formation 41ft. in
width. Drive on course of lode 46ft., formation showing
gold in several places. A drive also driven from this
level south, the end shows fairly good prospects by dolly-
ing, gold being extremely fine.” Not much change out of
that !
* * * •
Another falling-off in the Westralian gold-return
for Oct., compared with the previous month —27,331oz.
against 35,301oz. It is some 400oz. lower than Oct. last
year. But there is always an excuse. This time some
OOOOoz. left after the month, but was produced in it.
Well, this month will get the benefit of it, and when it
does the writer will bet that not a word is said about it.
Over 12,000oz. came from East Coolgardie (Hannan’s), and
nearly all of this from three mines which are producing
about one-third of the W.A. gold. Murchison, like most
Westralian mines, “wobbles” horribly. Last month it
returned 15,000oz., this only 3018oz. Dundas, too, after
working itself up to a monthly return of some hundreds
of oz., has gone back this month to 7oz. ! Instead of
undergoing the strain of inventing excuses, why doesn’t
some W.A. newspaper-man work out a return showing
how much these monthly returns represent for each
working mine in the country ? It would be interesting as
showing that, in a country where mining is dearer than in
any other part of the world, the owners are satisfied with
the least return.

to look after the Armadale and other mines in W.A., and
whose name is often in the Coolgardie papers.

* * * *
The fact that many thousands of gallons of
water are daily drawn from surrounding mines for
the Boulder, Ivanhoe, and Lake View batteries only
shows the trouble and enormous expense these mines are
put to to keep going. First it is one and then another
that furnishes water. Never a word of the cost of taking
up pipes and pumps and relaying them. Only rich mines
can stand it. Ordinary mines have no show in the West.

Gippsland (Vie.) for the first nine months of ’92
yielded 25,778oz. gold ; similar period, ’93, 38,078oz. ;
ditto, 1894, 51,109oz.; ditto, 1895, 63,425oz. ; first nine
months of 1896, 80,159oz. Which is another reason why
it is good policy for Vic. Government tq spend money on
opening up this hitherto neglected and despised end of
the province. Gippsland is the coal region and apparently
the most promising new gold region in Victoria, and it
has many other possibilities if they only had a chance to
develope themselves.

Charters Towers, last month—22,239 tons,

Queensland Govt, lately despatched an
emissary to America to study the tick question.
Boiled down, the report of the delegate amounts
to this: Dipping (on sf large scale) is not a success ;
inoculation is also mainly a failure ; and Queens-
land is much ahead of America in tick investiga-
tion. A certain proportion (from 2 to 5 per cent.)
of cattle die from the scourge, and the rest
become immune or tick-proof. All of which con-
clusions had been anticipated by such men as
plain, practical millionaire Tyson.
The Christmas Number of The Bulletin
will be published on December 12. It will be better
illustrated than any other paper ever published
in Australasia, and will contain a vast amount of
original matter of universal interest by Aus-
tralian writers old and new. Also, it will not
contain a single second-hand, stolen or cheap-
German block. In fact, it will not contain one
line, either of reading or illustration, not specially
done for the paper. Single copies, post free, to
any Australasian address, 7d. : to any foreign
address, 8d.

While Premier Reid is on the warpath
against the sweaters he might make an effectual
onslaught on the alleged ladies who offer the un-
fortunate girls, confined at the obstetric branch of
the_ Sydney Benevolent Asylum, starvation wages
which the girls are forced to accept or leave the
Institution perhaps penniless. Once a girl enters
service under these conditions her name and the
history of her “fall” are known all-round the
gang self-styled Select Society.
* * • *
Hermes : ‘ ‘ The boarding-out system of
the Orphan-schools is not a thing of unmixed
beauty. In Hyde Park (Sydney) I saw a poor
child of 14 years in charge of three rough young-
sters, two of whom were howling hideously and
absolutely refused to walk, and the child had to
carry the little wretches in turns to the tram-shed
while they kicked and punched her on the legs
and face. She stated, in answer to enquiries, that
she was an orphan ; that her mistress was paid by
Govt, for keeping her ; that she had to look after
the children and help in the house ; and that if
she gave her name and address her mistress would
beat her dreadfully.”

and the only suggestion which the staff could make
was that one patient was dying and his bed would
certainly be vacant very shortly, and if the new

A Melbourne lady, who is applying for a
divorce, states a most unusual ease. She says that
she and her .husband lived together happily for
some years, till he informed her that he loved an-
other woman, and could no longer profess affection
for her. He made the statement in a perfectly
courteous manner; there was no abuse, or lan-
*-*r breakage of dishes, or hysterics, only
thereafter they occupied separate rooms, and,
when they met on the stairs or elsewhere, they
treated each other with distant politeness. Later
on, she took a boarding-house, and he came to live
there, and was a model boarder. He always paid
up to date, never grumbled at the hash, and was
courteous, though distant. Also, the other lady
that he loved was never seen or heard of, and the
wife doesn’t allege any misoonduot. Her case is
that she has been deserted, inasmuch as the old
man left her and fled into the next room, and she
claims that his action in becoming a strange
boarder in the house, and dreaming of some person
or persons unknown, is equivalent to abandonment.
The Bulletin is inclined to agree with the lady.
Desertion into the next room, if persisted in for
six years, seems about the same as desertion into
the next continent. In fact, it seems to combine
all the disadvantages of ordinary desertion with-
out any of its advantages ; the all-seeing eye of
the old man is still there, yet he has ceased to be
the old man in the ordinary acceptation of the
term. Whether the other lady is a reality or only
a vision, or a portrait on a cigarette packet, or a
photo of Nellie Stewart, doesn’t appear ; the old
man says nothing, and merely wears a far-off ex-
pression in his eye and pays his board regularly.

In the early days
of Bathurst, N.S.W.,
writes a Bulletin
correspondent, poor
old (long since
retired in favor of
the next “oldest in-
habitant ”) used to
manage the collec-
tion at the local R.C.
services. Seated just
inside the entrance
door, the plate on his
knees, ’s short
prayer would accom-
pany the dropping
into it of each coin
as the faithful passed
in or out. These
prayers were of three
distinct sizes, and,
presumably, quali-
ties, adapted to the
respective values of
the* donations. To
the chink of the mod-
est “ sprat ” would
be heard the shaky
treble of : ‘ ‘ Bless
yer offerin’.” “ God
bless yer offerin’,” in
a slightly higher key,
would greet the de-
scent of a shilling,
while to the mad
rattle of a half-crown
a still higher and
more fervent ‘ ‘ May
God ’lmighty bless
yer offerin’ ! ” went
soaring aloft. Whe-
ther the increase in
the strength or size
of the prayer was oc-.
casioned by joy at the
capture of the larger amount, or whether
had in his mind s eye the parable concerning a
camel and the eye of a needle, I know not, but
certain it is that on all occasions the old man
rigidly adhered to his “scale.”

Sydney Sunday Times is a stupendous
failure as a mining tipster. But on the subject of
people who don’t wash themselves it is absolutely
solid. An extract
“ Now that we are commencing to perspire it is time to
ask, Do you take your morning bath ? ’ You may shru"
your shoulders and say that I am nobody to ask such a
question, but the truth is that many fashionable ladies—
those who clothe themselves in purple and fine linen—
have not the most elementary notion of cleanliness.
Their bodies are strangers to soap and water. In this
respect men are in advance of women. So long as the
face, neck, hands, and any other exposed portion o f ~c
body is clean, most of us are satisfied. It is for the same
reason that only a comparatively small trade is done in
underclothing. Now, dirt anywhere on the body is a
source of discomfort, and its presence demoralises. It is
impossible to believe that a high moral tone is compatible
with an unclean skm. A scrupulously clean person may
not possess good morals, but it is almost next to impossible
for a dirty person to be moral and pure. It seems in-
A LIKELY SURMISE.
S HE: “ You wretch! I believe your wife is too good for you.”
HE: “She is—she is a Christian Endeavorer.”
credible that in Sydney, with the dust and dirt of the
atmosphere settling on the skin and mingling with its
fatty secretion, any woman can live without bathing,
and that one can live under the same roof-tree
with a woman who is prejudiced against soap and water.
Nowadays even the richest and most idle women spend
less time in cleansing the skin than they spend in decor-
ating their bodies with finery, and they endeavor to buy
at so much a box that good complexion which Nature
would give them in return for the attention required to
keep the body healthy.”
“Most of us ” are satisfied! Evidently it is a
woman who writes, so she ought to know. Also,
the same lady, no end of a moralist, says :
“ I often think what a pity it is the French custom of
kissing the hand is not made the custom here ; it is sim-
ple, innocent, and even chivalrous. A kiss on the lips
should not last longer than a second.”
♦ * » *
Officialdom in New Guinea is beginning to
bestir itself. It is now proposed to introduce a
form of civic marriage among the Papuans.
Wherein native marriages were a failure is not
clearly specified, but in future any parties so wish-
ing may be married formally in the presence of a
Govt, official. Also, any parties already married
may ratify their union in the same manner. Papua
is evidently on the Right Path. Already she has
learnt how to hang, and now marriage, divorce,
prostitution and disease are within reasonable dis-
tance. The farce of civilising a people who, in
the essentials of comfort and morality, were pro-
bably ahead of ourselves is simply unspeakable.

The famous Koo-wee-rup (Yic.) village
settlement has evidently got over its first diffi-
culties and is developing into a settledj.community.
At all events, the first Koo-wee-rup Settler to be
judicially flogged is to get his spell at the triangles
a week or two hence, and that seems to lend an air
of permanence to the place. When a new town-
ship has had its first flogging it is evidently get-
ting into line with the institutions of this great
country, so to speak, and has emerged into the*
light of civilisation. The cat and the gallows are
civilisation.
According to Meston’s report, the native
women in the Q. Peninsula are the prey of whites,
kanakas, and Chinamen, and everywhere a source
of discord. The old men of the coast tribes sell
their women to whites for flour and tobacco.
Meston goes so far as to say that if the girls at
Mapoon Mission were not guarded at night they
would be disposed of in the same manner. He
rebuts the charges of treachery made against the
blacks, and asserts that along the telegraph' line
natives have never interfered with a single official,
though they could easily have speared every man
in the service. The only remedy for existing
troubles lies in reservations and strict isolation.
♦ * * *
An extraordinary decline of litigation in
Victoria is due in a great measure to the shocking
examples of Speight v. Syme, widow Kilpatrick
v. Huddart, Parker, and other fruitless suits.
Also the hollowness of Crown prosecutions in the
Mercantile and other cases has helped to make the
name Law stink in the nostrils of Common Sense.

Dunedin Savage Club
prides itself on going the
whole hog for savagery,
and, to show how savage
it is, the chairman wears
turkey - feathers in his
hair, and commands si-
lence with a thighbone.
Apparently Dunedinites
are too unversed in liter-
ary history to know that
the original (London)
Savage Club was so
styled, not after the
noble red or ignoble
black man, but after that
clever Bohemian. Rich-
ard Savage.

THE ADIEU.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The music falling from her gentle touch
Dropped deep into the soul :
All sadness of the earth lay bare to such
As listened for the dole.
Every farewell that ever has been breathed
By twin souls torn apart
Seemed thrilling through the air, and each was
wreathed
With a twin broken heart.
Your grey eyes—ah ! for whom, sweet girl !—were
dimmed,
Just for a space with tears :
While landscape of a might-have-been lay limned
On canvas of my years.
J. JINGLE.

* * * *
Negotiations for a settlement of Adelaide
Hospital troubles lasted for four weeks without
result. Four of the ablest of the striking doctors
were willing to go back, but the Medical Uiiion
would not allow them to do so. Dr. Napier
agreed to give up all the wards but one in whic
he is a specialist—midwifery—but the Union
stipulated for a resignation of the lot. papier, by
the way, has incurred the enmity of Adelaide
doctors by working up a big private consulting
practice, and this constitutes portion of the milk
in the eoeoanut. The Ministry having failed to
settle the trouble, two more doctors are at once to
be imported if necessary.
The Tory members of S.A. Leg. Council-the
democrats withdrew from the ballot lately ap-
pointed a committee to enquire into Hospital
affairs. Of the five appointed two are doctors—
Campbell and Magarey—in close sympathy with
the local Medical Union, which passed resolutions
antagonistic to Napier and Smith, the imported
doctors, before their arrival, and has since consis-
tently boycotted them. As repeatedly stated
here, an impartial tribunal cannot be found m
Adelaide, or even, if including inedicoes, in Aus-
tralia.

An Adelaide correspondent to The Bul-
letin :
You frequently refer to the poor salaries paid Sydney
and Melb. shorthand writers and typists. Do the Eastern
stenographers grumble at 305., 255., or 20s. a week ? Just
a few instances to show them where they are allowing
themselves to drift. Here, in holy Adelaide, I know of a
lady typist in a leading patent-agent’s office who gets 15s.
a week and thinks herself well off. She writes 130 words
a minute— writes it, mind you—does all stenographic
work in connection with specifications, and types them as
well ! Another is employed in one of our “ best” solici-
tors’ offices and gets 12s. 6d. a week. Bear in mind that
these are first-class shorthand-writers and typists. Sala-

* * * *
Two damsels of the lost legion fell out
during Cup week. The trouble ended in the
police-court, where one of the lydies accused the
other of carrying salt over her left breast to enable
her to commit perjury without risk to her con-
science. Whereupon accused unbuttoned _ her
dress with great velocity and gave the horrified
Bench ocular demonstration of the falsity of the
allegation. Reward—24 hours for “contempt.”
• * * *
At a Sydney suburban police-court, one day
last week, a certain theft-case was called. “ Wm.
Briggs,” said the sergeant, “you are charged with
stealing a set of harness from the premises of one
Michael Simpson some two months ago.” “ Not
guilty, your Worship ! ” excitedly interrupted the
prisoner. “It was three months ago ! ”
Years ago, on a Bland
river station, a Chinaman
was bitten on the hand by
a snake. The usual bush
method of walking the suf-
ferer up and down was re-
sorted to while a man rode
to the nearest township—
six miles— for medicine. He
got the medicine (a bottle
of three-star), and returned
hastily to the station. The
manager, now a well-known
man, was superintending
operations on the Chow. He
took the bottle, drew the
cork, sniffed. “Oh, damn
it all,” he said, “ I’m not
going to give good stuff
like that to a blanky Chow.”
And he didn’t.
* * *
The larrikins have
found a means of extracting
fun from the wooden boxes,
containinggasineters, placed
at the foot of the street
lamp-posts in several Melb.
suburbs. Thus : A nail is
inserted in the meter, and,
as soon as the smell of gas
has become sufficiently pro-
nounced, the larrikin, whilst
sheltering himself behind
the lamp - post, inserts a
match, with the aid of a
stick, in the box, fragments
of which are scattered about
the street by the resultant
explosion. Who says that
the masses take no interest
in contemporary science ?

There are some polite
squatters out back. A Dar-
ling-shed shearer was rough-
ing his sheep. Next morning
he found a note over his
pen : “ Sheep rough; please
improve.” He sheared
cleaner next day and the following morning found
this note : “Improved. Thanks.” Fact.

A Texas (Q.) Bulletin correspondent:—
‘ A weird procession passed along this road a few
days ago. First a man, apparently much over 70,
his knees knocking together with weakness, lead-
ing an almost equally shaky horse bearing a few
rags of swap, &c. To the horse’s tail was attached
a piece of greenhide, at the end of which was a
perfectly blind old man. The first man was a
boko. They were making for the railway to try
and get to Sydney. ”
A Sydney suburban clergyman was visited
the other day by a scared man who wanted to be
married. The clergyman was willing, but his
client was ill at ease, and doubted the parson’s
ability to assist him. “ No, I don’t think you can
do it,” he said. “Why? What’s the matter?”
asked the parson, “Is the young woman agree-
able ? ” “ Oh, yes, she is anxious that you should
fix us up ; but I’m afraid you can’t do it for us.”
“Are you a married man?” “Yes, I am, and
that’s the trouble.” “ And is your wife living ? ”
“ Yes, of course.” “Then you can’t marry any-
one else.” “ Well, I don’t want to marry anyone
else.” “Why, then, did you come to me at all? ”
“The wife asked me.” “This is a most extra-
ordinary case.” “Well, you see, it’s like this : I
married her last week at a registrar’s, and
Lieutenant KLATING, N.A.V., the veteran
N.S.W. rifleman.
Kerry, photo.
she hasn’t had a wink of sleep since, thinking
the wedding was not all right and proper; she
wants to make sure and ease her mind, and we
thought as you could marry us over again.” “Oh !
if that’s all you want bring the wife here and I
will marry you again.” “And how much will it
cost ? I paid £1 already, and I’m out of work.
Will you fix us up for ss. ? ” “Well, if you can’t
afford to pay I’ll not make any charge.” And the
scared one hurried off and returned with his wife.
The parson met him a few days later and inquired
how she was. “Oh ! she’s quite peaceful now and
sleeps like a top. She’s too sleepy in the morning
to wake up and get my breakfast.”

A “ drain ” throat
Is a sore throat, a “more” throat, a “four” throat,
A sere throat, a queer throat, a beer throat,
Long or short throat, pint or quart throat,
A “ smile ” throat, a “ bile ” throat, an “ ile ” throat;
Why not then call
This newer throat, non-brewer throat —
The “ sewer throat ” ?
THE GREAT “DRINK QUESTION.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In these hard times—it seems to me—
When most of us are “ up a tree,”
And fortune flies those who pursue it,
The Question, which we all debate,
And one we’ve often heard of late
From many a hard-up, drouthy mate,
Is: “Who is going to do it?”
W.C.T.U.

Why should the values of gold and silver be “governed
by the relative rate of production ” ? If 20oz. of silver be
produced for loz. gold, it doesn’t follow that 20oz. silver
should be worth loz. gold. If the world was in the habit
of using 30oz. silver for loz. gold, silver might be the
dearer of the two; if it used loz. silver for loz. gold,
then silver might sell at ss. per ton. If Australia
produces snakes and sheep in equal numbers, it
doesn’t make them the same value unless the
country wants snakes as badly as it does sheep

Ex-Judge M‘Farland (admitted in 1847) is
the senior N.S.W. barrister. Justice Stephen
(1850) next, and Chief Justice Darley (1853) third.
John Wallis, reputed to be 98 years of
age, died suddenly at Penrith (N.S.W.) last week.
He fought at Waterloo —at least, like Geo. TV.,
he said so.
Chief Justice Darley ascended N.S.W.
Bench Nov., 1886. He had as colleagues Judges
Fawcett, Manning, Windeyer, and Innes ; three
of these are dead and the fourth on pension. Since
the Chief took his seat Stephen, Owen, Foster,
Deffell, C. J. Manning, Simpson, and Cohen have
been appointed. Of these, Foster is on pension,
and Deffell dead. Judge Darley, therefore, in
ten years has become the senior of the B nch.
Much quiet canvassing is being done by aspirants
for the Judgeship vacated by the death of Mr. Jus-
tice Innes. It is said that the Chief Justice has
reasons for desiring Attorney-General Want to
appoint himself to the vacancy, and that there are
many things less likely than “ Mr. Justice Want.”
The gentleman’s judicial attitude in the Crick case
guarantees his capacity.
Lieutenant M. J. Keating is the veteran
Australian rifleman ; he occupies the same relation
to Aus. rifle-shooting that W. G. Grace does to
English cricket. He began his consistent career
in 78 by winning an all-comers’ match in Sydney
at 900 and 1000yds. Since then, never a year has
passed without distinction for him in rifle matches.
He is the one man in Australia who has won the
Queen’s competition four times. This year he
won it by securing an aggregate of 370 against R.
Jeffery’s 368. He has made rifle-shooting a study
and a science; for instance, in ’95, in the Sydney
Rifle Assoc, members’ match, he began shooting
with a wind-gauge six feet to the left, and scored
34 out of a possible 35 at 500yds. Then the wind
changed round to a southerly “buster,” but
the veteran, altering his wind-gauge to 20ft.
to the right, put up another 34 out of 35
possible. He is also a dandy pistol-shot—just as
good as Hamilton, of Q., in fact; Keating’s best
is 35 out of 36 at 30yds. on a two-inch bull, Hamil-
ton’s 41 out of 42. Keating shoots very quickly ;
at Hobart in ’88 he scored 73 out of 75 with such
rapidity that his rifle-barrel was too hot to touch.
A friend, seeing him shoot in this way, as quick as
winking, ran up to remonstrate, but the marksman
replied only by rattling in 12 bull’s-eyes. Lieut.
Keating went to the Soudan with the Contingent,
and, on arriving in England, he was subjected to
an indignity which he deserved for being con-
cerned in such a business—the. Duke of Cambridge
laid his hand upon Keating’s shoulder, and mut-
tered something in a Dutch voice. Keating is aged
40 but feels 24.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “ Babeskin ” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. Thos. S. Loney, 208 William-st., Sydney.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ _ v Sydney, Nov. 10, 1896.
Mr Dear Myee—
The new ladies’ club has taken unto itself pre*
raises in Phillip-street, and (of course) has called
itself the Victoria. Who was it that originally
said that all women were King-worshippers, Queen-
worshippers, and lord-worshippers? The club is
in form like unto a dwelling-house, most cosily
and prettily furnished, and is divided into three
parts—the residential club, the Salon, and the
restaurant. Residential members belong to all,
yet the Salonites will by no means be permitted
the privileges of the higher-priced clubites, and
the restaurant is for the use of all, as per terms.
And yet there are not three clubs, but one club]
The Athanasian difficulties of the rules yield to
study, and point to the future arising of cliques,
which will always be when women are gathered
together. On the ground-floor is a pretty tea-
room-the feminine of bar; upstairs there are
sitting-rooms and bedrooms. Culture will be im-
parted at weekly afternoon meetings and monthly
evening gatherings.
To reduce “calling” to a science the new club
will institute a sensible “ calling bureau,” or card-
clearing house. Visitors will deal out the paste-
board for each acquaintance who belongs to
the club, instead of spending whole days in de-
positing the same in different spots among the
distant suburbs. Then the favored ones will
return the compliment, and there will be at stated
times a general sorting-out. The telephone is in a
retired corner, and discreetly curtained off. All
the arrangements are made for the formal opening
next Thursday. Male associates will be welcomed,
but they must rely on intellectual delights only, as
there is no billiard-table, no smoking-room, and
no bar.
Six nights of “ Romeo and Juliet ” have filled
the Lyceum d.c. with all the folk not departed to
Melb. for the races. This is quite a record run for
the sentimental classic, and it has been helped
along by several theatre-parties. One on Thurs-
day night consisted of many cycling notables
assembled for the Carnival, and was given, along
with a dinner at the Australia, by a visiting
enthusiast from Melb. The play was perfectly
dressed and staged. Mrs. Potter has an instinct
for color and a genius for getting effects out of
garments. She acts with her clothes all the time.
When she threw her scarf to Romeo, after press-
ing it with loud, twanging kisses in the balcony,
an irreverent audience guffawed at the inartistic
salute ; a kiss audible over a whole theatre would
surely have given the lady away to the nurse in
the room behind. In the coaxing scene, she
charged at that garrulous old party with a long
red flag, bull-ring fashion, and pranced round her
in Burnes-Jonesey green-and-white. After being
repulsed by pa Capulet, who fulfilled stage tradi-
tions by wearing velvet boots and losing his
temper throughout the play, she glimmered like a
ghost in voluminous white until the end, when she
did a Svengali death-scene, face to the audience,
with Romeo underneath playing table.
Kyrle Bellew went the right way to get hugged
by making Romeo a handsome Italian boy in
vivid scarlet, and afterwards in sad-green. Mer-
cutio took his mortal stroke in russet-brown, a
favorite tint with Scot Inglis, and Cyril Keightley
spmewhat quenched the fire of Tybalt in a swag-
like garment; Denham, as the apothecary, was so
ragged that if one pin had given way, the whole
garment would have collapsed.
A curious crowd assembled one morning last
week near the Post-office to see two ladies start on
a bicycle tour to Melbourne. The start had been
previously announced, not for good faith, but as a
guarantee of publication. As an advertising
medium the bike still holds the world’s record.
The wheelwomen were clad in the new costume,
the skirt of which is built on the roller-blind prin-
ciple and jerks up and down with a Spring, thereby
enabling the rider to combine comfort with ele-
gance. But when they reach Yarra’s; gentle
stream they will find all the Melbourne inhabit-
ants too busy acquitting bank-directors to notice
even a new cut of knickers. There “ rationals ”
are as common as the women who wear them,
and have ceased to excite comment.
The liosehill Racing Club had two fine days for
the Birthday meeting on Saturday and Monday
last, and an encouraging attendance. The sec.’s
eloquent invitations to the “ fair sex ” to grace his
lawn have had due effect, for the gathering con-
tained many of the Nicest, more of the Next-to-
Nicest, and an unplaced majority hovering on the
outskirts of the blessed. Rosehill, sometimes
called the Caulfield of Sydney, beats the bleak
heath of the V.A.T.C. easily in beauty, and can
crow over Randwick in its traffic arrangements.
On Monday trains ran quickly and punctually. It
is a beautiful little course with a fascinating
glimpse of the river from the back of the stand.
I may whisper in confidence that there are two
cheval glasses in the ladies’ room and poudre de
riz on the dressing-table. So the toilet accessories
are all right. Wagering women were there in
great force, picking winners industriously, and
rejoicing, to the disgust of every one around them,
in the fall of Tim’s rider in the Steeple—which
was at first thought fatal—because they had
backed The Ace. They blatantly shouted con-
gratulations to each other while the ambulance-
cart was carrying off Mr. Gathorne Hardy, who
swelled the long list of Guvmentouse casualties—
not seriously, people will be glad to hear.
Lady Tichborne —you may know her by a hat
she is fond of wearing with a flamboyant pink
bow—was scrutinised on the lawn as she has been
all the week at the Australia. She has been much
worried, too, by interviewers dropping in at 3
a.m. to inquire all about her impressions of Cress-
well as the original of a man dead long before she
was born, until she finally declared herself “ not
at nome to callers and handed her companion as
a stop-gap to the tormentors. Lady T. was simply
dressed in a blue alpaca skirt and grass-lawn
blouse, and walked ungloved, which —if you have
nice diamond rings—is now the correct snap. She
was accompanied by an escort of two in decorative
telt hats and touristic suits of grey tweed. Three-
energetic young women wheeled themselves out
trom btrathfield in neat tailor-made habits. A
bank-manager’s wife in black and white was keep-
ing up her proverbial luck by winning sweeps all
da .y- howns, as a rule, were fresh and summery,
and showed a coming boom in green, a trying tint
for pale wearers.
Whenever there’s joy in the dailies over one
yellow-skinned sinner who is converted and bap-
tisedjt is always now recorded in the par. that
“interesting ceremony” was jubilated by a
Chinese choir. This points the fact, methinks,
that m the near future we shall be able to raise
locally all the celestial choirs we need. Enter-
prising theatrical managers have already begun to
aid the boom in Mongol melody ; there is an in-
digenous lay in the plot of the “Serpent’s Coil,”
now running at Her Majesty’s, sung by a real live
Chinaman in the land of Chow-puppies and baby-
towers.
Attractions round the Hampden track at the
League of Wheelmen Carnival on Saturday were
in the following order: —Three bars, two tea-
rooms, and one cycle store. There was a well-
filled Reserve, and many of the best-dressed
appeared in tony frocks. A pretty frock of the
artless type was of green muslin over white silk—a
lettuce salad _ effect. Tea was struggled for by a
thirsty public and an evidently overwhelmed
waiter unprepared for the demand.
A matrimonial boom is setting in strongly in
Sydney. Next month no less than six “ sassiety ”
weddings will set six well-known couples honey-
mooning.
The Society of Artists is holding its Inter-
provincial Art Union Exhibition in the Tem-
perance Hall, Pitt-st., enhanced by an evening
diversion called a “drawing-room entertainment.”
This consists of a man in tweeds abruptly darting
from behind the curtain and falling into the
“Barber of Seville ” overture, and another man
hastily choking down a voice-lozenge and begin-
ning a monologue. The pictures seem to enjoy it,
and there’s usually a fair sprinkling of the public
in the chairs laughing also.
When men dwelt in caves and ate raw bear,
they fed in private. The instinct still clings to
the Anglo-Saxon who prefers to chew apart from
his kind. If he eats a plate of oysters he does it
furtively behind a screen, and thinks it indecent
to drink tea save under cover. The prejudice
seems too stubborn to be overcome, or else open-
air cafes might be established in dozens of places.
For instance, what a site for a cafe near the Mac-
quarie-street entrance to Sydney Botanic
Gardens. A cafe-concert, I mean, all lit up at
night, with waiters rushing round with wine, lager-
bier, and sugar-water, and a girl singing raucously
to the band ! But if that is too daring a vision,
surely there is nothing wrong with having tea-
kiosks erected here and there in the gardens ?
Sydney parks are so badly kept that the very
word “ park ” sounds disreputable and conjures
up a vision of a dead-beat prone on the dusty grass
asleep amidst squashed figs, bushels of dead leaves,
orange-peel, and swirls of newspaper. What
their after-dark aspect is I don’t know, for no
respectable woman can venture into one' of the
enclosures after nightfall.
At the Samuell concert, at the Town Hall on
Saturday night, one of Sydney’s numerous infant
phenomena appeared—a youthful Pecliotsch, aged
uine—-and posed as the Australian Paginini, with
his little fiddle. The baby-genius is rife here.
Wherever one goes one hears a musical prodigy of
tender years thundering on the piano with the
vicious wrist-force of five-and-twenty in a suburban
drawing-room, or filing-down one’s nerves by
making the violin bow squeak like a pencil on a
slate. What is the ultimate end of all these suck-
ing St. Cecilias? Talent is abnormally developed
in some Sydney children. Unfortunately, it. gene-
rally takes the musical form. There are enough
talented juveniles now exercising their little digits
to keep up the supply of local genius well into the
next century. Gifted posterity will be, in fact,
crowded out if existing talent isn’t merely a sprint.
But these youngsters, somehow, haven’t any stay-
ing power ; they promise well, but don’t fulfil
prophecy of greatness. The infant prodigy, as a
prodigy, may be wondered at, but not enjoyed.
To play a “ piece” excellently is not the aim of
the musician ; musical expression is only valuable
as it shows the mind of the artist. Childish per-
formance expresses nothing at all.
Mrs. Hamilton, winner of Tattersall’s big
prize, .£13,500, on the Melb. Cup, is a shrewd and
careful Sydney suburban widow.
The latest Sydney vogue is for fat men to give
admiring girls a treat by cycling in knickerbockers
and slick silk stockings. One stout person bikes
regularly round the Block in pale grey, and there
are two of him so much alike in hat, face, figure,
and dress, that I look up to see if he has
“ Tweedledum ” or “ Tweedledee ” on his collar.
My Melb. correspondent writes :
“ The race-child was much in evidence at Flem-
ington on Cup-day. By child is not meant the
weird maiden who has tripped the land for the
last half-dozen seasons, always in the innocent
garb of childhood with guileless short skirts and
carefully dyed-and-frizzed hair, and broad ribbon
hat-strings tied under her sharp-looking, unyouth-
ful face, and who never seems to get any younger
or older, and who is indigenous to Melbourne only;
but the infant of five or six who is lugged about by
a .K a ily-attired mother, and who becomes tired out
and a nuisance before the day is half over. No
doubt, the fine weather on Tuesday last was the
cause of so many children being dragged out to
make stumbling-blocks on the lawn. A creche
should be provided for the race-child under the
grand-stand.
“A more brilliant sight than the Cup race with
Newhaven leading, and vast spaces of daylight
between him and the field, hasn’t been seen at
Flemington since Carbine’s year. Both of the
stands were crowded, and the lawn with its new
rose-gardened fencing looked like a huge bunch of
flowers. Never were chiffons so gorgeous and
variegated ; almost any gown there present would
have been regarded a few seasons ago as over-
gaudy for ball-wear. Even the sombre-looking
Govt. House set bloomed out into vivid blues and
pinks and violets. Lady Brassey’s pale grey had
a scintillating corselet of gold tissue. Lady Idina
B. was most festively attired in pale blue silk and
chiffon and lawn embroidery ; and Mrs. Freeman-
Thomas’s frock was simply a mass of filmy white
chiffon over silk. Most women carried parasols
of scarlet silk or some other shade equally vivid,
which helped out the brilliant tout ensemble nicely.
One lady annexed the whole rainbow in her gown,
laid on in broad stripes. She only needed a pen
and ink and a petition-form to suggest Cole’s
Book-arcade. Absolutely the best-dressed woman
out on Tuesday was one whose gaudy gamboge
tresses quite neutralised the effect of her exquisite
white crepe gown.
“ The belles of former years who have com-
mitted matrimony seemed to read a silent homily
against the alleged happy state. A few years of
double harness seem to take all the snap out of
the Melb. society girl. Some women, who, three
.or four Cups agone, made their names rattle like
a dog’s chain through all the screeds of the fashion
scribes by reason of their dash and chic, turned
out this season but sallow', dowdy ghosts of their
former selves. Other matrons guard against this
total annihilation of charms by perosided tresses
and complexions as fresh as paint can make them,
but in'"both cases the result is a bad advt. for
matrimony.
. Eacfcbf the gowns which the hostess of Clive-
den and the wife of the Chief Justice wore during
the meeting had done hard service during the
Sydney festivities. The latter’s prettiest gown—
white silk with cornflower fixings—was worn on
Cup-day, while the former sported her smartest
frock—white silk with a sort of inky pen-wiper
pattern in black on it—on Derby-day. Mrs.
Brough, who came out in the stewed-rhubarb silk
and Frenchy toque of Mrs. Ponderbury (Ist act),
brought Emma Temple in mannish blue cloth
with white collar and cuffs, and pretty Lily
Titheradge in.girlish white muslin. An imposing-
looking Sydney belle, in flower-besprinkled grass-
lawn over green silk, chaperoned a daintily-pretty
Melb. girl whose sister has been hoisted by an
Hinglish marriage into the ranks of the top-lofty,
and now regards her belongings from that proud
eminence.
“ Men’s fashions were as varied as the costumes
in a circus-ring. Melb. men never seem to have
made up their minds as to the correct garb for a
race-meeting. Even the Govt. House set seemed
divided between the vast encircling ‘frock’-coat
and silk fcat and the obsolete ‘ Beaufort ’ or sac
and ‘ boxer.’ The best-dressed men this season
let themselves go in the matter of neckties and
vests. Gorgeous tartan cravats tied French
fashion with loose flowing ends, and vests of gay
buff cloth, peppered over with blue or pink or
green spots, gave the ‘ smartest ’ a milliner-made,
even dolly appearance. Some sported waistcoats
of black brocaded cloth with spreading patterns
of palms and scrolls on them. Some Westralians
paraded the lawn in flannels with low-cut collars
and bold advts. for their own particular wild-cat,
iii, the shape of alleged nuggets dangling from
their watch-chains.
“ The sight of Cup-day was a tall, lanky, clean-
shaven, curly-locked individual, frock-coated and
billytoppered, who strutted the lawn, closely fol-
lowed by a diminutive nigger in livery, who care-
fully carried the lordly one’s field-glasses.
. “None but members of the Guvmentouse party
were seen within the Governor’s box at Fleming-
ton during the spring meeting—contrary to the
custom of Loch and Hopetoun, who used to issue
invitations to the charmed circle promiscuously.
Neither were any guests present at the vice-regal
luncheon, which fact is inexplicable, considering
that the stewards pay for the ki-ki.
“ The distinction seems to be, not to receive a
card of invitation for the race-week garden-party
at Govt. House. On Friday last the sacred lawns
of the vice-regal abode were trodden by the pro-
fane feet of scores, who spent the time in wonder-
ing how the others had got there. That each
person present was armed with a passport is
certain, for the gates were sternly guarded by
armed men, who demanded “Cards, please,” and
eyed each comer in a manner which would have
daunted any not shielded by a vast allotment of
pasteboard. This rather humiliating method of
admitting guests was instituted during the Loch
regime, and has been rigorously adhered to by
each succeeding Yic. Excellency, though the plan
is unknown in any of the other provinces.
“Lady Brassey, gowned in the cream silk and
pale blue and gold confection which became
familiar to Melb. folk during the ’95 festivities,
received the crowd on the upper lawn ; on the
lower a band poured forth “ Chinatown ” strains,
and afternoon-tea was struggled for ineffectually
in a rather limited marquee close at hand. Mrs.
Freeman Thomas wore her Derby dress of grass-
lawn over white silk and bore aloft a vivid green
’brolly. Lady Idina Brassey sported her Cup
gown of pale blue silk covered with tinted lace.
Many well-dressed women, including Lady Hamp-
den, favored sombre garbs of magpie-like black and
white. A good deal more grass-lawn was visible on
thebacks of the women present than under-foot, and
among the few who managed to wear this hack-
neyed fabric with any distinction were two who
had built their gowns on rosy-pink silk. The Hon.
Helen Brassey, in white embroidered muslin and a
flapping hat, was trotted round during the blanks
in the afternoon’s entertainment by pa, and under-
went much hugging and admiration from hordes
of gushful women. Young Freeman-Thomas, in
white piqu4, was also on view during the after-
noon.
“ Lady Brassey seems to have overcome her ob-
jection to watching the gee-gees throw their riders
and themselves in the steeplechase, and went out
with the Govt. House party to Flemington on
Saturday. Lady Hampden, however, did not
turn up, and missed thereby the most unpleasant
day of the whole meeting. Despite the cold,
showery weather the dressing, on the whole, was
very brilliant. Lady B. was in demure grey with
accordeon-pleated frills and narrow bands of black
velvet. The Brand girls wore white, the bon.
Alice breaking through the gloom which
had enshrouded the family lately by ap-
pearing in a highly colored green hat.
Lady Madden, in serpent-colored foulard with
dabs of white on it, brought her three daughters
all smartly enough attired to suggest vast raids on
the chief judicial salary. Lady Clarke, in b’ue
foulard, patterned with white, brought pretty
Miss Hilda Cumming, in a Bond-street gown of
grey silk, with trimmings of a strange new-pink
shade. One of the features of the afternoon was
a vast, comprehensive tea-party, whereat ‘ sas-
siety ’ endeavored to devour ice-cream and im-
mature strawberries with chattering teeth, while
the icy north wind froze the tea in the urns, and
the rain saturated the tablecloth.
“Sadie M‘Donald is not visiting the Vic. pro-
vinces with the Chinatown Co. She unfortunately
sprained her back while dancing, and is laid up in
a Melb. private hospital.
“Miss Alice Henry, a pen-woman whose level
head is associated with prematurely gray hair, has
started operations in Melbourne as a ‘ town-
shopper ’ and general undertaker of female com-
missions. Her business desire is to make herself
useful, on moderate terms, to far-away matrons.
She will buy their clothes for them, engage ser-
vants and governesses on their behalf, supply
information re dressmakers, attend auction-sales,
and, possibly, tend in the direction of furnishing
husbands for the girls, although the prospectus is
silent on this point. Also she will be prepared to
trot her clients around town, when they happen to
journey up from the interior, and give them
ocular demonstration of how bargains are picked
up. A lady of education and taste, alert to all
the wiles of the wicked draper, and blessed with
plenty of tact, Miss Henry is a type of New
Womanhood which has come to stay. Her office
and impressive door-plate are at No. 7 Queen’s
Walk, Melb.
“ Of her first commissions, it is said, one came
from Queensland for a white perambulator; another,
from Tocumwal, for a pulpit. ‘We have had an
extraordinary demand for perambulators of late,
Madam,’ said the head of one go-cart department
when the proxy alighted on his floor. ‘ What age
is your baby ? ’ The lady, who, like Marie Corelli,
is happily unmarried, went elsewhere for the baby-
brougham. The pulpit call dazed her most,
though. She finally got one at the Cathedral
Chapter House. ”
Y ours affectionately,
CLEO.

Personal Items.
Gov. Brassey is quietly picking up plums
in Australian real property.
R. S. Smythe edited in the fifties the first
Australian picture paper —the Illustrated Post.
Melb. Trades Hall boss coterie now goes
in for top-hats and frock-coats. The “ frock-
coaterie,” in fact.
Dr. Alfreda Hilda Gamble, M. 8., 8.5.,
one of the resident surgeons of Melbourne Hos-
pital, is booked for matrimony by a leading Melb.
sawbones.
Crown Solicitor Stuart, S.A., lately re-
turned to work after an illness during which three
doctors gave him up. And the fourth one, who
prophesied a recovery, died himself.
Judge Boucaut (S.A.) has a horror of
murder cases, although he has tried but one (which
ended in the rope) during his 18-years judicial
career. Recently he exchanged sessions with
Way, C.J., and now the latter has to try the
Bessie Mann case in December.
Ex-M.L.A. John L. Dow loves to tell
how, when he lived at Edom, a little village not
far from Melbourne, he received from the post-
man a letter addressed, “Hon. J. L. Dow, Sodom.”
Across the address the jokeful Edomite postmaster
had written, “ Not known at Sodom. Try Go-
morrah.”
Vic. Acting-Agent-General Andrew Clarke
spent just 10 years in Victoria, and was all the
time in receipt of a rattling good State screw.
Pensioned in ’62, at £BOO a year, he has drawn to
date in pension cheques some £27,2- 0 of public
funds in return for doing nothing. He is a first
cousin of the late Marcus Clarke.
Apropos the fact that Purves, Q.C., lately
tried to quote the Lord’s Prayer in court but
boggled, trot out this one, for the benefit of the
generation which knows not Joseph. Bookie
No. 1 wagered that Bookie No. 2 didn’t know the
Lord’s Prayer. No. 2 began the Apostles’ Creed.
Whereupon No. 1 paid the wager.
Gov. Brassey has been touring the Vic.
Alps, and has been so much impressed by the
scenery that he has promised local residents to
“ write a panegyric ” about it. A Brassey pane-
gyric of scenery ! A dash of statistics, a chunk of
quotation, a bit about the Empyah and the navy,
and an allusion to Brassey’s contractor-father.
The death of Parkes removes the last of
the band of persons officially connected with the
trial and execution of the mad O’Farrell in 1868.,
Judge, hangman, barristers and solicitors—both
for prosecution and defence —are all gone. And
the Duke of Edinburgh, who was the one man
whose life was sought to be taken, survives, hale
and hearty.
Nothing m this life used to rile Judge
limes more than to say that he was grandson of
the celebrated Margaret Catchpole. As a matter
of fact, he wasn’t. On the Catchpole-Margaret-
Reibey question Mr. G. B. Barton, who lias lately
acquired curious documentary information from
England, will shortly have something very in-
teresting to say.
