The father of the
drought-ruined S.A. farmer
Thyer (who lately mur-
dered his wife and five
children, afterwards suicid-
ing) was taken to the luna-
tic asylum the week after
the tragedy. He was once
a wealthy squatter, but the
advance of agricultural set-
tlement, together with dry
seasons, effectually ruined
him.
In N.S.W. Bar circles,
petticoats are generally un-
derstood to be at the bot-
tom of a recent judicial
breeze. There is always a
woman beneath the Bench,
tugging at His Honor’s
gown, and when both wo-
men get under the Bench
the squeal begins. In Mel-
bourne, the other day, Judge Hartley Williams
would have been Chief Justice had his wife not
offended the wife of a Minister of the Crown.
G. A. Lloyd, who obtained leave of absence
from N.S.W. Leg. Council last week on the score
of ill-health, was the first in N.S.W. to start the
regular business of buying gold-dust, as an inter-
mediary between miners and the banks.

that the Premiers of the various Australias have
been asked by the Colonial Office to suggest the
names of eligible citizens to receive knighthood on
Jubilee Day (June 20, 1897).
N. : “ Fitchett’s Trafalgar is most excel-
lent as artistry. But what a contrast to his Sun-
day preaching ! According to this, the Trafalgar
killed would pretty well all go down to Sheol in

John E. Kelly, who died last week at Peak
Hill, lost £6OOO in attempting to run the aggres-
sively agnostic Stockwhip in Sydney some 20
years ago. When he was elected for the Bogan,
Parkes wrote to him, requesting a personal visit
as soon as he reached Sydney. A portfolio for
certain, friends whispered ; but when John called
Parkes merely showed him the autographs of three
or four kings and asked him to second the Address
in Reply.
Melbourne has been unlucky in
its Munros. There was “ Lady ”
Munro who used to make an unholy
spectacle of herself in the publio
streets. Also, Premier Jimmy Munro
and his Federal Bank clan, and
thirdly David Munro, the engineer,
of City of Melbourne Bank noto-
riety. What’s wrong with the name ?
A certain lately-retired Austra-
lian Judge was “often moved to tears.”
There was another Judge who was
often so moved, especially when there
was a woman in the case. The pre-
sent scribe has seen a brief drawn by
John Edwards, the younger, solicitor,
for G, P. Smith, counsel, in which this
paragraph was underscored “ Plain-
tiff will cry in the witness-box, and
Judge Pohlman will cry with her. See
that she doesn’t cry him into a verdict
for her. ” As a matter of fact plaintiff
did cry, Pohlman followed suit, and
almost stepped off the Bench to wipe
her eyes.
Archbishop Murphy, whose epis-
copal jubilee was celebrated at Hobart
the other day, with much pomp and
ceremony, was born on Waterloo Day.
Was in India, conducting missions at
Hyderabad, at the time of the Mutiny,
and armed his students for the purpose
of giving the mutineers a taste of
Donnybrook. But the students just
had to keep on spoiling for a fight.
Thirty years of Tasmania have left
the venerable cleric with one idea and
no hearing worth mentioning. The
one idea is to complete the cathedral,
the foundation-stone of which edifice
has been laid no less than seven times,
each laying resulting in additional con-
tributions from the faithful. Sydney’s
Cardinal performed the ceremony on
the present occasion, and another £lOOO
was planked down to gladden the heart of the
popular octogenarian.
H. H. Champion does not suffer from
excess of modesty. In a notice of “E. Nesbit’s ”
work in a recent issue of H.H.C’s paper, he
turns aside to supply this thrilling' item :
E. Nesbit is the wife of Herbert Bland, one of the
Fabian essayists, and a smart journalist whose character
sketch of the Editor of this paper can be seen at this
office-on payment of half-a-crown to the funds of the
Anti-Sweating League.
The man who thinks the populace is going to rush
along to read about his characteristics, “ on pay-
ment of half-a-crown,” has too much honest self-
esteem for the profession of letters —he should be
a society actor or a show musician.
“HIS CHRISTMAS BOX.”
MRS. SKINFLINT: “ Josiah, don’t you think the boy wants his hair
cut? ”
MR. SKINFLINT : “ How long is it till Christmas? ”
MRS. S. : “Six weeks.”
MR. S.: “ Let him wait till then, and Ell make him a Christmas-box
of a hair-cut! ”
There was lately discharged from Vic.
public service, on account of broken-down health,
a poor old woman who had done charing work at
Parliament House for 30 years. Speaker Berry
recommended that she receive a small gratuity
from the Government; but Premier Turner replied
with an emphatic “No!” and the unfortunate
creature, who is over 70 and a pauper, has to
go to an asylum like any other aged charing-
woman. When will old-age pensions for work-
people come along?
Hoo Ki, the Bowlahmine Chinaman, mar-
ried a Caucasian girl. When Hoo first saw his
son and heir he showed no emotion for some
seconds ; then turning to Mrs. Hoo Ki he ejacu-
lated : “ Lachel, you foolee me. Him baby
gotem led hair. I gotem black hair. You gotem
blown hair. Me know Mister Hooligan, policee-
man, gotem led hair. Me know—me setem cab-
bagee, come up cabbagee ; me setem lettucee,
come up lettucee ; setem Chinaman, come up
policeeman—no fear ! ”
Albert Edward of Wales is in sore trouble.
The son of the late Sir James Mackenzie claims a
million pounds which the latter is alleged to have
advanced to his royal friend, and Baron Hirsch’s
executors have made out a long bill for sundry
very large amounts lent by the Baron to Tummy
at various periods. Under these pressing circum-
stances, W. W. Astor is considered a not “un-
likely ” candidate for the hand of Princess Vic-
toria of W ales.
Kerry, photo.
J. CARPENTER, champion road cyclist of Australia.
Premier Reid, in the matter of appoint-
ments to N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench, is nearly
as lucky as Parkes. In two years he has had
three vacancies at his disposal. If the present
vacancy is offered to the very best man available
in Australia —the man with most all-round ability
and who would do most to make the office re-
spected, Edmund Barton, Q.C., will have the first
refusal.

vacant on the retirement of the veteran Judge
Forbes, is, so goes the story, to be conferred on a
comparative m-w-chum, who has, like a certain
well-advertised detective, the merit of being
“ loyal to the er —Crown Law Office.” A N.S.W.
District Court Judgeship is a most comfortable
post : =£lsoo a year salary, liberal travelling ex-
penses, and £750 a year pension.
It is said in Melbourne (where he has been
on a visit) that Sir Smith, the Westralian Excel-
lency, is as tired of W. A. as its people are of
him, and will return to England on an extended
leave before long. Smith is one of Joe Chamber-
lain’s friends. He dislikes Premier Forrest, who
returns the dislike with interest. Smith thinks a
heap of his dignity, but it is hard to be dignified
while bearing a name like Jerry Smith.

“Jim” Carpenter, among Australian-born
cyclists, ranks as boss of the road. Twenty-six
years old, he didn’t start cycling until years
ago ; which comparative tardiness of beginning
results in comparative tardiness of sprinting.
For Carpenter, game as he is, can’t “ pick her up ”
quickly m a finish. But no man, so far, has been
able to camp on Carpenter’s tail in a road-race.
His first race was 36 miles on the road from Pen-
tridge out to Beveridge and back ; he won by
three miles. Last year in Melbourne he made
fastest time in every road-race run. This year
he excelled himself and beat everybody by plug-
ging from Warrnambool to Melbourne, 165 miles,
in Bhr. 40min. And while he is the most
enduring, he is also the most unlucky. He
scarcely ever rides a race without falling,
or without something breaking, or without
several things conspiring against him. He
was brought down in the recent 25-mile race in
Sydney through no fault of his own, and it would
be hard to say what rider’s chance at starting was
better than his ; but his worst streak of crazy-bad
luck was when he was winning the first prize (.£250)
in the last A.N.A. sports, and was downed on the
post by Hourigan stealing up unseen. Born of
Irish parents and a miller by vocation, he is one
of the best “doers” on this continent; he could
work hard all day long and maintain his condition
on corned beef. He is so strong within himself
that he has offered to race Bilmartin 200 miles on
the road for £2OO. He weighs close on list,
stripped, stands sft. 6in., measures just on 40in.
round the chest. His weakest part is his arms,
but when they have boxing-gloves on the ends of
them they are very serviceable indeed.
Judge Backhouse, ex-Justice Windeyer,
and Premier Reid all arrived in Sydney before
they were 12 months old.
The window of Jimmy Mirams’ stationery
shop at Collins-street, Melbourne, is very charac-
teristic of the little man behind the counter.
The stock on view includes Bibles for the regener-
ate few and whisky-flasks and packs of playing-
cards for the goats. As a teetotaller Jimmy sells
the flasks under protest, so to speak.
John Norton’s account of the narrow escape
of a Goulburn man, in a trial for treason-felony,
recalls a little-known incident in the life of Victor
Hugo. While an exile from Prance the poet took
refuge in the island of Jersey, but was expelled by
the States on refusing to drink the toast of the
Queen at a public banquet. This was the cause of
his going to Guernsey, where he lived for so many
years.
Judge Boucaut, of S.A. (an ex-Premier of
the province), was 05 on October 29. He breeds
horses for India, while Way, C.J., devotes his
leisure to sheep. It has been suggested that if a
man were smart enough to steal steeds from
Boucaut, jum bucks from Way, and fowls from
Bundey he would so cripple S.A. Supreme Court
bench in respect to his trial as to ensure getting
off scot-free.
The late Baron von Mueller was absent-
minded, as savants often are. When a guest one
warm night at a Toorak dance, he was missing
awhile from the festive scene, but was afterwards
found by the hostess in the ball-room. During his
absence he had by some means exchanged the
crush hat with which he had been fanning himself
all the evening for an oval-shaped piece of wood
with a knob in the centre of it.
S.A. Premier Kingston’s greatest enemies
are perhaps the religious cranks. Anonymous
letters in the Tory papers comparing the Premier
with Ananias are of daily occurrence, and occa-
sionally the menu is varied by subtle references to
Jeremiah, and Pontius Pilate, and Judas Iscariot,
and other old Bible cranks; but recently some-
body wrote warning Kingston of the fate of
Rehoboam. After examining the subject all
round, the S.A. Premier feels rather compli-
mented, for although Rehoboam, like Kingston,
got into a warm corner on occasions, he reigned
over Jerusalem for 22 years, and Kingston reckons
if he can last that long he will have a big chance
of settling the Adelaide hospital difficulty.
Mr. C. H. Kerry, the N.S.W. pigeon-snot,
who won the much-contested Hordern Shield, is a
true son of Australia, and a thorough sportsman.
Kerry , photo.
Mr. G. H. KERRY, winner of the Hordern
100 guineas pigeon-shooting trophy.
He has tramped after game on plain and moun-
tain, shot and retrieved numberless waterfowl on
lake and river ; shot by moon, by stars, by ear.
He has bored his way into coastal scrubs and
filled his bag with pigeons and himself with ticks ;
has wriggled on his stomach a half-mile by night
over clay pans for nothing, because the whistlers
sighted him first. Also on occasions he has
emptied both barfels and two more into said
whistlers, and come home laden with ducks and
mud. He is an enthusiastic horseman, and
learned to ride in his birth-place, at Snowy River,
the break-neck district. Kerry learnt to shoot
by getting himself shot; at the age of nine, he
“ sneaked ” a curiosity which his elder brother had
bought for £1; he loaded it with powder and
shot in the delusion that it was a gun. Then in
the presence of several covetous mates he pulled
the spring upon a neighbour’s rooster. However,
he continued to live.

POLITICAL
POINTS
In N.S.W. Parliament, the President of
the Council and Speaker of the Assembly are
both natives and Protectionists.
Since '62, N.S. Wales has spent in round
numbers half a million sterling on exhibitions.
This allows for the receipts from the Garden
Palace Exhibition.
Sixty-three years ago N.S.W. abolished
capital punishment for cattle-stealing, forgery and
theft. Tell us—what evil consequences ensued ?
John Haynes’s motion for the utter abolition of
hanging is merely late in the day.
Three Australasian Premiers—Turner,
Kingston, and Seddon—are steadily killing them-
selves with hard work. In each case there are
only two, or at most three, really hard workers in
the Cabinet, and these carry the whole weight of
the Government.
Coolgardie is sure that it would have got
the Menzies railway but for some sinister influence
which moved the Govt, to change its mind. Local
opinion sets down the loss of the line to the Separa-
tion cry, or to the pilling of a certain politician at
the Coolgardie Club.
Seventy truant-children cases were brought
before Richmond (Melb.) Court, and the excuse in
43 cases was “ no clothes ” or “no boots.” Apropos,
Victorian truant-inspectors make their billet an
easy one by only looking up the truants on the
school-roll, the gutter class not being interfered
with.
Be the interruptions and amendments of
the Socialists and other democrats at such affairs
as the Melb. Trafalgar meeting, the local “great
dailies ” are trying on the “ conspiracy of silence ”
so well served its purpose in the case of
Jos. Symes. But it won’t work so well on all
occasions, as the big newspapers will find to their
cost one day.
Queensland Govt, is about to encourage
the immigration of people from Germany and
Southern Europe. As far as the Australian is
concerned, the cheap Latin is as deadly as the
Chow and the Jap. Between Chows, Germans,
Greeks, Kanakas, Hindoos, Italians, Japs., and
Malays, Queenslanders will be a lovely mixture
next century.
The Electoral Bill, now before S.A. As-
sembly, abolishes the Absent Voters Act, origin-
ally passed for the benefit of workers, particularly
seamen, absent on duty on polling-dav. Although
largely availed of by the latter, the system was so
successfully made the means of Tory abuse and
roll-manipulation that a large Assembly majority
demands its abolition.
THE IRONY OF FATE.
(A sketch in George - street, Sydney.)
Had not N.S.W. Speaker Abbott taken
back his decision preventing Premier Reid’s reply
to Opposition leader Lyne’s personalities in the
discussion on land-valuers the other night, it was
determined to move in dissent. This would have
meant that either Reid or Abbott should go, and
would, to all intents and purposes, have been
treated as a crisis. Prophets say “Joe” will
some night decline to “ take it back.”
The closure which now has Tasmania by
the ears was introduced into Australian politics
by M'Culloch in Victoria in 187fi. The “ Gag ”or
“Iron Hand,” wherever introduced since, has
been supposed to need local Parliamentary
authority, Tasmania alone depending on the
authority of the British Parliament.
Had Copeland introduced a Bill simply to
throw open N.S. W. public-houses during certain
hours on Sundays, he would have carried his
measure without difficulty, but by mixing un
Good Friday, Christmas Day, and public and
private clubs with the prime question, he makes
liis Bill vulnerable by, at all events, the genius of
delay.
N.S. W. Referendum Bill was thrown out
of the Leg. Council by 30 votes against 9. Th e
Reid Government, only a few months ago, sen**
what it considered as 10 reliable supporters
to the Council, in addition to those already
there, yet on this essential measure it only mus-
tered nine votes. The democratising of a House
of irresponsible nominees is a hopeless under-
taking.
What animates the current craze to build
palatial Houses of Parliament somewhere about
Sydney is the fact that .£lOO,OOO has been already
voted as a first contribution for the purpose. That
vote was taken at a rollicking, plentiful period and
when Federation loomed dim. Some member
should test the sense of Parliament to-day by
moving to rescind that vote. Otherwise’ the
Premier may begin the plunge.
Ass-President Moulden (S.A.), when in
Sydney the other day, sneered at S.A. Labor poli-
ticians, “ who could not earn £4 a week except in
Parliament.” His predecessor in the presidency
of the League has just assigned his estate, which
is pot expected to pay more than 9d. in the £. The
leader of S.A. Opposition (now a member of the
League) publicly confessed, some time ago, that he
could not afford to be in Parliament if it were not
for payment of members ; and more than one
member voting the same ticket is notoriously in a
similar position. So that Solicitor Moulden, who
once posed as a Liberal himself, and who was
Attorney-General for awhile under Dr. Cockburn,
is wielding a two-edged sword.
It is a good thing for a Ministry to be per-
sistently boosted by the metropolitan press. Ex-
Premier Dibbs became known as the boss blunderer
of Australia; yet Dibbs was a diplomatic and
tactful person compared with Reid, only the city
press persistently called him a blunderer till the
name “ caught on.” Reid’s whole career has been
a persistent falling over his own large, awkward
feet, still the public hasn’t yet begun to realise
that Jollyandfat is the most astounding blunderer
who has held office in recent years. Consider a
few of his Government’s performances : The pro-
duction of that bogus list of Dibbs’s civil service
appointments; the bogus list of Victorian prices
(both of these documents had to Ve withdrawn
and apologised for) ; the arrest of Crick on no
shadow of evidence; the capsize of Reid’s first
surplus by the State Auditor, who showed it to
have been manufactured by taking credit for
amounts which belonged to previous years ; the
capsize by the State Auditor in the same report of
the well-worn yarn about the Dibbs deficit, which
proved to be partly manufactured by Reid ; the
back-down re the promise to establish a State note
issue ; the Land-tax muddle. G. H. Reid’s is the
Great Muddle Minis* of Australia.

Under the N.S. Wales
Public Health Bill every
municipality is' to (1) pro-
vide isolated accommodation
and attendance for every
family in which an infec-
tious disease may appear;
(2) compensate owners for
infected bedding and other
property destroyed ; (3) es-
tablish a place and appa-
ratus for proper disinfec-
tion ; (4) make a reasonable
allowance to tenants for cost
of removing their goods from
a condemned habitation ;
(5) provide a medical officer
to enforce the Act. In the
present state of things this
means 41 quarantine stations
and disinfectingstationsand
disinfecting apparatus and
salaried medical officers for
a district all within sight of
SydneyP.O. tower. Another
good reason for hurrying up
municipal federation in and
around Sydney.
The Seddon (M.L.) Government goes to
the country with a programme which includes the
Old Age Pension scheme, State fire-insurance re
form of the Local Government system which’has
grown too large and cumbrous, the acquisition on
a larger scale than heretofore of bio- estates and
the establishment of workmen’s villa°-es on the
outskirts of cities. This straight and definite
programme contrasts strongly with the Tory
party’s hazy maunderings about promoting “ free-
clom of enterprise ” and similar hunks of lofty
moral fog.

St. Just: “ Youth is, above all, needed in
Australian politics. Take the junior member of
Yic. Parliament, Cook, sent in brand-new, and
observe that he does just as well as almost anyone.
Impulse only comes from the young. Thus all
remarked on the glorious youth which character-
ised the Parliament that ushered in the French
Revolution. The Old Man of the Sea is the so-
called mature politician, who, like the cunning old
soldier, won’t fight. The go-ahead Turner
Cabinet is the youngest ever known.”

Melb. Argus quotes with enthusiasm the
sneer of a globe-trotting Frenchman, M. Leroy-
Beaulieu —“ The Australian colonies seem to be-
lieve that they have a mission to lead the world in
the path of progress.” The French gentleman
didn't get his notions of national sentiment from
the Argus, anyhow. Nobody has ever accused
that lamentable journal of wishing to bustle man-
kind along the path of progress.
Alfred Saunders, “Father” of the M.L.
House of Representatives (brother to A. E. Saun-
ders, the Walworth, Eng., M.P.) was born June
12, 1820, landed in Nelson (M.L.) in ’42—“ one of
the first.” In ’55, sat in Prov. Council, and
was J.P’d in ’5B ; the following year, for slating
Judge Travers in the Examiner, he was “sent
up ’’ for six months, fined ,£l5O, and struck off the
magisterial roll. He was reinstated instanter on
petition, and from gaol was again transferred
to the Prov. Council, having been elected thereto
whilst still in durance. Then they sent him up
as M.H.R., where he declined the Col. Treasurer-
A. SAUNDERS, Father of M.L. Parliament.
Wrigglesworth & Binns, photo
ship under Wm. Fox; he was twice elected
Superintendent of Nelson province, resigning in
fi/, and it was during his term that the murderous
Maungatapu quartet gang (Burgess, Kelly, Levy,
and Sullivan) were captured. After a long trip to
England, he settled in Canterbury, where in ’77
J'® was M.H.R d for Cheviot, and yet again in
7J. In ’BO he was chairman of that drastic body
of reformers, the Civil Service Commission. Lin-
coln took him to herself in ’B9—and to that seat
he has since stuck As a writer, “ Our Domestic

ship. 1,8 said Seddon offered the Treasurership
to Saunders on Ward’s resignation, but the
I ather of the House declined.
There is a half-penny postage on news-
papers iii Freetrade England and a half-penny
postage in Protectionist Victoria. And N.S.
Wales will have a similar postage when it get's a
straight-going Government, able to look the morn-

When that circumnavigating Yankee skip*
per called in at Brisbane the other day, local
quidnuncs wanted to know why he had paid
Bananaland a visit. The Yankee averred he had
been informed Queensland was the only country
without a public debt, and he had called to see a
land so interesting. Considering that the Q. debt
averages about £69 per head of the population, the
Yankee’s remark savours somewhat of sarcasm.
Melbourne Conservatives are very angry
indeed with young Tom Brassey for delivering a
lecture in which he says that Socialists are not the
fiendish folk they have been represented to be, but
are on the contrary, the apostles of a desirable
mode of social evolution. The address was “mis-
chievous,” the Argus says. The anger of the
Tory rump is all the more furious because the
young man spoke with the Government House
aureole round his head. They see. in awful visions,
a red flag flying from the white tower on the south
bank of the Yarra, with a cap of Liberty sur-
mounting the flagstaff.
A Bulletin correspondent:—
At Albany (W.A.) “ forts ” —one of the leading defences
of Australia from the Russian invader —there are 26
officers, non- com missioned officers, and
men, including two engineers attached
from other corps. These 26 are sup-
posed to man and work six 3in.
breech - loading guns on barbette
mountings, two nine-pounder muzzle-
loading field-guns, two Maxim guns,
and two Hotchkiss sixpounders. (The
Hotchkiss guns are not mounted).
The men are supplied with carbines
in use since 1879 seventeen
years; and the forts are laid
out not, apparently, to protect
men working at the guns, but
as slaughter-pits. One gun is
on an elevation 370 ft. above sea-
level ; and above the level of the
gun, and not 20ft. from it, are
huge granite rocks. If a single
common shell struck there the gun-
ners would be taken away in frag-
ments. Another gun is fully 100
yards from the magazine, and in
bringing ammunition to it the men
would be exposed to open fire from
the enemy. It is easy to see from
this how adequately Albany forts
are maintained (vide Major-
General Hutton at a meeting of
the Federal Defence Commit-
tee at ’Orae).
Aid. Fuller, M.L.C.,
is being- brought out by
the National Ass to run
against Tucker for the Ade-
laide civic chair. The election
is on Dec. 1, and the light will be
close, as many Tories have a vote in each six
wards. A bill to abolish plurality of votes for
mayor, aldermen and auditors is now before Par-
liament, but it is sure to be rejected by the Leg.
Council.
The land-grantrailway syndicates now per-
vading Tasmania don’t ask for so very much
after all. There are two which want to build a
railway to the west coast. One—a Melbourne
affair—will condescend to build and own the line
if it gets the necessary land gratis and receives a
grant of about 960,000 acres in addition. The
English syndicate wants the land gratis, a grant
of 288,000 acres, and the province to permanently
guarantee 2 per cent, interest on the cost of con-
struction, equal to £20,000 a year. They don’t
ask anything more than this ! The syndicate is a
modest and retiring affair and easily satisfied.
Sydney Evening News fills a leading
article with lofty scorn because some N.S.W. Pro-
tectionist politicians sent a cable of congratula-
tion to M'Kinley, the coming U.S. President. It
was a somewhat asinine act, certainly, but con-
sidering the placid approval of the Noose when
a similar crowd of Freetraders cabled their con-
gratulations to Cleveland (who hadn’t the civility
to reply) on an exactly similar occasion, the
News would seem to have a bad memory or
to suffer from a very bad attack of hypocrisy.
Moreover, the Protectionist politicians didn’t
make themselves ridiculous by calling a publio
meeting over the matter as the Noose crowd did.
A Celebrated Novelist.
Captain Lacie, the celebrated Australian writer,
and author of “ Tales of Outlaws,” “ Narratives of
the Bushranging Times,” “ Convict Records and
Chronicles in Van Dieman’s Land and Norfolk
Island,” “The Gem Finders,” &c., &c., is a born
traveller. He has explored most parts of Aus-
tralasia and visited nearly all foreign countries.
Most of his writings are well-known in the colonies,
and the following, in his usual concise, graphic
style, will interest our readers : “In preparing my
series of ‘ Bushranging Narratives ’ and ‘ Tales
of Outlaws,’ I underwent great physical hardships
in order that I might describe from personal ob-
servation the chief haunts of noted desperadoes of
the past. This was particularly so on the wild,
and at that time practically unexplored, west
coast of Tasmania, round the Macquarie Harbour
district; in the frightful glens sacred to the ‘ bauri
vine,’ and the tremendous ranges around the
Frenchman’s Cap and Cradle Mountain, I endured
great exposure, which debilitated and ran down
my system. Sleeping in wet clothes in the bush,
with unsuitable and uncertain food, brought on
severe kidney and liver troubles, tending to chronic
rheumatism. Having had former beneficial ex-
perience of the effects of Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure, I
took a course of that medicine for a few months,
and I am pleased to say it has entirely restored
me to my former robust health. ”

An outlay of £30,000 on additions and
alterations to Menzies’ Hotel has brought that
venerable hostelry more than up to date. The
building is now as big as its reputation, and the
beauty of the interior will be a joy for ever to
some of Menzies’ oldest adherents, whose souls are
generally understood to go there when they die.
It is the squatters’ heaven, the globe-trotters’
delight. A winter garden attached to the Moorish
hall, where small parties may feed alfresco during
the summer months, is a notable new feature.
Phil Goatcher, the scenic artist, was engaged to
re-decorate the main hall, and the ceiling is a
dream of fair nymphs tempting the general public
to walk right in and buy a drink at the bar. Good
liquor and good management have been the secrets
of Menzies’ success. The old place stood still and
said nothing whilst vast coffee palaces were spring-
ing up to the glory of temperance principles, but
to-day the palaces bewail their awful failure with
a loud, snuffling voice, and the hotel is going
ahead as though boom-time had returned.

Some big druggists have lately suffered
from a novelty in sneak-thieving. A well-dressed
man calls on a leading doctor and asks for his
card as “a patient wishes to write to him from
,” an adjoining town or suburb. The card
is then presented at a neighboring druggist’s a
little before the time of changing assistants, and
a couple of syphons of mineral water got on the
strength thereof. An hour later, when the new
assistant is behind the counter, the syphons are
returned empty, and the usual deposit thereon is
handed over by the unsuspecting counter-hand
when asked for. As no deposit was paid at first,
this is all “ bunce.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
There can be no question as to the inferior
quality of the older horses running in this year’s Cup.
Season after season the best three-year-old form is shown,
with rare exceptions, in the V.R.C. Derby, and these
Derby horses, as a rule, represent “ class ” thereafter.
Take the notable Derby horses of 1893, ’94, ’9s—who would
now be the representative four, five, and six-year-olds, if
they were racing. Carnage, Sailor Prince, Pounamu,
Patron, The Harvester, Malachite, Bonnie Scotland, Wal-
lace, Osculator, Auraria, and Waterfall. Where are they
now? Not one of them ran in the Cup. The Cup fields
this year and last were remarkable for not including one
classic performer of note, and the result was the same in
both cases. Two out of the four placed horses were
youngsters, finishing first and fourth respectively on
either occasion—Auraria and Waterfall, Newhaven and
Resolute.
* * * *
The Y.R.C. stewards must be feeling rather
sore re their enquiry into the running of Broken Hill on
Derby Day. Mr. David James’ horse ran nowhere in the
Hotham Handicap, likewise The Skipper. Three days
later The Skipper shows vastly improved form in Mel-
bourne Cup, whereas Broken Hill runs just as moderately
as before. David James may well complain that the local
authorities ought to spare some of their suspicions for the
running of local horses. There was at least one glaringly
apparent stiff-’un started for the Yan Yean Stakes on Cup
Day. Why didn’t the stewards hold an enquiry into that
case ?
* * * *
Another sample of the stuff that newspapers give
to punters nowaday's. From Melb. Herald : —“ The
wagering on the Melbourne Cup was very brisk this after-
noon when the card was called at the Victorian Club.
Preston was supported to win £5OO at 100 to 2, but subse-
quently 100 to I t was offered without finding takers,” &c.,
&e. An inch of betting-market news is devoted to the
exciting fact that somebody put a “ tenner” on Preston,
after which Ikey Mo made an abortive attempt to get a
further 30 “ bob ” out of the horse’s dashing admirer !
Should Hopscotch not be sent to Hingland, we
shall have him carrying the coin of fly-flat punters in next
Melbourne Cup. Experience teaches nothing to such
Machiavellians. They talked in dark mysterious whispers
of the “cunning crowd” associated with Long’s cham-
pion, as though “Bill Long” and “ Jimmy Hayes” were
Hopscotch, and the four legs of the owner and jockey
were capable of winning in a canter whenever “the
money was on.” In short, they reckoned without the
’orse, who has always performed in big handicaps like a
thoroughly honest non-stayer.
* ♦ » »
When the vice-regal lady happens to be a New
Woman, she will undoubtedly agitate to be emancipated
from the senseless task of placing a blue ribbon round the
neck of the winner of a horserace. It is not an edifying
spectacle to see a five-foot woman trying to lasso an un-
ruly six-foot quadruped ; in terror, all the time, of having
her dress damaged, her gloves dirtied, and her feet
trodden upon by the playful beast, which doesn’t appre-
ciate the honor, and only wants to lash out among
the crowd. The owner wants to get his horse away to
be rubbed down, and the crowd don’t care, and the
operator doesn’t like the job, so it’s about time such fool-
ishness was abolished.
* * » *
A handsome male animal down on his luck
always appeals to the sympathy' of lovely woman. After
the Cup, whilst Resolute was being rubbed down by a
couple of stable hands, and nobody else was troubling
about him, Miss Wilson came along to cuddle his head
and commend the poor dear old boy for having done his
best under the circumstances.
» * * »
On Wednesday before Vic. Derby Day a cor-
poration laborer dropped down dead in the street from
sunstroke, and on the Friday and Saturday following the
shopkeepers couldn’t find overcoats and waterproofs
enough to supply the demand.
* * * *
The luck of N.S.W. investors in Adams’ Melb.
Cup consultations this year has been remarkable. There
were 5 sweeps, the first prize in every instance going to
N.S.W. Out of a total of 15 “big money” prizes (i.e.,
Ist, 2nd, and 3rd horses) 8 went to N.S.W ; 3 to Vic. ; 2 to
W.A ;1 to Tas ; 2to Maoriland. Queensland and S. Aus.
apparently “ weren’t having any” this time.
* * * »
Rumored that Tas. Govt, intends, after the end
of the year, to enact that local applications for Adams’
tickets must be made through the Post Office. If true,
this is snaring revenue with a vengeance.
* * * *
Newhaven’s most enthusiastic worshippers,
previous to the Derb.v and Cup, were N.S.W. visitors who
saw him win his races at Randwick, but missed seeing
him get beaten in the Caulfield Guineas. The chestnut
colt had not so deeply impressed the Victorians. His
half-head victory over Excel, with Nada close up, and his
easy defeat by Officer, were what the local race-goers had
seen, so it was not until Newhaven ran away with the
Derby that they could regard him with awe. Then they
fancied him for the Cup, if they were reasonable mortals,
and took Resolute (meeting him at an advantage of 151 b.)
to be his most dangerous opponent. The books, how-
ever, fielded strongly against Resolute on account of the
big fellow’s shockingly bad feet, which, by the way, are
not likely to stand much more ’ammer, ’ammer, ’ammer
on the ’ard ’igh road to distinction.
* * * *
Newhaven better than Carbine, is he? Wait
until he has given, as Carbine did, 531 b. and a four-lengths
beating to another Highborn, a horse that subsequently
won the Sydney and Australian Cups, carrying more than
9st. each time.
* * * *
The effect of Newhaven’s popular Derby and
Cup wins was visible in the big attendance at Flemington
on Oaks day. Although the weather was not specially
inviting, grand-stand receipts were higher than for two or
three previous years.
Thunder Queen for the Oaks, and Hopscotch
for the Flying Stakes, were two of the best things “ on
paper ” at the V.R.C. meeting, nevertheless, the “ books ”
fie.ded against both as though they thought they knew
something. Results proved that Ikey, for once, knew
nothing.
Last year Hopscotch was beaten at the finish of
the Flying Stakes by' Hova, Wallace, and Mostyn. The
cattle that met Hopscotch on Thursday didn’t include
any sturdy fliers of the Hova and Wallace class, and going
away with the lead, exactly as he did last year, the big
chestnut was able to keep it to the end.
Thunder Queen’s gallop in the Oaks was ljsec.
faster than the previous record, and sJsec. faster than
Auraria’s time.
The performances of Bloodshot and O’Trigger at
Flemington explain why The Bulletin had a good deal to
say about these stable companions before they had
started to distinguish themselves.
Nada broke down in the Melb. Cup race and
may' not run again.
Chris. Moore’s finish on Hymettus, in the two-
year-old race won by Hypnotist, was just what is expected
from a jockey' who is getting home comfortably' with
about a stone in hand. Whilst Holmes, on Hypnotist, was
riding vigorously, Moore sat upright on the other horse,
although he was only half a length, or so, behind. There
seem to be two versions of the explanation which Moore
gave to the V.R.C. stewards, who accepted it as satisfac-
tory. According to one version, the jockey thought he
was winning, and therefore didn’t bestir himself. The
other story states that he didn’t think Hymettus would
make another effort if called upon.
* * * *
The most “ tony ” dog-cart, at Flemington
races, held three Chinamen, with a European groom.
* * * *
The C. B. Fisher Plate, in the V.R.C. meeting,
just over, was a reminder of a great sportsman, now on
his financial beam-ends, and a worthy commencement has
been made with a subscription—Mr. Falkiner heads the
list with £5OO. There never was such a big dive made on
the Victorian turf as with the Maribyrnongstables, which,
however, were more the work of Hurtle Fisher than C.B.
The wiser, but not wise enough, C.B. altered the stock
from racers into Merinos, Shorthorns, and Clydesdales.
* * * »
The Amiable managers were “sent up” for a
year at Rosehill on Saturday, over the mare’s barefaced
running in the Shorts The Rosehill proprietary'deserve
well at the hands of the racing public for their endeavors
to protect the interests of their patrons, the idiots who
find the sinews of war to keep the ball rolling. Randwick
and the other clubs do sometimes make an example—of
some poor devil of a countryman, but it remains for Rose-
hill and Kensington to tackle the influential party.
* * * *
Ted Power’s stable scored three good wins at
Flemington with O’Trigger, Bloodshot, and Homespun.
The last-named animal seems to have a talent for coming
with a rattle at the end of a meeting. He won the Fare-
well Handicap at V.R.C. Autumn, and the Final Handicap
last Saturday, the boy Callinan bringing him home neatly
on each occasion.
Merry Boy, who was one of The Bulletin’s
fancies for a place in the Cup, managed to capture the
V.R.C. Handicap (1J miles) in pretty good style. Cook’s
sturdy horse, although only a second-rater, will always be
worth considering for long-distance races when the han-
dicapper treats him kindly. Over hurdles he might prove
equal to the best of them.
Hopscotch was backed freely for the 2 miles
C. B. Fisher Plate, on the chance that the pace, with
only three horses running, might not be strong enough
to find out his weak spot. However, Tir6 and Bloodshot
made satisfactory time (3min. 35jsec.), and the big mare
was beaten badly. Are the “ fly-flats” quite satisfied?
Of two-year-olds that didn’t win at Y.R.C. meet-
ing, the handsome Patriot is most likely to distinguish
himself anon.
The gate-receipts at Flemington this time were
the biggest for five y'ears, the increase in grand-stand
attendance being explained by an exceptional gathering
of South Australians and the new rush of sports from the
boomsome West. No doubt the prestige of the big event,
which suffered from the successive victories of two com-
parative “ rotters ” in Glenloth and Tarcoola, had been
much restored by Patron and Auraria, and now Newhaven’s
brilliant win has put the Cup back upon the pedestal
where Carbine placed it in ’9O. It would pay the V.R.C.
to engage Newhaven, at a high salary, to appiar again
next Spring.
The two dining-saloons under the Flemington
Stand—one for the vice-regal “ set” and the other for the
high priests of Horse, local and visiting are ele-
gant retreats. The stewards’ room, entered through a
fernery, is perhaps the more poetical feeding-place, whilst
the Governor’s is somewhat stronger in expensive details,
but a hungry aesthete could be equally happy in either of
tfiem. The lilies and languors of decoration around the
walls make an appetising contrast to the roses and rap-
tures of cold tuckgy, &c., on the festive boards. And the
festive boards, by thS way, don’t seem to be spread for
more people than the Club is reasonably justified in
lunching gratis.
* * • *
Thrice only has the Melb. Cup been won by a
chestnut. Glencoe in ’6B, Patron in ’94, and Newhaven
are the trio.
* * * *
Newhaven’s pedigree is a bit of a facer for the
scientific breeding enthusiasts, as, if he races at ’Ome, the
fatal letters (h.b.) will be subjoined to his name on
account of the breeding of his great-grandfather’s dam
being unknown. If the gallant chestnut goes on all right
he will, however, be able to hump the noxious adjunct
and yet win and beget winners in due season.
* * * *
“ The Australians,” writes a travelling Bulletin
correspondent, “ had rather a rough passage from
Southampton to New York. One of the funniest inci-
dents of the trip across occurred upon the first day out,
when most of the team were seated in the smoke-room.
The great bulk of the passengers were Americans return-
ing home, and one Vic. batsman, who had had 1 quite
enough ’—which remark applies to most travellers on the
day of embarkation—got into a heated argument with 6ft.
of solid muscle (a young Bostonian) upon the question of
supremacy between an Englishman and an American.
The young American, declaring that a ‘ God-damned
Yank ’ was as good as an Englishman any day, stood up
and began taking his coat off to fight, whereupon a friend
of his interposed : ‘ For God’s sake, don’t fight him ’ —
meaning the batsman— 1 he’s a trained athlete.’ That
was enough : the Bostonian at once got his coat on again,
and he and the Gumsucker soon afterwards shook hands.
Considering that since the batsman’s attack of rheumatiz
his comrades have referred to him as the 1 old crock,’
the joke will stick—he will probably be known as 1 the
trained athlete ’ for many a day to come.
“ On nearing New York it was announced in
the saloon that a sailor had been caught stealing from
trunks in the hold. For a lark, the boys began chaffing
Joe Darling, telling him that his baggage had been
broken into —which, queerlv enough, proved later to be
quite true. When caught the sailor had on a brand-new
suit of Darling’s clothes, an Australian XI. tie, and a ring
belonging to Darling. On landing at New York the ship-
ping co. fully recompensed Darling for the damage done,
and” the sailor now stands committed for trial.
“Any needed further illustration of Harry
Musgrove’s business capacity was furnished in the final
stages of the tour. On Saturday, sth Sept., at 5.15 p.m.,
the last ball of the tour was delivered at Hastings, and on
the following day (Sunday), at the Tavistock Hotel, Lon-
don, where the team had returned the previous evening,
Mr. Musgrove had prepared and presented to each man a
full and complete statement of the whole trip showing
receipts and expenditure, with vouchers for each item ;
and with it each man's cheque in full payment of his
share. The older members of the team were completely
astounded at such expedition, some of them remarking
that more than one former manager had not yet furnished
statements in full, although years had elapsed. The
natty little manager has left an unprecedented good
impression amongst the cricket authorities at ’Ome, and
while his services are available no one else will personally
conduct Australian Elevens in England.
“ The Americans have shown every considera-
tion to the Australians and their womenkind ; in the
way of hospitality' they far exceed the Englanders.”
* * * *
The second day of N.S.W. Wheelmen League’s
Summer Carnival turned out a distinct success from a
racing standpoint. The fact that no more than 8000 paid
at the gates proves that the Sydney public is thoroughly
aware that the League is unable at present to accommo-
date spectators. The League cannot hope to score big
financial successes when thousands of people are forced
to stand all the afternoon or else sit down in the dust;
nor will a long-suffering public rush to pay double money
for a grand-stand and reserve which are so unfortunately
situated that they give the worst possible view of the
racing.
Carpenter, in his heat of the Sydney Wheel
Race, bulloeked himself into third place with splendid
gameness ; and his riding was a big contrast to that of
O’Brien, who always w'aits for somebody to do the pacing
for him.
Cartwright, the ex-ped., considered by his stable
to be a dead-bird for the League Cup (half-mile), started
his final sprint with the bell’s ringing, but was out of it
half a lap from home. Fallick (his racing-name is F.
Gregory), an old interprovincial footballer, won his race
(£2O) with a long, determined spurt, and M‘Combe was
third. What a desperate struggle he made to get up !
Don Walker made even his admirers stare last
Saturday. In his heat of the Sydney Wheel Race (two
miles) he and M‘Lean paced one another lap and lap up to
the field ; and Walker, just before the last lap, went past
his field on the outside like a flash, and after leading for
700yds. was beaten only on the post by little Congdon.
Likewise, in the final, Walker paced Kuhle up in the third
lap, in almost Bilmartin style.
The Five-mile Championship of Australasia,
with its £25 first prize and gold medal, was a plum for
which Ken Lewis and Parsons reserved themselves.
Lewis, during the week, had had a bad fall on the track,
cutting his knees and considerably incapacitating him-
self ; and Parsons, who is much out of form, had been
with Johnny Coleman to Coogee in the morning. Parsons
expected, if the race were slow, to have a chance in his
finishing sprint with the strong wind behind his back
down the straight. The race, however, was a succession
of exhausting spurts, and the final struggle began at the
very beginning of the long- last lap. Now Pither, now
Walne, led, but Parsons, riding with splendid judgment,
watched Lewis and waited on his wheel. Half-a-lap from
home Lewis wetit to the front, and Parsons jumping
instantly', the pair shot out with a terrible spurt, which
left the others standing. For 50 yards Parsons promised
to give Lewis the race of his life, but, entering the straight
Lewis picked up his machine in brilliant fashion, broke
away from Parsons and won by several lengths. The
moment the winner flew past the post he sat up, swerved
from side to side, and after going a little way fell in a
faint. Parsons, though sick, too, was able to walk in and
raise some sort of a smile.
W. J. C. Elliott, off 195yds., won his heat of
Sydney Wheel Race as he liked, but was walloped out in
the final through want of condition. He is training for
the Austral —the great Melb. cycling event—for which he
has been favorite several times. His chance, like every
other man’s chance, will depend in great measure on the
way the race is run. As a foot-runner he was almost an
even-timer, and it is to his quickness with his legs that he
owes his cycling pace. He is a nerveful, clever rider, and
the best old-timer to carry a man s money in the Austral.
The best new man to back is either Clinton or Matthews.
Matthews, trainer Lee’sprot6g6, is on 250yds., and, as Lee
recently imported a special racer for his man, and also
scratched him for all recent Sydney engagements, the in-
ference is that Lee sends Matthews this time for certain.
Elliott gives Matthews 100yds., and, should there be a
good pacer in front of Elliott to bring him up, Matthews
goes down in finishing. There are not many who can
sprint with Elliott. j
Walne’s riding was a treat. He, Meadham, and
M'Combe paced each other in their heat of the Sydney
Wheel Race, and all got paced. In the final, the back
men, Walker, Kuhle, Meadham, Walne, and M'Combe,
brought each other quickly up to the,leaders. Beginning
the last lap, Walne shot out, and, sustaining a splendid
run all the way, came home two lengths in front of Kuhle.
Being a bridge-carpenter, Walne is always in hardy con-
dition, and it was purely condition that enabled him to
snatch this £ 100 prize.
Dunlop tyres added another win to their endless
list when Ken Lewis secured the Five-mile Championship.
Dunlop camps, lasting a fortnight each, will shortly be
started on Australian cycling grounds, and all the best
riders will strive to lower the world’s records.
Plugger Davison nipped the £7 first pacing
prize in the Five-mile Aus. championship. Davison has
been unwell lately, suffering from rheumatism.
Last Saturday was a day of numerous and
bunched fields, yet there was not one fall. In the last
race (the Open Handicap) 44 started, and though accident
was always imminent, all came through safely. Parti-
cularly Harris, who won the first prize of £l5.
G. Meadham will have to husband his strength
if he wants to win another £lOO prize. He would have
had a good show in the Sydney YVheel Race, had he not
ridden the triplet just before against time. The triplet,
steered by Elliott, did a gruelling go against the wind,
and rattled off the mile in 1.57 dead —Parsons’ time.
* * * *
Bilmartin goes to Westralia to race until his
disqualification period closes.
Plaeke, the big fellow who just pulled round
after his rough-and-tumble with Queensland giant Barker,
has posted £lOO with a London paper for a match with
Jem Smith. Bad as Smith is, he is good enough to
slaughter the fanatic Plaeke.
* * * *
Cavanagh, about the best, of the Lillie Bridge
riders, came to grief at last, and was buried last week.
Next Monday night the proprietary give his widow a
benefit meeting, consisting of popy racing and trotting.
A. G. Butler, a Sydney boy and med. at St.
Mary’s (London) Hospital, took the 1000yds. Challenge
Cup from the holder at the London athletic club sports.
The time was only 2 3-ssec. worse than the professional
record.
* * *
Boidi, the Italian —a faster man than Bilmartin,
probably—reckons that he has no chance of winning the
Austral Wheel Race from scratch. .
* » » »
Towns, who lately beat Neilsen in besUand-best
boats, went down before the old’un in the sculling handi-
cap, in light skiffs, at Hexham, N.S.W., last Saturday.
Neilsen 401 b., Towns 451 b., and Peter Kemp 301 b. were in
the final heat. Towns gave up, and Neilsen won from
Peter Kemp by a length. At Balmain regatta, in All-
Comers' Race (22ft. boat), Towns (40sec. behind) beat,
Neilsen by two lengths, but in best-and-best bpats Neilsen
(55sec. behind) turned the tables, and beat both Towns
(60sec. behind) and Kemp (53sec. behind). Towns' style
pleased the critics, and a big future is predicted for him if
the rowing revival continues.
* * * *
Corbett and Fitzsimmons are not likely to meet
in England, as the National Club..adheres to its resolve
not to allow Corbett to fight at its rooms. Corbett says
he won’t fight privately, to which Fitz has a decided ob-
jection, as he “ is out for the stuff.” '\
*** * "
After each side had scored one easy win, the
final of the Vic.-N.S.W. bowling contests was won by 98
to 94 points by the Victorians, who were in the rear up to
the last couple of ends. The rubber was practically won
by a magnificent shot by the lanky Scotchman Munro,
the hero of all three games. As bowlers go, both sides
were trained a trifle fine, and the games were marked by
a remarkable absence of the adjournments for refresh-
ments iioually inseparable from the ancient game of
bowls.
* * * »
There promises to be a deadlock between the
SA. and N.S.W. cricket authorities. S.A.C.A. is deter-
mined not to play N.S.W. on Dec. 18 in the absence of
Giffen, Hill, Darling, and Jones, and as N.S.W. has
already refused to give way, there will be trouble. There
is a great deal in the argument of the younger province
that she cannot be expected to find a team outside the
four named.
* » * *
The League of American Wheelmen confines its
membership to “ white cyclists over the age of 18 years.”
The Australian Leagues are lesS punctilious regarding the
color line, and open their ranks freely to all, irrespective
of creed or nation, no rule even existing to debar Ah
Geap, the Chow champion, should'he feel inclined to join.
So far, however, the only man of color in any of the Aus-
tralian organisations is Dicky Darrell, the West Indian
ped and trainer. Dicky is the darky of the abnormal legs
who flits about the arena at all Melb. cycling meetings,
and who, on one occasion, started in a race himself in a
moment of mental aberration.

The Dunlop Pneumatic Tyrw*Go. advertises that
the American Dunlop tyres being unsuitable for Austra-
lian roads will not be guaranteed by them. As our roads
are superior to the American, can it be that a good tyre
for a bad road is a bad tyre for a good road ?
* * * •
George Bailey, who was a member of the first
Australian eleven, has just been appointed Diocesan
secretary to the Hobart Anglican Synod. He has been an
officer of the Union Bank for 25 years, and should fill his
new post with credit. Church and cricket both flourish
on “ gate.”

Curran’s patent safety pocket is the age’s
triumph and the sneak-thief’s despair. It intro-
duces a simple little spring into the pocket-mouth
which effectually prevents the introduction of the
wrong hand, and guards , against accidental loss,
besides having numerous other recommendations.
As applied to a bridegroom’s vest-pocket, the
new invention is calculated to save much anxiety
about the ring, and it consequently claims public
patronage as an aid to morality and matrimony and
Coghlan’s statistics.*

mining venture is the best spec, for small investors;
provided the whole of the capital be expended
solely on the mine, and does not find its way, as so
frequently happens, into the shark’s maw. Par-
ticulars of such a venture, in connection with an
already-proved-payable large reef in New South
Wales, will be forwarded to anyone applying. No
calls, no forfeiture, no liability. Address: “Pros-

ydney.*

Child, of Oxford, says—“ There were certain

and smallpox, and these might be eradicated with
ordinary sanitary care and precaution.” All
should read a large illustrated sheet given with
each bottle of Eno’s “ Fkuit Salt.” The infor-
mation is invaluable. Eno’s “ Fkuit Salt ” keeps
the blood pure, and prevents disease and pre-
mature death. Sold by all chemists and stores.*
The Society of Artists’ art-union consists
of 100 prizes, value £lOOO Tickets Is., obtain-
able from leading booksellers, newsagents, &c.,
throughout Australasia. Exhibition opens in
Temperance Hall next Monday night, with draw-
ing room entertainment by the clever John D.

art union ticket. The admission on other days is

‘ ‘ Denticura, ” the invention of Rev. Rior-
dan, of Bathurst, N.S.W., is Australia’s con-
tribution to the great horde of toothache remedies,
and it is claimed that Denticura will murder an
achiiig molar in less time than any other instru-
ment or drug extant, bar the dentist’s forceps or
front-step. It is composed of two metal plates
which are clasped over an erring tooth, and forth-
with set up a current of electricity that turns the
pain into a dreamy kind of thrill like that of the
kiss of a plump 16-year-old blonde with a breath
of violets and honey. For the tooth of the other
sex, the other kind of ecstasy is supplied, fitted
with a waxed moustache like Judge Madden’s.
And a big sale is confidently anticipated for
Denticura.*
Prof. Kirk, of M.L., lecturing, the other
day, on the forests of Maoriland, said the kauri
was a wonderful tree. There were kauris standing
in the forests to-day which must be at least 4000
years old—trees which were growing when Abra-
ham dwelt in Caanan.

When, asks a Bulletin correspondent, is Aus-
tralia going’to have a general “Australian Herd Book”
corresponding to the “British Herd Book”? British
shorthorns are degenerating, and stud sires of any value
are becoming scarcer year by year. British stock-owners
are, therefore, contemplating importations of stud
animals from Australia, but are restrained by the absence
of an official pedigree record. There is a stud-book for
blood-racers, and that is why so many officially-recognised
pedigree horses have of late gone to England and else-
where. Official records are the only guarantee the non-
Australian resident can be decently expected t<? go by,
and their non-existence means a tremendous annual loss
to Australia.

Orange-colored flannel shirts have been
found the best attire for tropical countries, as
yellow is a better protection against the sun than
even white. But it wouldn’t be over-safe to wear
them at Coolgardie or the Towers on “ Patrick’s
day ” or the Glorious Twelfth.

The proprietor of a Melb. seaside bathing-
place relates a touching tale of gratitude. A visi-
tor to his baths, unable to swim, had dived into
10ft. of water and, not unnaturally, disappeared
from view after a few seconds of gurgling protest.
The cries of the other bathers brought the owner
on to the spot, and, after three long dives, he
in imaged to raise the apparently drowned to the
surface—the rescuer himself being by this time
utterly exhausted and bleeding at the ears. After
a few minutes’ rest, however, he set to work, and
in an hour had restored the man to life. In another
hour the rescued, having been warmed up with
whisky and hot bricks, was sufficiently recovered
to ask for his saviour, and, upon the latter appear-
ing, he turned his head round the corner of the cab
into which he had been lifted and observed impres-
sively : “ I’ll never come into your blanky baths
again ! ” Another well-to-do man, who had fought
desperately before being baled out, thanked his
rescuer effusively and handed him sixpence. He
wasn’t a Governor either.
A celebrated London surgeon lately re-
moved from a woman a tumour so hard that a
knife would not cut it. It was taken away by
dissecting out the cellular tissue to which it was
attached. When sawn open after removal, it was
found to consist of a hard shell of calcareous mat-
ter, inside which was closely packed a full-grown
doubled-up baby in such excellent preservation
that even the brain was intact and in the softness
of life. Twenty-two years ago the mother entered
Guy’s Hospital, telling the surgeons a history of
pregnancy which should have resulted in a birth
three months previously. Extra-uterine foetation
was diagnosed, and as the woman was not suffer-
ing from anything beyond inconvenience, it was
decided not to interfere. Recently the tumour
began to enlarge greatly and painfully, and opera-
tion was resorted to. That child had actually lain
for 22 years in that limy shell like a dead chick in
an eggshell.
One of the best results of Protection in
Melbourne is the umbrella-making industry, which
at the present time wouldn’t need protecting at
all, but for the prejudice of certain Anglomamacal
shopkeepers in favor of imported brollies. The
better class article can be made up as cheaply in
Melbourne as it can be indented from ’Ome, never-
theless a few old-fashioned tailors, and haber-
dashers, here and there, continue to import (from
dealers, not manufacturers) and pay a customs tax
upon umbrellas identical in every respect —stick,
frame, and silk cover—with those that are pro-
curable from the local Hartnell and Co., and other
firms, on much better terms. The big buyers, of
course, now go to the Melbourne maker for all
their “good stuff,” whilst continuing to import
the cheap rubbishy products of sweated London
labor. With these the local factories can’t com-
pete, nor do they want to be able to. The um-
brella trade in Melbourne supports a small army
of girls who earn a decent living, and look quite
smart on it.

Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
• March 7th, 1895.
To Here Rassmussen,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight da 's
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Eriday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

A GEUEEAL’S DAUGHTER.
CURED OE RHEUMATIC AND NEUR
ALGIC PAINS, ANAEMIA, NERVOUS
HEADACHE, AND GENERAL WEAK-
NESS BY DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK
PILLS.
The Hampshire Independent sent a special
commissioner to ascertain the facts in the follow-
ing case. His report was as follows : “ I received
instructions to interview Lieutenant General
Clement R. Shaw, now living with his family, in
honorable retirement, at St. Paul’s Vicarage,
Shanklin, Isle of Wight. I explained to the
General that I "was anxious to obtain some per-
sonal explanation as to the narrow escape I heard
one of his daughters had recently experienced.
At that he brightened visibly. ‘ You must know,’
he said, ‘l’m just a bit of an enthusiast on this
point, but the tale is very short. Nearly two
years ago my daughter—-the one you referred to —
came home from India, and when I joined her in
London I found her ill in bed. She had rheumatic
and neuralgic pains ; she was perfectly bloodless,
listless, and in a generally weak and prostrate
condition. We called in a doctor, and lam bound
to say that he succeeded in getting her out of bed ;
but she was still absolutely colorless, was in great
wretchedness, and suffering from what I should
call anaemia or bloodlessness. She had a kind of
fever, nervous headache, and other pains. We
went to Bembi’idge, in the Isle of Wight, and
there I first heard of Dr. Williams’ Pink-Pills for
pale people. Indeed, I was recommended to try
them, and I did so. My daughter took the first
bottle, and they had a marvellous effect upon her.
She regained her color, she lost her pains, and be-
came altogether different; and after she had been
taking them some time, she came in one day after
her walk, and said to me, “ Why, father, I feel
quite a glow through me. ” She went on taking
the pills, and I am glad to tell you that she re-
covered completely. I have had a great deal to
do with medical men in this country and in India,
and they laugh at the idea that patent medicines
cure any disease. But I have given you this case,
and I know my daughter’s recovery was due to
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, without
a doubt. Their effects were marvellous, and Ido
not speak without first ascertaining the fact.’'’
This wonderful remedy is offered to the public
with a full confidence that there is no disease
arising from a watery or impoverished condition
of the blood, or shattered nervous forces, which it
will not cure if the directions are faithfully fol-
lowed and the treatment persisted in. Dr.

DENTAL ART.
There are, perhaps, few cities in the world where
scientific dentistry has attained to such proficiency
as in Sydney. Anyone interested is invited to
inspect the establishment of Mr. J. I. Marshall,
28 Wynyard Square, Sydney. There, in conve-
nient proximity to the well-appointed waiting-
rooms and surgeries, is a laboratory replete with
all the latest appliances required for supplying
artificial teeth of the highest order of merit. Mr.
Marshall is always pleased to show these speci-
mens of the dental craft to enquirers.

EASILY
CURED
S.M. Herald, apropos the Dederer com-
mutation, says that the execution of youths is
“ not unknown ” in Australian history. Not un-
known? Why, the very first execution the his-
torian comes upon is that of James Barret, a
youth of 17, who was hanged in Sydney for rob-
bery in 1788. ' ' ■
N. : “In Melb. I notice good houses being
built, right in the thick of the mushroom deserts
of empty, rubbishy, building-society houses that
cannot be sold or let. And these new places let
freely, too. Meanwhile, the house mullock of the
Permanent Evanescent Building Society is being
hitched up to decorate Bungaree and Bungeeltap.”
Ibsen is said to be a very provincial-minded
snob. He goes every day to the Grand Hotel in
Christiania to be gazed at whilst he strikes a
series of high-art atti udes. His recalcitrant hair
becomes, by comparison, more aggressive under a
carefully poised and polished silk-hat, and he
carries a small mirror to assist him in playing vari-
ations upon his whiskers with a pocket-comb.
At a recent meeting of Kyneton (Vic.) Agri-
cultural Society, a valiant champion of the magpie
stated that “he had often seen mice thrown into
the air and dexterously caught before reaching the
ground by the magpies, who gobbled them up in
very quick time.” The trail of the American
agricultural anecdote is over this statement.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the Well-known jeweller of 175
Ehzabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Mr. W. Collins, who was commissioned to
engage meat-preserving, canning and salting ex-
perts in America for one of the Q meat companies,
remarks to Sydney D.T. :
“ ‘ Australians have been doing very badly in the meat
trade compared with the Americans. Here we have
better meat, but the difference in price is tremendous,
and all because we do not put it up properly. Cattle in'
Chicago yards are worth a shade over three times as much
a,s they are in Q. markets ; i.e. , cattle of an equal quality.
Our idea is to make money in the industry where now
there is none. One of the great pulls which the trade has
m America arises from the fact that although their cattle
cost three times as much as ours, they have a good market
for the best cuts of fresh meat. The prime joints are
sent as far as New York, 100 J miles from Chicago, and
fetch good prices. If the whole of the meat went into
the cans they could not, afford to pay so much for the
cattle.’ . Mr. Collins, is confident that very much better
meat will be put up in Q. when the men he has engaged
get to work. His principal man was in charge of Messrs.
Nelson Morris’-cannery, Chicago, where from 2000 to 3000
cattle are killed daily.”
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.*
Apparently, the “Pinafore” variety of
British sailor is real, after all—at least, Melbourne
Age reports having discovered him. According
to its statement, a wildly-patriotic tar belonging
to the bloated ironclad Katoomba invaded a Mel-
bourne pub. and proceeded to sing, in a dreadful
voice, the “ Death of Nelson.” At the end of the
first verse, he suddenly grew suspicious of a small
man who was doing nothing in particular at the
other end of the bar, and asked him, truculently,
what he thought of Nelson. The man was
French, and began to reply in badly damaged
English that he had no objection to Nelson, but
when the briny one realised, by his accent, that he
wasn't a true Briton, he promptly knocked him
insensible. Then he indulged in copious blas-
phemy, and “ stoushed ” two Turks, who tried to
interfere on the partially-deceased Frenchman’s
behalf, and was just proceeding to pick up his
fallen enemies and batter their heads against the
floor when a right-thinking person struck him on
the ear with a lemonade bottle. And, instead of
six months’ hard labor, the comic rowdy was fined
ss. This public-house row is the nearest thing to
active service that Australia’s subsidised fleet has
yet accomplished, and the ss. fine is the first divi-
dend this country has got in return for an expendi-
ture of something like a million pounds.

An Artistic Soul.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The opera-company broke up in San Fran-
cisco, and Antonio, the tenor, borrowed his way
out to the furthest mud-bank on Poverty Point
in less than a month. He had been down to four-
penny meals—and less than three per day—for
over a fortnight. It could not be considered
luxurious.
Redemption sometimes comes by way of good
looks,, and Antonio was dark and very handsome.
Some women loved his beauty and would have re-
deemed him. Antonio loved many women, and
consequently was not redeemed. No woman
willingly buys expensive feathers to decorate her
rivals.
Antonio knew much of books, and in the Public
Library, where he sat to read and to save shoe-
leather, he met a Professor who was also a Man,
kind-hearted and intermittently a drunkard. He
was married, and not too happily. Lecturing
when sober, and stray bits of journalism at other
times, enabled the Professor to keep a roof over
his wife’s head and a hat over his own.
Antonio was invited to share the humble home,
and was temporarily redeemed through the par-
tiality of the Professor for educated foreigners.
One day the Man came home unexpectedly and
saw the passionate face of his handsome guest
bending close, very close, over his unwilling wife.
Returning to the city he bought a revolver and
several drinks. Speeding home again per sub-
urban train he got fooling nervously with the
weapon, and it went off. A passenger was shot.
An arrest, a sobering-up, and a trial. The Pro-
fessor hopelessly swore he bought the revolver to
shoot a dog that disturbed his rest o’ nights ; the
wounded man and fellow-passengers swore it was
quite an accident, and a nominal fine was imposed.
* * * *
A newspaper brevity of a few days later :
This morning l the Italian opera-singer, Antonio, at-
tempted suicide at ’s restaurant by shooting himself
in the mouth. The hospital surgeons say the wound is
not likely to cause death, but Signor Antonio will not be
able to sing again.
Two years later a tall, dark foreigner and
a very pretty, fair-haired young woman paced a
windy wharf at Wellington, Maoriland. A
variety-show had just collapsed. As the couple
walked up and down in the gathering night they
used bitter words to each other.
Antonio . was protesting that he had only run
back upstairs at the lodging-house to get a glove
he had left in the room. At which his wife
sneered : “Yes, and left me standing in the hall
while you made love to the chamber-maid.”
Antonio drew, close to the woman and hissed :
Damn you ! ” in very good English.
The woman laughed.
“You ought to curse yourself, ” she said, “you
coward ! ” J
“I am not coward,” blustered Antonio.
Tauntingly the woman said : “ You are ! You
sought to bite the kind hand that fed you in San
n rancisco, and feared vengeance so much that you
tried to shoot yourself. Your cowardice spoilt
your aim.”
Further recriminations, some tears • then some
kisses in the shadowy night at the end of the
windy wharf, and the couple sauntered lovingly
aboard the steamer.
* * * *
Wanderings in many lands ensued, but the
wanderings led ever towards Poverty Point.
Variety-shows innumerable started on tour with
Antonio as manager and Madame as star. Some-
times hotelkeepers sent w r ord on ahead warning
trade-friends to look out for the Antonio crowd.
At other times the star would go on strike because
Antonio made violent love to one or other of the
lady dancers. Either of these things generally
broke the combination into dishevelled sections.
* » * *
A year or two more and Antonio is estab-
lished in a small, but pretentious, fruit business.
Some admiring compatriots have done this, other-
wise Antonio and his pretty wife would have
starved. A small fruit-shop is a sordid cage for
an artist of Antonio’s standing! But he still
loved the ladies—and himself. The woman chafed
and fretted. “She, too,” said Antonio, “has the
artistic soul.” But the bickerings grew more
numerous and bitter. She threatened often to leave
him, and just as often he threatened suicide. Then
she would tell him he was afraid, and shortly
afterwards there were tears followed by protesta-
tl(ms Ul? dying l°ve and passionate kisses.
Ihe neighbors complained to the police, and
Antonio’s countrymen swore excitedly when they
found he was always making excuses for the non-
payment of their loans.
At last Madame actually packed up and went
away for good. Though white with passion, An-
tonio politely raised his hat to her as the steamer
swung off from the wharf. For a week he was
mad with grief and for the want of good clothes.
The shop was shut up, and the fittings and furni-
ture seized for rent. The translation into English
of a column of Italian for a newspaper gave An-
tonio a guinea, wherewith he bought a revolver
and writing materials. To about a score of women
he wrote a letter each. One very loving one he
addressed to his wife and sent to the local news-
paper.
In the morning they found him dead by the
stage door of the local theatre. Only one chamber
of the revolver had been used ; only one had been
loaded. In his pocket was an old opera-pro-
gramme with the name of Signor Antonio starred
in big letters. The name was underlined heavily
in red ink.

merly an operatic singer, and manager of various theatri-
cal combinations.

Cow-Spanking.
TARANAKI, M.L.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is nothing in cow-spanking. I tackled it
when I first came to Taranaki—but, never again '
Broke 1 was, and humping bluey, and Taranaki
isn t the best place in the world to carry a swag.
Its too wet, and the roads are goat-tracks’
Besides, they are all small men there, with hold-
ings of only 100 to 200 acres apiece. My first iob
was on a place where they milked 120 cows Talk
about slavery ! You get up at half-past three in
the morning; muster cows in the dark; start
milking at four.
Milking isn’t hard work ! Wait till you have to
milk 120 between six—especially when the boss
and you are the only men, the others being his
missus and kids. Your wrists start first : they
ache worse than they would with a first day’s
shearing. Then, after a day or two, your hands
swell, you get cramp in them—in the mornings
you can hardly straighten your fingers. You are
also pretty certain to get cow-pox ; likewise your
nails fester. That’s one thing about Taranaki : I
cion t think a man in the district could get small-
pox—not if small-pox was the only di ease left in
the universe.
Bailing up your cows is nice, too. You have to
wade over your boot-tops in filth and drive the
beggars out. They always stand in the dirtiest
place they can find, and you may think yourself
lucky if you don’t get knocked over or slip down
If you do, you might be boiled for a week and
then you would smell unclean. When you have
got them bailed, it’s ten to one that the first you
go to leg-rope plants her foot in your stomach.
Very often the leg-rope breaks—they soon go
rotten in the wet- and then where are you ? Most
likely sitting out in the yard wondering what
struck you, with the milk making pretty shades
of color with the muck.
My boss was a religious man and had a notice
stuck up Swearing and smoking strictly pro-
hibited in shed and yards.Y"ct I've seen him
when he had put his trust in a rotten leg-rope, get
up and talk to the cow with a milking stool in a
manner that wouldn’t disgrace a Salvation Army
captain.
We would get the morning’s milking done about
8.30 a.m., when one of the youngsters would cart
the milk off to the factory. Another half-hour
would go in scraping down the bails—phew l
Breakfast would be about 9, and you had hardly
time to light your pipe before you were hurried off
to feed the calves.
I eeding Calves is fine fun—for the fellow looking
on. I had about 40 of the brutes to feed, and they
weren’t the youngest. There was a trough in the
paddock, and I would ladle the skim-milk out of
the tank where it was stored, and mix it with meal
and what they call hay-tea (made by boiling hay
in water till it gets black —a job done after work
at night). As soon as I got this stuff into the
trough, about 25 of the calves would nearly burst
themselves trying to see which could drink the
most in least time. The other 15 would put on
frills and stay in the furthest corner. I would go
after them with a bucket -Kinging out “Soun!
ti ll .! wasas dry as a wooden god stuffed
with fire-bricks I hey didn’t gi ve a moo for what
u j, m fcile bucket—-quite willing- to suck my
hand, however And when lat last-got one of
hem to put his head in the bucket he would
generally bunt, me and the bucket into next week.
By this time the calves at the trough had
finished, and would chase me round trying to suck
my shirt or trousers, and frightening the others
away Perhaps I used to swear most just at this
part but the swearing was very even all through.
len I had finished with the calves and left
them all sucking each other’s ears, the waggon
would be back from the factory, and I helped un-
load and feed the p!gs. After that, I just had a
minute toput on the water for washing-up, and it
ould be dinner-time. When dinner was over, I
vent and washed out big milk-tins for a couple
of hours, which generally made it close on three
oclock At three, I caught a horse and went
' Che ?°' v / a £ ain > knocking off milking about
T balf-past— quite ready for tea. By the
time i had a smoke, and got the hay-tea jigging it
v.as 10 o’clock, and I turned in for about five or
six hours.
Did I say no more cow-spanking for me ? Then
1 say it again—harder.

Louis Becke to The Bulletin from Lon-
don “ Going down the Strand the other day, the
weather horrible—sleety rain, cold wind, muddy
street, traffic blocked, drivers and pedestrians
pushing, struggling, and cursing to get ahead. A
procession comes crawling along out of a side-
street off the Strand—ten miserable wretches of
sandwich-men. Oh, the haggard faces, hunger-
filled eyes, and abject, despairing looks of each
man as the poor devils toiled along ! And the
sandwich - boards bore the inscription— ‘ Buy
1 issot s Life of Our Lord Jesus Christ.
1 ; rice i Six Shillings.’ Some of the men looked
like Christs, staggering towards Calvary. What
a subject for a painter with a sympathetic, bruta)
brush ! Title for picture, ‘ Christs of Trade.’ ”
According to the cablegrammer, Francis
Hart, of Westralia, the mannikin who helped
Gov. Robinson with his opera, was among the
crowd which howled down a Transvaal lecturer,
and called him bad names, and wanted to stoush
him with a chair-back, because he hadn’t sympa-
thised with “Dr. Jim.” This sort of thing is
supposed by the cablegrammer to be the kind of
local intelligence that Australia craves after. Yet
who in all this continent cares to know that the
little creature who helped Robinson with his opera
has been making a common Billingsgate of himself
at a London meeting ? Australia is full of shearers
and the like who use exactly the same kind of
language that F. Hart seems to have been using,
and more of it. To cable out Hart’s poor weak
abuse to the land of the great Australian adjective
is sending one small cinder to Newcastle.
